THE RED PAGE.
Hodgkinsoniana.
WILLIAM OSWALD HODGKINSON
was born at Wandsworth, Worcester-
shire, in February, 1836, so that he was
62 years old when he died in Brisbane
the other day. He was one of the finest speci-
mens of bulldog Englishman that ever reached
Australia. A little, square, chunky, man, full of
blood, with a square jaw and a noble head —the
best brachycephalic type —he was as full of grit as
an egg is full of meat. But he had more than
grit—he had a good brain an pa bright wit, with
an excellent foundation of book-knowledge and
man-knowledge. A versatile fellow —he spoke as
well as he wrote; and in his best days he wrote
well. He sketched a little too; had a taste for
the arts; scribbled verses. But his action-side was
uppermost. All his life he wanted to do and dare.
He would have been at home with Sidney, but
more at home with Drake. And his pluck never
failed him ; his spirit never failed him until
nearly the last, when, broken in health and for-
tune, he returned to Queensland, the province oi
Australia most endeared to him by friendships—
returned to take a humble post in the service of
the Govt, where he had been a Cabinet Minister
—and returned to die.
* * » *
All his life long Hodgkinson loved (as becomes
a man) fighting, drinking, adventure, and the
ladies. That he occasionally loved to excess may
have been his fault, but on the whole it was not
his misfortune. He was never content with exist-
ing : he wanted to live, and he lived. - His fighting
he learnt at historic King Edward’s School, Bir-
mingham. Edward Benson, late Archbishop of
Canterbury, was one of his school-fellows ; Joseph
Chamberlain, Rhodes’s co-conspirator against
Transvaal liberties, was educatedatthesamescbool.
All King Edward’s boys had to fight as a matter of
course ; and there were well-recognised classes or
grades of pugilistic capacity through which every
youngster mounted by degrees, with much nose-
blood and buffeting. They called Hodgkinson
“Fighting Billy,” He hadn’t heard the name
for 40 years till within a year of his death, when
an old schoolmate tapped him on the shoulder in
George - street. Sydney. “What! Fighting
Biliy ! ”
Hodgkinson’s chief opponent in the early days
was a big lump of a butcher-boy. If they fought
once they fought a dozen times, and a dozen times
the butcher-boy licked Hodgkinson. He used to
get in a favourite blow under the chin that
knocked H. out, and made his throat swell up
like a pumpkin. Then he’d go home and inter-
view his father. This was their regular colloquy :
“Hallo, Will! Fighting again ? ” “ Yes father.”
“ Licked again ? ” “ Yes, father.” “ Come along
to the stable.” Then they adjourned to the stable,
where Hodgkinson would get another licking—
with a strap—for being licked by the butcher-boy
with his fist.
But Hodgkinson-’s turn came at last. The thir-
teenth contest was fought in a tunnel, with a
policeman to see fair play, and Hodgkinson
managed to avoid the throat-blow and pummel the
butcher-boy into saying he’d had enough. Then
Hodgkinson told him he must come up and see
Hodgkinson’s father. They went up, a sorry-
looking pair ; and Hodgkinson’s father satisfied
himself that the butcher-boy had been thoroughly
beaten, and gave him half-a-sovereign. Hodgkin-
son got a pony. Half-a-dozen years afterwards he
came back to England and Birmingham, and,
passing along a well-remembered street, saw a fat
young man in a blue apron bending over a carcase
in a butcher’s-shop. It was his old antagonist.
He stepped up and hit him a vigorous whack on
the back. He turned in astonishment—“O,
Master William ! is it you ? ”
* * * *
In 1851 Hodgkinson was taken up to London to
see the great Exhibition. That was the chance he
had been pining for. For long he had yearned to
be a Pirate ; and, first chance he had, he gave his
father the slip, and made his way to the East ludia
Docks. There he found what looked like a suitable
ship, stepped on board, and confided his ambition
to the captain. The captain warmly approved
of it, but told Hodgkinson that as an essential
preliminary of piracy he must give his full name
and address, to be entered in the ship’s books, and
state where his parents were to be found. With
the Spanish Main before his eyes, Hodgkinson told
everything. The next thing he knew he, was
in a cab, with the captain alongside, bowling along to
a hotel, where he was delivered up to Ins father.
But Hodgkinson insisted on going to sea, and
shortly after he was shipped as third midshipman
on a barque bound for Australia. Things were
very different from what he had expected. But
the incident of the voyage which finally turned
him against salt water smelt strongly of—eggs !
It was before the days of freezing-rooms and
patent preserving methods, and Hodgkinson’s
captain, who had a weakness for eggs and poultry,
had the waist of his vessel lined with hencoops
full of cacklers. It was the duty of Hodgkinson
and the other middies, Green and Tompkins, to
go in rotation round these hencoops, and collect
fresh eggs for the captain’s breakfast. When they
were fortunate in finding a good number, the cap-
tain was occasionally graciously pleased to invite
them to breakfast in his cabin, where they got
potted-meat and marmalade and other delicacies
which they much preferred to the salt junk and
hard biscuit of the midshipmen’s mess. When
theydidn’t get eggs the captain let them know they
were in his black books. It was a long voyage,
and the hens were very erratic. Some weeks they
wouldn’t lay an egg, though the middies dosed
them with lime and ashes and all sorts of specifics.
At other times eggs were a drug in the hencoops.
Hodgkinson set his wits to work, and determined
to save a stock from the feast-weeks to carry him
over the famine-weeks. So he did. By judi-
ciously doling out the supply in the good
seasons, he was able to accumulate a stock
for the bad ; and after that, while Green and
Tompkins were cursed at frequently, he basked in
the captain’s sunshine all his watch long.
However, one morning about half-past nine,
Hodgkinson was sitting down in his berth
reading, very well satisfied with himself.
The hens had been off the lay for a
fortnight, and Green and Tompkins were
desperate, while Hodgkinson produced his two
eggs, or three eggs, or four eggs, when his turn
came, as regular as clockwork. That very morn-
ing he had given the captain’s steward four ad-
mirable eggs ; and, though he hadn’t been asked
to breakfast, he consoled himself with the thought
that the pleasure was only deferred. So when the
captain sang out, “ Pass the word for Mr-
Hodgkinson !” he expected that the old man
wanted to say something complimentary, and got
into his uniform coat in a very well-satisfied frame
of mind.
Hodgkinson found the captain on the breax of
the poop. He was pacing quickly up and
down with his hands behind his back, and looked
excited. “Ha!” he said, ‘‘Mr. Hodgkinson!”
“ Yes, sir,” and Hodgkinson saluted. “ Er-00-yh !
you young devil ! ” and the captain glared.
Hodgkinson couldn’t understand what was up,
but put a bold face on things, and suggested,
“You sent for me, sir?” “Yes, I did.
Er-00-gh! ” And he grunted again ferociously.
“You gave my steward four eggs this morning,
Mr. Hodgkinson?” “Yes, sir.” “Four fresh
eggs which you found before breakfast ? ” “Yes,
sir.” “Do you call that egg fresh, you young
whelp? And that egg? And that egg? And
THAT egg?” And as he spoke his hands came
from behind his back, and he dashed four rotten
eggs one after the other into Hodgkinson’s face.
“Up to the masthead, sir!” roared the captain.
“Up to the masthead !! ”he screamed. “UP TO
the Masthead ! !! ” he howled, dancing with
rage, as Hodgkinson stood dumbfounded, drip-
ping eggs and odours. Hodgkinson went up to
the masthead, and remained there eighteen hours,
without a word of a lie. Long before that time
had elapsed he had decided that the sea did not
suit him. There and then he sacrificed his hopes
of becoming a Pirate—and he never regretted the
sacrifice.
Long before the ship got to Hobson’s Bay
Hodgkinson had determined to quit her first
opportunity. The fo’c’s’le hands had a nasty habit
of growling, in a thick sea-voice, whenever
Hodgkinson neared them, “Isay, Bill, were them
eggs fresh this morning?” and exploding in a
hoarse chuckle. The Bay, when they reached it,
was full of ships—ships without men. It was the
crisis of the gold-fever, and the crews deserted as
soon as they touched port. The day after they
arrived Hodgkinson deserted too. He smuggled
himself into a kind of bum-boat—a vessel of small
rig that came down the Bay with provisions, and
travelled up to Melbourne in the cook’s copper.
They got there at sundown. Hodgkinson spent
the night in a tank on the wharf, and next morn-
ing walked up to town for breakfast. He came
to a pastry-cook’s, and ate a dozen of tarts “ while
you wait ” —remember, he was an English public-
school boy, fifteen years old. He had only a
two-shilling piece in his pocket, and when he had
finished he tossed that down and waited for the
change. He had forgotten that he was in Aus-
tralia, and at gold-rush time. The tarts were 10s.
a dozen ! Luckily, the girl in the shop took pity
on him—somehow, all through life, whenever
Hodgkinson was in a fix, a girl took pity on him
—and having ascertained that lie had deserted his
ship, she told him to wait and see her father—he
was a bootmaker, and wanted a boy to help in the
business. The bootmaker came along presently,
and Hodgkinson stayed with him for a day or two,
But he hadn’t come to Australia to learn shoe-
making ; and presently, a man came along who
was going up to the diggings with horses, and
offered to take Hodgkinson.
* * * *
He jumped at the chance, and off they went.
They had a mob of about sixty horses, and the
uniform price was five ounces of gold apiece. On
the way to Bendigo the mob rapidly grew less.
They would meet a lucky digger returning to
Melbourne, only too glad to get something to
carry his swag. He would get out his chamois-
leather bag of dust; the drover would produce a
pint-pot, and the digger would dribble dust in
until the drover reckoned there were five ounces.
Then he’d fix himself and his bundle on the horse
somehow, say good-day, and off.
Hodgkinson was full of zeal and eager to find
customers, and one day he had gone a little ahead
of the party when he met a man who took a fancy
to the mare he was riding. As the fellow seemed
eager Hodgkinson made the price six ounoes; got
it; and expected to be praised for his smartness
when he handed over the dust. To his surprise,
his employer called him a little d d fool.
Hodgkinson couldn’t understand it. “Why,”
said the drover, “didn’t I tell you the price was
five ounces ? Why didn’t you stick to the other
ounce yourself ? Fow’ll never get on in Australia!”
However, he was a very good fellow, and when
they reached Bendigo he gave Hodgkinson a £lO
note and left instructions with the storekeeper to
give him a lot of provisions and watch to see he
didn’t come to harm. So Hodgkinson started life
on the diggings in ’52. The sixteen-year-old had
his own claim, but mostly he was cradling for
others. He got the name of being a good and
lucky cradler, and had plenty of work. The
tailings were his share, and if they didn’t come to
much lots of men would throw in a few weights
with the next load. After a bit he made a rise,
got about £3OO worth of gold, and went to the
escort-tent to deposit it—before that he had fought
shy of the Commissioner, for he hadn’t a license
for his claim.
As soon as he saw Hodgkinson, the Commis-
sioner—his name was Bull—said : “ Your name
Hodgkinson ? ” Hodgkinson said that was so.
“ Arrest that boy ! ” And a trooper took the lad
in charge. “Do you know, you young rascal,”
said Bull, “that your parents don’t know what
has become of you, and there are letters all over
the place asking us to look out for you ? I’ve got
you now, and you must drop digging.” So he
made Hodgkinson license-clerk, and a couple of
months after he was sent to Tarnagulla as Assis-
tant-Commissioner —when he was 17 years old.
* * * *
Hodgkinson never knew how his life would have
gone if the Crimean War hadn’t broken out. He
was on fire at once ; took the first ship home, and
sent in an application for an army commission on
the strength of his service as Assistant Gold Com-
missioner of Victoria. They wouldn’t give him the
commission ; but he managed to get into the War
Office as junior clerk, which he considered next best
to being at the seat of war. Hodgkinson
held that clerkship until the spirit moved him
to write an article for The Times. To his surprise,
they put it in as a leader, and sent him £lO 10s.
for it—which he spent chiefly in purchasing copies
of the paper and posting them to all his friends.
The article was about the conduct of the Crimean
campaign, and most of it was a warm attack on
the War Office administration. Hodgkinson was
too proud of his coup to keep his authorship quiet,
and a few days after the article appeared he got a
note from Lord Panmure’s secretary, requesting
him to call on the Secretary for War at 11 o’clock
next morning. Hodgkinson went, and there was
Lord Panmure, with the chief of nis own depart-
ment, and two or three others. He was taxed with
writing the article, and asked if he considered it
was part 'of his duty as a junior clerk to criticise
the administration of his chief. Hodgkinson
admitted that technically his conduct was open
to question; “but,” he said, “it was your Lord-
ship who was the real cause of that article being
written.” “What, sir!” said Lord Panmure.
“Yes, your Lordship; I applied to you for a
commission in the Crimea, and if you had granted
it assuredly I should never have written that
article. So I think your Lordship must take at
least a share of ,the responsibility.” Panmure
couldn’t help smiling; and “What did I tell
you ?”■ said the departmental chief ; “ I said the
young rogue would have his answer pat.”
The end of it was Lord Panmure said he per-
ceived that there were excuses to be made; still,
it was an act of insubordination, and he thought
Hodgkinson had better leave the War Office.
However, he would recommend his oolleague in
the Treasury to take Hodgkinson in—and the
colleague did.
* * * *
So Hodgkinson got a junior clerkship in the
Treasury, and six months afterwards went up for
his examination as a second-class officer, and
passed with flying colours. The only subject he
failed in was book-keeping; and one of the
examiners came to him and advised him to with-
draw his paper in that subject. Hodgkinson
could see, of course, that if the examiner gave such
advice his other papers must be considered
exceptionally good, so he said his thanks and took
the recommendation—in all the other subjects he
came out head of the class. He was then 21, and
the youngest second-class officer in the Depart-
ment. (All his life, Hodgkinson might have
added, though he succeeded in nearly everything
else, he failed in book-keeping.)
* * * *
Presently the Treasury chief, Lord Manners,
took Hodgkinson into his office as private sec-
retary. Hodgkinson had by this time passed the
examination for first class—and brilliantly. He got
along well with Lord Manners, though occasionally
tumbling into scrapes. Among other things the
Treasury department had charge of was the regu-
lation of the Royal Pensioners, and on one occa-
sion the Prince of Sausageschwillenstaufen, or
something like that, made a formal complaint to
the effect that the ranger of Greenwich Park —
where he resided, with right of entry —had intro-
duced a, number of breeding sheep to the Park,
with the result that the modesty of the ladies of
his family had been so continually shocked that
they were obliged to cease promenading in the
Park. Hodgkinson replied to the effect that he
grieved exceedingly that whatever was done by
the sheep in the Park was done according to the
immutable laws of Nature and the changeless
cycle of the seasons, and these laws were not, at
the time of writing, included in the list of things
over which Her Majesty’s Department of the
Treasury had the honour to preside. Therefore
Hodgkinson subscribed himself, with expressions
of the greatest possible regret, his Serene High-
ness’s most humble and unworthy servant, &c.
Hodgkinson put the letter before Lord Manners
to sign, and of course he never read it. Next day
in came Sausageschwillenstaufen—foaming ! Lord
Manners rang for Hodgkinson at once. “Mr.
Hodgkinson, did you 'write this disgraceful
letter?” “I did, your Lordship—and it bears
your Lordship’s signature.” “Oh !—ha ?—hum !
—yes ! —you can go, Mr. Hodgkinson. And may
I ask what your Serene Highness finds to object to
in this very accurate statement ? ”
That was all Hodgkinson heard before he got
out the door ; but half-an-hour afterwards he was
sent for again and told by Lord Manners that ho
must n’t play any more such pranks, or he would
have the whole Royal Family down on his Lord-
ship. But Hodgkinson couldn’t help himself—he
was full of blood and spirits, and always in a state
of ebullition. The head of the Contract Depart-
ment had a habit of stroking his face with his
hand as he talked ; and one day he told Hodgkin-
son there were complaints about the quality.of the
service black-lead, and enquired if he knew any
means of testing it. “Why, yes,” said Hodgkin-
son ; “ just take a little in the palm of your hand
and rub it. If it is good black-lead it will leave a
substantial mark, with a good body and colour;
if it is bad it remains gritty in your palm and
makes no mark.”
The head of the Contract Department thanked
Hodgkinson ; and a day or two after all the black-
lead contractors were summoned to attend before
him with samples of their product. Hodgkinson
was present to see the fun. The chief seated the
contractors in a row, and began taking samples of
their stuff, and rubbing it in his palm, orating
upon the iniquity of supplying an inferior article
to a paternal Government, and—stroking his face
as he went along. By the time he had reached
the fourth contractor the room was full of smiles,
and Hodgkinson was choking behind his handker-
chief. The chief turned and saw him. “ Mr.
Hodgkinson, I see nothing ludicrous O Lord! ”
—as his reflection met him in a mirror on the
other side of the room. Before you could say,
“Victoria by the Grace,” those contractors had
been hunted down the stairs, and Hodgkinson
was being lectured severely for his failure to inter-
pose in favour of an absent-minded colleague.
But that was the first and last Blacklead Parade
in the Department.
* * • *
Such a high-spirited youth was not fitted for
English official departments bound together with
the crimson thread of red tape; and in 1859
Hodgkinson returned to Victoria. He joined the
Ape staff as reporter, and in a few months was
sub-editor; but resigned his place to accompany
Burke and Wills’ exploring expedition in 1860.
He quarrelled with Burke, as everybody quarrelled
with that incompetent leader, and returned to be
second in command of the expedition which
McKinlay headed in 1861. He did his duty loyally
and well; but maybe Hodgkinson was impatient
of control, or maybe McKinlay exercised his
authority too forcibly; for when the expedi-
tion ended there was disagreement between
leader and lieutenant. McKinlay was a fine
fellow and a big man ; Hodgkinson was a fine
fellow and a little man ; and McKinlay couldn’t
keep his temper. Rightly or wrongly his lan-
guage to Hodgkinson was abusive ; and one day
he threatened to strike Hodgkinson. Hodgkinson
presented his revolver at his leader’s head, and
told McKinlay that if he struck, he would
shoot; and McKihlay turned on his
heel _ raging. When the expedition, after
crossing Australia, reached the Burdekin,
Hodgkinson stayed in Queensland as a journalist,
and began his thirty years’ career in that province
as newspaper-proprietor, miner, goldfields war-
den, explorer, magistrate, politician, member of
Parliament, and Cabinet Minister. In 1875 he
led an expedition to explore the country west
of the Iraniantina, and returned successfully
after sixteen months, having bridged the gap
left by Sturt and Landsborough.
Everywhere he made his mark. His manhood
was as much appreciated by the miners of North
Queensland as his talents were admired. The
Hodgkinson goldfield was named after him;
the Royal Geographical Society named him
Fellow and awarded him the Back medal for
his services in exploration. In politics he
did not succeed so well ; his character brought
him to the front, but was too genial to keep him
there. As a lieutenant of Griffith, he lost popu-
larity along with his leader; and political exigencies
made him espouse projects and policies—such
as the introduction of coloured labour—to
whioh his best friends and supporters were
strongly opposed. When the old Queensland
political parties died in the Coalition of 1890,
Hodgkinson followed Griffith, and was for nearly
three years Minister for Mines and Education.
Never was seen such a jovially reckless Minister.
The fossilised officials of the Department still
shudder at the mention of his name. His
chief clerk once brought him an educational
report carefully endorsed in a precise hand, “This
matter is still in nubibus.” Hodgkinson seized
the document and scrawled across it, “Wait till
the clouds roll by! ” And the incident was typical
of his administration.
In the crash of 1893 Hodgkinson failed to find a
Parliamentary seat; and went to Westralia. At
first he did well in mining speculation, making
£3OOO in a few weeks. Then he did badly,
and returned to Sydney sick at heart and
sick in body, to live as an invalid in a little cottage
at Waverley. His doctor told him to prepare for
death; but Hodgkinson would not die. He
resolutely got better; though his improvement
was only a flicker before the end. His pluck was
unquenchable ; but his years would not be denied.
* * *, *
Scores of good stories are extant concerning
Hodgkinson in his prime. One of the best relates
how, at a rough Queensland mining township
where he was warden, he was invited by an
assembly of Irishmen to preside over their St.
Patrick’s Day dinner. The dinner was held in the
dining-room of the public-house. Hodgkinson sat
at the head of a long table, opposite the door,
with all the Murphies and Flanagans of the neigh-
bourhood in two rows down the table on each side
of him. Hodgkinson led up to the toast of
the evening wit*h a long and eloquent pre-
amble extolling the ancient glories of Ireland
which (with the liquor) excited the O’Briens and
Mulligans to enthusiastic frenzy. It was
“ Hark to him ! ” and “ Glory be to you, Mister
Hodgkinson!” And “O, Mister Hodgkinson,
Oi love you ! ” and “Iloquent! he’s the bhov for
iloquence ! ” all the length of the table. Then
Hodgkinson closed his long exordium. The toast
that I have to propose you—are all your glasses
filled?” “Yis, Mister Hodgkinson, yis, ye
darling ! ” The toast that I have to propose to
you, gentlemen, is Bloody end to the Pope!” There
was just one instant of paralysis before the pan-
demonium. Hodgkinson seized it, slipped from
his chair, dived under the table, got between
the legs of the Muldoons and O’Flahertys to the
door —where he had a horse waiting—and didn’t
come back for three days.
• * * *
That incident, too, was characteristic of Hodg-
kinson. May the turf lie lightly on him ! —for in
an age of timid and emasculated self-seekers he
was a brave man, a generous man, a White Man.

■Queensland Aborigines Protection Act.
Reported that Sydney Police Swimming
club is snuffing out. Reason—refusal of support
from superior officers. When the club announced
races for members, requisite leave of absence for a
few hours was refused. Yet, in the height of the
plague-scare, 30 men could be spared for a band
performance. This, in face of a promise that
swimmers should receive preference when appoint-
ments were being made, sounds most peculiar.
It is about time that that commission of enquiry
arrived.

Bards of the Backblocks.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.!]
His Last Asset.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Near the camp of Mangy Dingo, somewhere on
the Upper Russell,
Where the scrub has blanched the faces to the
pallor of the dead ;
Where existence is a gloomy and a sordid sort of
tussle,
With the leech and flea and fever in the chilly
river-bed —
Where a claim is mostly stripping, and the wash
goes just a fraction,
And the gold is fine as powder, and the men
are slightly cracked;
Where there is no change of seasons, and the
principal attraction
Is the lady aborigine (in defiance of the
Act*)—
Stood the shanty of M‘Vicker, looking peaceable
and placid ;
For his dangerous explosives were all kept behind
the bar,
And his very strong solutions broken down with
nitric acid,
Always took at least a fortnight to corrode a
whisky-jar.
I had travelled through a twilight that was not
exactly cheering,
Since the morning glow had vanished at the
borders of the scrub ;
And I drew a breath of comfort as I entered on
the clearing,
Where a glimpse of sun was playing on the iron
of the pub.
What the deuce is on ? I wondered, as I saw the
crowd collected
Round the place that represented Mangy
Dingo’s pride and boast,
And a gent, of aged aspect unmistakably
ejected
From the premises (I heard him bumping hard
against a post).
What in thunder is the matter? And they
swelled and grew important,
And the facts that they related, intermixed
with dash ! and blank !
Made a tale on which a parson might with ease
become exhortant
In the interests of water and promotion of the
tank.
Loamy Harry (they had given this euphonic
appellation
To the gentleman reposing in the mud beneath
a tree)
After months of sober diet had succumbed to a
sensation
That had led without the option to a flaming
jamboree.
With the absolute abandon of a thirst that had
been growing
Into all the pomp and splendor of a Lowe r
Flinders drought,
He had started on the snake-juice; and he kept
M Wicker going
At the still that had been fashioned from a shot-
gun and a spout.
He had drunk a six-months’ washing—oh, the
time he had been having !
He had sold his claim, and sent it on a scintil-
lating burst.
He had jumped the ancient packhorse that was
suffering from spavin
'Cross the bar—and yet his efforts had not laid
his giant thirst.
And his blankets and his billies he converted into
liquor,
And the boots he wore on Sundays, and his red
Crimean shirt.
Till the last belonging vanished down his gullet,
and M Wicker
With a final objurgation threw him out into the
dirt.
So we stood around and marvelled. And the
corpse grew animated ;
With a grumble of displeasure and a blessing
for old Mac,
Loamy Harry slowly staggered to his feet and
softly skated
Through the mud in curves and zigzags, dis-
appearing down the track.
Late at night—the land lay sleeping, and the
moon hung o’er the clearing
Dreamily—l was awakened by the barking of
my pup.
There was wafted through the silence to my camp
a sound of cheering,
And a husky voice that hiccoughed : “ Fill ’em
up, boys, fill ’em up ! ”
It was Harry ! Made resourceful by a thirst that
knew no shrinking.
He had gone and struck a bargain with the gar-
dener, Ah Sin,
Which had. brought him many shekels ; and at
present he was drinking,
In a state of maudlin sorrow, what he’d got for—
Loo , his gin !

SATURDAY, AUGUST 4. 1900.
The Commonwealth Capital.
Tiie seat of Government of the Commonwealth shall be
determined by the Parliament, and shall be within terri-
tory which shall have been granted to or acquired by the
Commonwealth, and shall be vested in and belong to the
Commonwealth, and if New South Wales be an Original
State, shall be in that State, and be distant not less than
100 miles from Sydnej’.
Such territory shall contain an area of not less than
100 square miles, and such portion thereof as shall consist
of Crown lands shall be granted to the Commonwealth
without any payment therefor.— Commonwealth Constitu-
tion, Clause 125.
When the report of the Commissioner sent
forth by the N.S.W. Government to spy out
suitable situations for the Commonwealth
capital has been judiciously put in the fire,
and the Federal Legislature has sat down to
seriously consider what kind of a Common-
wealth City it wants, then Australia will
have a chance, probably its first chance, to
find out whether its new Government is, or
is not, equal to the chances that lie before it.
In the matter of the capital, to begin with,
it has a great and wonderful opportunity.
And the reason why, in The Bulletin’s;
opinion, the Commissioner’s report should
be burnt unread is that his investigations
have, to a great extent, started at the wrong
end and on the wrong basis. Because the
Federal territory on which the capital stands
is to be of not less than 100 square miles
there has been a tendency to look for a con-
venient spot of that area (ten miles by ten)
and not much more. Because it is to be not
less than 100 miles from Sydney there has
been an inclination to consider spots 101
miles or 100| miles from that city. There
has been manifested an unfortunate habit of
considering the alleged “ claims ” of this and
that town, and of receiving deputations of
the inhabitants ; whereas the central idea
should be to disqualify every town,
if a suitable site with no town on it
can be discovered. Also it should have been,
explained to the inhabitants who deputa-
tionised the Commissioner that they were so
many separate and individual drawbacks ;
that each one of them made the chances of
his district less ; that his very existence was-
one more reason against selecting his town,
and that if two equally suitable places could
be found, one with inhabitants and the other
without, the one without inhabitants would
be unhesitatingly preferred.
* * * •
The Federal territory that is wanted is an
area of at least 5000 square miles —say, 71
miles long and 71 miles broad, or more if
possible—on an elevated region, with a cold
climate, good scenery if obtainable, a reason-
ably good water-supply, no inhabitants or
as few as possible, and no railways or mayor
or corporation anywhere near it if a place
without these drawbacks can b"e found. If
there are inhabitants they must be put up
with as a necessary evil, but they shouldn’t
be sought for. The site decided upon
should be as far from Sydney as it can
be without getting more than half-way
to Melbourne or Brisbane. It should lie at
least some little distance back from the
coast, to guard it against attack. And the
Federal Government should not only be
sovereign of this area, but it should be sole
ground landlord of every inch of the 5000 or
more square miles for ever, and the law
which fixes the Federal capital should de-
clare the soil to be absolutely inalienable.
* * * •
The political capital of Australia must
needs grow, sooner or later, into a large city.
This is especially so if it is located far away
from any existing large city ; in that case
it will probably become a great indus-
trial and commercial centre, as well as
a political one. But in any case it
must necessarily become, in time, a
place of magnitude. The people of Austra-
lia, by making it their capital, will give
the land a value it doesn’t now possess. The
people will create that increased value, and
it should therefore be the people’s property.
The land on which the Federal city stands
will have the greatest increase of value ; but,
for miles and miles around, the agricultural
soil will rise in price through the establish-
ment of a new and adjacent market. If the
part of the selected territory, which is State
property now, is sold when the site of the
Federal city is selected, the great bulk of the
unearned increment will be given away gratis.
If the part of it which is private property
now is allowed to remain private property,
all the unearned increment will be given
away gratis. Therefore, the State land in
the selected area should be made the inalien-
able property of the Federal Government,
and the private land should be compulsorily
purchased—at a liberal price as so many
acres of wilderness, but as so many acres of
wilderness only, and with no consideration
at all for the extra value which the people
are going to confer upon it by building a
metropolis there.
* * * m
The people who created Sydney and Mel-
bourne may be excused for missing their
opportunity. There was no evidence to
show them that Sydney and Melbourne
were going to be great hives of humanity,
and that the State was throwing away a
magnificent revenue by selling instead of
leasing the street frontages. But the people
who make the Federal city cannot be ex-
cused if they throw away their opportunity.
They know that they are laying the founda-
tions of a metropolis, and that, if they part
with the fee simple of the land, they are
throwing away a prospective rent-revenue of
hundreds of thousands per annum. The
Federal territory should be let out on build-
ing and agricultural lease only—probably
the best method would be to let it on per-
petual lease with a re-appraisement of value
at frequent intervals. In doing this
the Federal authority would merely be
following the example of London—with a
difference. London is also built very largely
on leasehold ground—the difference being
that the ground landlord is a private indivi-
dual instead of the State. Doubtless the
State would prove a much less grasping
ground-landlord than private owners do in
London, and the case is quoted merely to
show that there is no practical reason why a
great city should not be built up under such
conditions. The reason why the area of the
Federal territory should be at least 5000
square miles is sufficiently obvious. The
Federal authority should seek to annex, for
the benefit of the people of Australia, every
penny of the additional value which the ex-
penditure of the people’s money in building
up a capital will bring into existence. In an
area of 100 square miles (JO miles by 10), the
Commonwealth might be sole ground land-
lord inside, but for many miles beyond its
narrow borders the boom in land values
would extend, and everywhere outside the
ten-mile radius the private landlord would
gather in the profit. And while the people
at large, by the expenditure of their good
legal tender, make these additional land
values, the people at large should gather in
every penny of the profit.
* * * *
The Commonwealth city should be high
up on the N.S.W. tableland for a number of
reasons. Australiaisgoingtomakeanewcentre
•of population. It is going, in course of time,
to gather a million, or many millions, of people
in and around that centre. It is going to
practically create a million or more new
Australians, and it wants to make them of a
hardy race such as Australia may be proud
of in the future, and such as will prove an
iron wall of defence for this country in
some coming hour of need. All the
existing great cities of this continent
lie low, in the hot, muggy, relaxing atmos-
phere of the coast. Australia is too
new as yet to see the results. There
has not been time for the Australian
to grow weak and nerveless and effeminate
in these surroundings. But all the world
over it is known that the man who comes of
twenty generations of mountaineers is far
more than a match for the of
twenty generations of dwellers on a steaming
coast, and when this country is going to
grow a million, or a few million, new Aus-
tralians it wants to grow the best sample
obtainable. When it is going to draw popu-
lation to a new site it wants to select a site
which will grow such population ■' as this
country, and every other country, wants.
* » * *
The site of Australia’s metropolis should
be as beautiful a one as can be found because
its beauty will attract population, and be-
cause any person who would select a com-
monplace location when a beautiful one is
obtainable is only fitted to be an aider-
man. It should be as far away from
the coast as is possible without sacri-
ficing other advantages, partly because
it is from the coast that our invad-
ing enemy must come when he does come,
and the capital should be so placed that the
arsenals, the Treasury, the military head-
quarters and the banks can’t be seized by
any sudden raid, and the Government be
scattered into the hills just when Australia
needs all the wits its Government has got.
Also it should be away from the coast
because the coast is low and hot, and it is
there that the degenerate Australian will 1
grow when, in courseof centuries, his environ-
ment begins to tell on him. The Federal
city should be as far away as possible from
any existing city, partly because land is
cheaper there, and partly because this
country wants to attract population to
places where there is no population,
and not to further congest the few regions
which are congested already—as congestion
goes in this thinly-peopled land. For the
same reason it should, if possible, be away
from towns and railways, and mayors and
corporations, and other alleged civilisation.
All these things make land dear, and, if a
suitable area can be found where there is
only wilderness, it should have the prefer-
ence. The stock gag of the Australian poli-
tician is tliat it is necessary to “ open up the
country,” and the most magnificent chance
of opening up the country which Australia
ever saw here presents itself. Why waste it
on country that is already opened up 1
* m m *
The theory here advanced is one which is
not likely to be received with any enthusiasm
in New South Wales outside the 100-mile
limit from Sydney. Nor is it likely to
appeal to any wealthy inhabitant inside that
limit, or in the other provinces. It is likely
to be cursed by land-jobbers, capitalists,
syndicates, banks, building-societies, and by
country mayors and corporations generally.
Its adoption would shut out every country
town of any importance from becoming the
capital; some small townships might be un-
avoidably included in the Federal territory,
but nothing of the kind would be
done if it could be avoided. Places like
Albury, Bathurst, Goulburn, and Armidale,
w'hich claim consideration by reason of their
importance and their population, would be
regretfully told that their importance was an
absolutely incurable bar—that the Common-
wealth didn’t desire to buy an expensive
city and draw population where there was
already much population, but to acquire
5000 square miles of prairie, and start with
a clean sheet. The whole tribe of capital-
ists would necessarily be hostile, for the
idea of Federal landlordism pure and simple
would shut them out from the grandest
land-booming prospect they have yet
seen. Therefore, the capitalist tribe
will necessarily state that out of its
large experience, gained amid the ruins of
many land-banks, and out of its knowledge
of finance, evidenced by the many institu-
tions it has steered to insolvency or recon-
struction, it can say confidently, with its
hand on its overdraft, that the idea of
Federal landlordism is a chimera. And the
support of the inhabitants of the district
selected would be worth little, for they
would be few. The best chance of intelli-
gent consideration for such a scheme lies in
the more distant provinces. The people who
are too far aw&y to rush for allotments in
the Federal city, and who consequently fail
to see why the land-boomers of Melbourne
and Sydney should divide the unearned
increment which these outlying people’s
money helps to create, may be better dis-
posed to give the idea a hearing.
V * * *
As for the money required to carry out
such a scheme, the raising of that must be a
subject for a future article.
To the Poreign-Shoddyists of New
South Wales.
a small Foreign-trade publication, designated
Our Country, has been started in Sydney, its
chief and most visible mission being to howl that
Victoria under Protection isn’t getting on so well
as N.S.W. under Freetrade. To the N.S.W.
Shoddy apostle there are only two places in the
world—N.S.W. and Victoria. One is allegedly
Freetrade. and the other isn’t, and the whole fiscal
question lies in comparing the two. The fact that
a wider view of the fiscal question might be
arrived at by also taking in Queensland and
Maoriland, which are more strongly Protectionist
than Victoria, and are also a little nearer to
N.S.W.’sown size, never enters into his calcula-
tions. His faith is brief and quaint, and goes into
these two propositions:—
(1) N.S.W. is nearly four times as large as Victoria ; it
had 50 years’ start of Victoria ; it has more people though
not one-third as many as it should have if it had got on
as Victoria has; it has abundance of coal, the great
essential in manufactures, while Victoria has only just
discovered a scanty supply ; it has now a far larger and
more valuable mineral production than Victoria ; it has
£1,500,000 a year more land revenue by reason of its extra
size ; and it has coddled its industries and created an
artificial prosperity by borrowing to the extent of over
£47 per inhabitant as against Victoria’s £4O.
(2) And if the very small and new Protectionist pro-
vince isn’t ahead of the very large and old Foreign-trade
one, that shows Protection is bad. In fact, if Goat Island
was Protectionist and all the rest of Australia was Free-
trade, and Goat Island wasn’t ahead of all the rest of
Australia in wealth and industries, that would prove that
Protection is thoroughly awful and disastrous.
* * • *
Our Country then proceeds to demonstrate, by
comparing the number of persons (55,640) em-
ployed in N.S.W. industries in 1899 with the
number (54,778) employed in the province one-
fourth the size in 1898, that N.S.W. is ahead after
all. If the numbers in each province for 1898, or
the numbers in each province for 1899, had been
compared, the very small province of Victoria
would have been ahead both times ; but a curious
inability to go straight and to be honest seems to
be in the shoddy party’s bones. But this, after
all, is a very small question. The big question is
that Freetrade and Protection are not everything ;
and borrowing has more to do with the present
state of Australia than either of them. Industries
may be artificially fostered and built up on a solid
foundation by Protection, but they mav also be
artificially boomed into an unhealthy and
transient semblance of prosperity, and built
up like a house of cards, by borrowing.
Borrowing for productive works is a dubious
pdlicy at the best, but borrowing for
unproductive works which should be paid for out
of revenue—borrowing to make employment which
can be credited by unreliable faddists to the bene-
ficent effects of Freetrade—borrowing because the
Freetrade tariff won’t yield enough revenue to
keep the country solvent and its advocates haven’t
.the common honesty to acknowledge the fact and
to face the consequences—is simply laying the
foundations of future ruin. And that is exactly
what New South Wales has done since its great
Shoddy policy came into vogue, as witness those
figures, taken from Coghlan’s “ Statistics of the
Seven Colonies ” for four years —the latest for
which statistics are issued:—
New South Wales.
1895- Net Customs revenue .. £1,974,827
Borrowed for unproductive works 559,552
£2,534,379
1896- —Net Customs revenue .. 1,367,431
Borrowed for unproductive works 562,658
1,930,089
1897- —Net Customs revenue .. 1,239,084
Borrowed for unproductive works 650,514
1,889,598
1898- —Net Customs revenue .. 1,250,289

2,083,780
£8,437,846

£1,718,582

1,886,190

2,131,000
7,534,225
* * * *
The sum and mystery of the Foreign-trade Lie,
so far as it concerns the eternal comparison of
N.S.W. and 'Victoria, is based on the suppression
of one-half of these figures. It is very beautiful
to say that “ Under the bright and enlightened ,
and beneficent policy of Freetrade we exacted, in
the last three years, £1,529,413 less from the
people in N.S.W. than the cruel and exacting
policy of Protection exacted in Victoria,” when
every care is taken not to add, “ And we made it
up by charging to loans £1,617,237 more than Vic-
toria did of the unproductive works which should
have been paid out of revenue, so that the alleged
relief which you experienced under Freetrade was
wholly owing to misappropriation of the loan
funds.” The fiscal policies of the two States
simply boil down to this: Victoria exacts more
customs revenue from her people now; N.S.W.
exacts less now and leaves every penny of the
saving to be a debt and a load on posterity. All
the calculations about the extra prices paid in
Victoria through Protection (even if they were
true, which they very seldom are) amount to
nothing without the corollary: “ The extra
cheapness of goods in N.S.W. now, is arrived at
by leaving the difference in the form of a debt for
posterity to pay.” All the tales about the burden
which has been lifted off N.S.W. through Free-
trade are less than half a truth unless it is added :
“and the amount which we took off is charged to
your children and your children’s children by the
simple process of paying so much less of our own
current expenses out of income and adding so
much more to their future burdens.”
* / * * *
Up to 30th June, 1899, N.S.W., in addition to
charging practically every penny of the cost of its
reproductive works to loans, had also spent
£10,915,047 of borrowed money for unproductive,
or purely revenue, purposes. Victoria similarly
misspent only £3,060,767 (these are the official
figures of N.S.W. Statistician Coghlan), and that
province paid for over £1,000,000 worth of its pro-
ductive works out of revenue, which may be
fairly said to reduce the real misappropriation to
£2,000,000. The moral is that every penny which
N.S.W. has saved from its people’s pockets by
taxing them lightly under a Freetrade tariff has
been made up by stealing the loan-funds to fill
the gap in the Treasury. Until the N.S.W. Free-
trade party is prepared to raise another £600,000 a
year by taxation, so as to reduce its loan steal to
the comparatively low figure of Victoria’s loan
steal, and then to compare the relative state of the
two provinces when the process of creating an
artificial boom out of loans has been brought to a
common basis, there is no possibility of fair com-
parison. It isn’t a case of Freetrade v. Protec-
tion. It is Freetrade and a big swindle v. Protec-
tion and comparative honesty.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Chinese Maelstrom.
“ Jerome Darnet ” writes on the Chinese com-
plication :
When so many who really know little of China and
Chinese character are gravely expounding, or are being
solicited to expound, their opinions in connection with
the Chinese upheaval, surely an Australian who speaks
Chinese (or rather a few of the myriad dialects which ex-
press the written language) at least as well as he speaks
English may reasonably ask audience.
Therefore, my countrymen, lend me your long ears.
The time has certainly arrived when certain of Australia’s
legislators should be tied with strong ropes until they de-
sist from pursuing a military policy which is quite un-
necessarily draining the provinces of this young nation
of their strength, in quarrels the issues of which no man
can clearly foresee, and of which little more can be de-
finitely said than that they promise to be baleful and
doubtful. It may be freely conceded that it is desirable
that the Australian nation should possess a high military
spirit, for the task of defending the lengthy shores of this


bf
island continent may, and probably will, yet require hosts
of resolute men, but it can be just as emphatically
affirmed, without fear of logical contradiction, that a
paltry five millions of people inhabiting this vast Aus-
tralian territory must not yet make military essays,
except in defence of their own soil.
Why should Australia assist in keeping open the Chinese
door, when her avowed policy is to keep the Chinese out
of our continent ? The massacre of unfortunate foreign
civilians is, of course, to be deplored, but few Orientals
are capable of making a distinction between combatant
and non-combatant enemies. The Turks, who have had
centuries of association with Europeans, are notorious
offenders in these matters, and infuriated Manchu sol-
diers can hardly be expected to be any better.
There have been many massacres of foreigners in China
in ages past. Once they were killed to a man. Over
300,000 persons, principally Arab and Indian traders, are
said to have perished. Of all the many writers on China
and the Chinese, the Frenchman Hoo and our own
“Vagabond” appear to have understood the Chinese
character the best.
The newspapers seem to generally voice the idea that
all Chinamen are at present raging against Europeans.
It is principally the Manchu troops—the Normans of
China—who are attacking the foreigners. The “ Boxers ”
who are assisting are real Chinese, but they are princi-
pally inhabitants of those districts in Northern China,
fairly close to the capital. The people of those districts
have been impressed by the glamour arising from the
proximity of the seat of the Imperial residence and power,
and have cultivated a sort of loyalty to the present foreign
Manchu dynasty. But throughout the rest of China proper
a great slaughter of the slaughterers is preparing. Every
true Chinaman has cherished the idea —handed down
through nearly three centuries—of the destruction of the
“Woo”—the barbarians—the foreign usurpers —for such
the Manchus are, and there is little doubt that even now
the clock is about to chime the fateful hour.
Western foreigners and Japanese—the violators of
Chinese territorial integrity—may be attacked by the
wrathful Chinese at the same time, for the troubles of
China can be classified under three heads —Inter-racial
(between Chinese and Manchus), Dynastic (being the
Court intrigues of the Manchus themselves), and Inter-
national. The Manchus are at present in the position of
the Norman lords of England when their native Nor-
mandy passed from their power.
There is this difference, however —the Saxon speedily
assimilated the Norman, and the Englishman was evolved;
but, although the Chinaman has so imposed his civilisa-
tion on his conquerors (as he always does) that the Man-
chu tongue and literature are almost as dead as Sanscrit,
the Manchus are still a race apart. The Chinaman has
compelled the Manchu to govern him according to
Chinese methods, as he has compelled all his various
evanescing conquerors, but the Manchu is ever the mark of
the patiently-waiting Chinese butcher, who, with Oriental
inscrutability, has watched the preparing of the situation.
The Manchus, finding their motherland —Manchuria—
passing under Russian influence, are conquerors without
a country, and naturally hate the innovating nations
who have brought matters to this pass. But, as already
foreshadowed, the whole Manchu race in China totters to
its Golgotha. Like previous conquerors of China they
will be swept away in a deluge of blood.
The reason of the harmlessness of China to outside
nations is that the strength of China is only that small
portion which the Manchus could contrive to get
exerted. The Chinese mass has always been unresponsive
and inert under Manchu rule. Soon the Empire,
with its complicated troubles and its fighting foreign
aggressors jealous of each other, will be a cauldron of
seething Hell-broth.
The Japanese sword, too, is thirsting for blood, no-
toriety and power. Let not Australia strain her strength
to secure any useless bays which may be with difficulty
gained in this tangled quarrel. Rather let our Govern-
ments, or, better still, our first Federal Govt, see to wise
measures for the populating of our tropical shores ; for
behind these shores are hundreds of leagues where our
land is empty of men.

The Woman and the Vote.
The thoroughly factitious, fictitious character of
the “agitation” for woman’s suffrage in N.S.
Wales was exposed in a recent Assembly de-
bate. Indeed and in ’ truth, there isn’t any
“ agitation.” There is a small wave of metropoli-
tan emotion, and even that is mostly composed of
oratorical froth with “ Miss Rose Scott ” atop,
and various “chivalrous” M’s.P. bobbing about
like corks in her enthusiastic wake. The average
woman of N.S.W. apparently knows nothing
about the suffrage. She has never given any
sign that she wants a vote. If she is un-
married, she is usually and properly occupied
in looking for a man—and The Bulletin hopes
she will get him. If she is married, she is usually
and properly occupied in minding her home and
her family—and The Bulletin hopes they will
repay her trouble. Politics do not interest her.
Politicians do —occasionally. She was interested
in Parkes, because he married three wives, and
brought the romantic fascination of Bluebeard
into real life. She is mildly interested in Arthur
Griffith, because he is “ such a nice young man,
my dear,” and follows the fashions in clothes.
George Reid is “ such a funny man, you know.”
The average woman is not sure that she under-
stands him ; but he makes her laugh,, and she likes
to be made laugh. And if you talk to the rare,
occasional woman who says she is interested in
politics, and reads the leaders in S.M. Herald,
the mystery of the “agitation” for woman’s
suffrage thickens as you listen. While she is
declaring that it is a mistake to support the
Indian famine fund, because she once saw
an Afghan beat his camel dreadfully, you
can follow her; but when she declares that
she is in favor of Federation because Mr.
Barton was quite horrid to her at a ball
once, and she is sure that anything he opposes
must be rightthen the clue through the maze
becomes indistinct again. The Bulletin's own
attitude to woman’s suffrage is one of modified in-
difference. The experience of Maoriland and
South Australia has proved that the woman’s vote
has little power to affect current politics. In the
main, the women vote as their nearest mate friends
do ; so that the strength of parties is left unal-
tered. They have more important things to
think of than political principles and details—
the price of meat and the trimming of baby’s
bonnet. And (The Bulletin is not jesting now)
these are more important things in the woman’s
sphere than politics are. It will be a bad day for
the community when woman becomes so interested
in Freetrade versus Protection that she neglects
her vital home interests. There are those who
suggest that a woman may become a publicist
without permitting her home and family to suffer.
As regards the average woman, that may fairly be
doubted. Her brain, her time, her energy, have
all decided limitations. But it is certainly
unjust that exceptional women should be
tethered to a domesticity for which they have no
vocation. “ Miss Rose Scott ” is at least as well
qualified to vote as the average man is. But it is
impossible to formulate an intelligence-test for
women where it is not applied to men. Therefore
all women may as well have votes —and for this
reason, that the innovation does not seem likely
to do harm, and may be productive of good. And
responsibility is apt to breed power; the responsi-
bility of the vote may breed in woman the power
of intelligent voting.
An Unripe Government.
In discussing his futile and ludicrously-inadequate
measure for the amendment of Sydney’s municipal
government, Premier Lyne explained that:
He had been asked whether the Bill would not check or
positively prevent the creation of Greater Sydney. He
thought the time was not ripe to carry out that proposal.
It was necessary to get the consent of a very large
number of bodies. The Bill he was presenting was laying
the foundation for the Greater Sydney when the question
was ripe, because if they remedied the somewhat deplor-
able state of things which had been existing for some
months past, and if they found in two years’ time that it
works well, they would know that it could be safely ex-
tended to the Greater Sydney proposal. He looked for-
ward to bringing about Greater Sydney, as it would mean
a reduction in the expenses, and would give a greater
standing and credit to the citizens than was possible with
the present system of smaller municipalities.
Which is all feebly absurd, not to say
imbecile. The time is as ripe for the estab-
lishment of a Greater Sydney as for the
building of the North Shore Bridge —only the
Greater Sydney scheme requires brains, while the
bridge only requires a plentiful shovelling out of
borrowed money, and the Lyne Ministry is a
better shoveller than constructive legislator. The
attention of William the Futile is respectfully
invited to these propositions :
(1) His amendment won’t necessarily bring much better
men or more intelligence into Sydney Municipal Council
than are there now. The Council will still be a very small
affair (only one among the 41 municipalities of Greater
Sydney), with very small powers over a very small area.
Big, capable, ambitious men, who want to do big things
and make a name for themselves, will still hold aloof from
that narrow area of narrow Bumbledom. When it is a
question of helping to govern all Greater Sydney, with
almost unlimited powers to make Greater Sydney one of
the model cities of the world, such men may be obtained,
but not till then.
(2) The reform of one municipal government out of 41
doesn’t necessarily bring union a whit nearer. If Lynb
holds that widening the municipal franchise will bring in
a class of men who will have a better understanding of
the advantages of union, then the obvious course is to
reform the 41 municipal governments, and get 41 councils
with an enlightened tendency towards union. To have
one reformed council with a reformed leaning to union,
and 40 stagnant ones that won’t listen, leaves things just
as they were.
(3) The short and simple explanation is this : It is easy
to build a bridge with borrowed money. It is easy to
reform the municipal franchise—that doesn’t involve any
intricate financial problems. But it isn’t easy to devise
some means of uniting 41 municipalities in all stages of
solvency and insolvency without injustice to any one of
them. And it is much pleasanter to say, “ I postpone
this question in a spirit of large and cautious statesman-
ship because the time is not yet ripe,” than to say, “ I
shirk this question because I don’t know what on earth to
do about it.” After all, it is the Lyne Government which
isn’t ripe.
And as it is a cheap and easy tiling to jeer at the
Government for not knowing how to do it, and
then leave the thing at that, The Bulletin offers
this suggestion as to how the financial difficulty
of making a Greater Sydney might be overcome —
in a sort of way, anyhow
(1) Greater Sydney to consist, at first, of 41 wards,
being the 41 existing municipalities.
(2) Representation in the Greater Sydney Council to be
according to population. The more populous wards could
be subdivided into districts, with an alderman for each
district. In very thinly populated ones there might be
an alderman for a whole ward, or one alderman might
represent two wards. But the existing boundaries to be
strictly preserved for the present.
(3) Assets which yield revenue to be valued by an in-
dependent commission of experts appointed by the
Government. Greater Sydney Council to take over these
assets and pay for them by assuming a corresponding
proportion of each ward’s debt.
(4) The rest of the old debts to be consolidated at a re-
duced interest as occasion offers, but to lie recorded in
the Council’s books as the debts of each separate ward.
(5) A general uniform rate to be levied to pay for light-
ing, cleaning, improvements, &c., and to pay the interest
on the Council’s debt— i.e., the proportion taken over
from the 41 separate wards, and any new debts con-
tracted.
(6) A separate rate to be levied in each ward to pay the
interest on its old separate debt (the portion not taken
over), and to provide for its gradual extinction. This
would not be uniform, but would vary according to the
amount Of each ward’s debt. It would be so graduated as
to extinguish all the separate old ward debts at about the
same date.
(7) When all the debts of the separate wards were ex-
tinguished, then Greater Sydney could be subdivided
afresh on a more sensible plan, there being no longer any
need for preserving the old municipal boundaries.
This is at least a possible way of combining the
41 municipalities, and saving an immense amount
in interest by consolidating the 41 separate debts,
without piling the burdens of the insolvent muni-
cipalities on the solvent ones. When, in the slow
procession of years, the time becomes ripe perhaps
the Lyne Government will seriously consider this
possibility.

Exploiting' the Northern Territory.
Under the guise of an interesting account of the
Arafura, Melb. Age publishes what looks exceed-
ingly like an expert’s report to a syndicate on the
said country. The district described comprises no
less than 20,000 square miles (!) of the N.E. corner
of the Northern Territory, and has apparently
been leased (or temporarily donated) for 42 years
from Oct. 1, ’99, at an annual rental of 6d. a sq.
mile, the lessee having the sole right (transferable)
to acquire mineral permits over any portion of
the territory, and also to acquire portions of it at
10s. per acre in fee simple, by the consent of the
Minister. The report of the expert—one J.
Bradshaw, F.R.G.S. —is a gaudy ana glowing
circumstance, and sets forth that large districts of
the region are very rich in gold and other minerals,
also that numerous herds of nuffaloes and wild
cattle abound and multiply. Says expert Brad-
shaw :
The eminent geologist, Tenison Woods, F.G.S.. after
making personal inspection of Arnheim’s Land—the N.E.
corner of Arafura Territory—expressed the opinion that
it would be found immensely rich in gold, silver, copper,
lead, tin and other minerals ; the recent discovery of
large lodes of galena on the Macarthur River tends to
confirm the opinion of Tenison Woods, as a few weeks
ago several tons of this galena were smelted at Mary-
borough, Q., and found to contain a large percentage of
silver. There is no doubt but that these lodes travel
northward and radiate into Arafura.
Also there are quartz and ironstone outcrops and
indication of coal in several places. The in-
genuous Bradshaw’s peroration concludes with a
fine flowing outburst:
The thermometer rarely rises above 95 deg. and the
monsoons are regular, cool and bracing. A considerable
revenue might be obtained from the buffalo now depas-
turing on the country—their hides being worth £1 each,
their horns from ss. to 10s. a pair, while their carcases
yield £4 per head in meat extract. What information we
have suggests possibilities rivalling those of Rhodesia or
any modern Ophir. No other similar area in Australia is
so richly endowed by nature as this ; ocean steamers
could enter any of the Arafura harbors and fill up with
cattle direct from the pastures. Apart from cattle-raising
and mining, I opine that your (the syndicate’s) country
might be able to supply in a few years the whole of the
artillery and cavalry horses for the entire British and
Indian armies.
Which is indeed a pleasing prospect for the in-
terested syndicators. But the reference to
Rhodesia is somewhat unfortunate, for the delect-
able Bradshaw proceeds :
By a system of firmness and tact I have had no diffi-
culty' in making good workers of the worst Australian
savages, and I have no doubt whatever but that the
natives of Arafura can be profitably utilised for all manner
of work, their remuneration being only food, tobacco, and
other trifles.
Also, the transparent Bradshaw thus charmingly
exposes the intentions of his employers, the Shin-
dy kit :
Should it be necessary to fulfill the pastoral covenants Oj
your lease, I estimate that 5000 head of cows could be
placed on the country at a cost of £12,500, thus, with this
increase, complying with the conditions—one head to each
square mile in three years.
There is, sad to say, one fly m the ointment :
The newly-revised land laws of the N.T. preclude the
granting of any future concessions on such terms as this
is held.
Apparently the intention of this amiable syndicate
is to evade as much of its covenants as it conveni-
ently can, to work the local nigger for all he is
worth and pay him as little as he can be induced
to accept, and to float sundry sub-syndicates to
work the transferred mining rights. The British
(the thing looks and smells British) boomster has
obviously got his paw on the N.T., despite all
warnings and precautions, and the Commonwealth,
when it takes over the Territory—which it should
do as soon as possible—will have to keep a very
sharp eye on that rapacious individual, lest he
swallow up the whole of the Northern province.
The most magnificent mineral-bearing district in
the country at Cd. a square mile—and don’t forget
to ask for coupons !

Woman Suffrage—for the Fifth Time
Victoria’s collective Fatman, he of the stolid and
immobile nature, is busy rounding up his few
fighters to battle once more against woman
suffrage. Four times have his representatives re-
jectee! the idea, and he hopes to delay matters by
asserting that there has been no demand for such
—and the time has not yet arrived for it. When
the Bill was first before the House nine years ago,
a petition bearing 30,000 signatures was presented t
in favor of it and nary a woman or even a small
girl petitioned against it, but this time (the
Bill comes before the 48 Fatmen this week) the
Tory remnant is getting up an anti-League
whose purpose it is to get a prayer signed
by the halt, the blind, the lame, and every-
body who can hold a pen. The League was un-
fortunate in its choice of secretaries, said ladies
being Misses Reid and Derham, but the speakers
at its first meeting were worse than unfortunate
in their alleged arguments. Said one: “He
objected most strongly to the forcing of the fran-
chise on the women of Victoria when they
had not asked for it, and he might say when
they did not want it.” And another pain-
fully illogical individual enquired how women
of the working classes were to form their
political opinions, and also whether politics would
not thus be brought to form a bone of bitter con-
tention between husband and wife. The gentle-
man’s tender solicitude for the working classes is
touching, but if he knew anything about those
erratic people he would understand that there are
457 different bones of contention between the
average man and his wife (including religion,
drink, the way she does her hair, and the brand of
tobacco he smokes) and yet the said bones do not
seem to interfere with their daily life to any extent.
Fatman objects to Woman Suffrage on general prin-
ciples. It is progressive, therefore it must be bad.
It would create a new set of voters who might de-
velop into original thinkers and workers; therefore
he views it with suspicion. Nevertheless, there are
signs that the 48 Landlords will this time put the
Bill through, with many distressing groans. It
has been rejected already four times, and even the
most refractory obesity tires of obstructing always.
The Voting-by-Post Bill—understood to have
been introduced partly to obviate the
necessity of Toorak’s delicate dames being con-
taminated by contact with the common plebeian
person at. a polling-booth—has passed the Lower
House. And, most powerful reason of all, Fat-
man wishes to stand well in the eyes of the coun-
try just now in view of the Federal elections and
the coming re-casting of the State Constitution.
So the 48 obstructives will probably come in out
of the wet.

Bananaland Parliament opened the otherday with
as little palaver as possible. Premier Philp’s
programme contained two noteworthy items, viz.,
a promise of an early introduction of one-adult-
one-vote, and a further promise of the introduc-
tion of certain private railway schemes. The
most obtrusive feature of the attitude of Banana-
land towards its present Govt, is the simple,
happy, undisguised desire of everybody to grab
something. North, South, East, West, the whole
community (despite its alleged financial pros-
perity) is imploring assistance from the Treasury.
Firstly, the graziers come along with a wail about
the rabbit invasion ; secondly, the Western pas-
toralists want a reduction of rent to compensate
them for their losses during the drought; thirdly,
everybody wants a new railway or a railway ex-
tension in his particular direction; and, lastly,
the Chows of Cairns are to the fore with a petition
for Government assistance in transporting their
bananas to Westralia, alleging with pride that
they are the backbone of the place. And, inas-
much as the present Govt, was returned to power
simply because it did promise nearly everything
to ev6rybody, it goes without saying that the
Philpocracv must make big. concessions some-
where ; otherwise the rabbit-infested grazier, the
drought-ruined squatter, and the Yellow Backbone
of Cairns will rise up as one man and cast the un-
satisfactory Govt, into the outer darkness. The
spectacle bids fair to be amusing. Between the
utter impossibility of satisfying everybody and
the urgent necessity of satisfying most of every-
body, the Philr Govt, is likely to have a happy
time.
As the various conferences of Melbourne’s subur-
ban councils on the question of creating a Greater
Melbourne have resulted in leaving matters as -they
were, and as the Govt, is too tired or too careless
to take the matter in hand, the Metropolitan
Committee of the A.N. A. proposes to grapple with
the subject. Its idea is to start by obtaining
Legislative enactment for a convention, popularly
elected, to draw up a scheme of union and a muni-
cipal constitution. Melbourne is at present gov-
erned by 24 municipal councils and seven practic-
ally-nominee boards and trusts, including the
supine Tramways Trust and the awful Board of
Works ; and, as each and every one of these sets
of councillors and commissioners is imbued with
an exaggerated sense of its own importance,
nothing visible or explosive or combustible is likely
to cause them to move in any desired direction.
The idea of taking the matter out of the hands of
aldermen and mayors, and relegating it to the
citizens themselves, is the best, most practical and
most democratic idea the A.N.A. has got hold of
in all its career.
• ♦ :
John Haynes, M.L.A., of N.S.W., is an ill-used
man in that, each time he brings forward his
Capital Punishment Abolition Bill, some miscreant
commits a specially horrible murder, apparently
for the express purpose of stirring up afresh the
blood-for-blood brigade. The notorious Butler
first played Haynes this scurvy trick, and now
the Breelong blackfellows have blocked him
again. All the same, capital punishment has got
to go. Barbarisms often die hard ; but die they
always must in the long run. Haynes,
however, was not wise in asserting that
all murderers are, ipso facto, insane. Con-
structively, this is doubtless pei’fectly true,
but the shallow-pated average advocate of
capital punishment would be but strengthened in
his opinion by an utterance which, to him, would
be mere foolishness. U.S. Congress, by-the-way,
has abolished the death-penalty in all federal con-
victions except those for treason and under the
articles of war of the army and navy, and for
murder and rape —thougli in these two latter
cases a jury is allowed to qualify its verdict by
adding “ without capital punishment,” this adden-
dum carrying with it the penalty of life imprison-
ment. Haynes has now given notice of a Bill to
abolish the extreme penalty for all crimes save
deliberate murder. This, The Bulletin thinks,
is the furthest step which N.S.W. Parliament is
likely to take at present —and it would be ex-
pedient to, as in America, place the power of life
or death in the hands of the jury.

N.S.W. Roads Department has struck a new
and what seems a very shabby method of dealing
with tenders in the distant backblox. The plan
followed is to call tenders for certain specified
road repairs, and when the offers are opened to
reject them all, at same time stating the price
named by the lowest tenderer. Next, to call for
tenders afresh. Obviously the tenderers, mostly
the same body of men, will judge that their next
try must be something under the quoted “lowest,”
and so the cutthroat game proceeds. Here is an
instance, from Scone Advocate :
Tenders were called in Feb. last for road contract No.
48, and on March 2nd. A. Windsor’s tender (£60115.) was
published as the lowest. Fresh tenders were called for,
and on March 20th., A Windsor’s tender for the same
amount was again published as the lowest. Still, fresh
tenders were again called, when A. Dickenson’s for £6O ss.
was accepted. It would be interesting for outsiders to
know what is the object of this. If £6O ss. was not too
high on May 18th, then £6olls. should have been accepted
on March 2nd.
This is indeed a fishy game.
The excision of the Lyne-Jesse? Orange duel
from N.S.W. official Hansard is a small thing in
itself but should lead to the establishment of a
principle on which proper excisions should be
made. Speaker M ‘Court is supposed to have
control in such matters, but is the House quite
satisfied to leave Hansard so wholly at the
scissors’ end as this latest incident seems to make
it? Politicians have little difficulty in “doctor-
ing” a newspaper or several newspapers. Recently
a wink caused the reporter of a “ great ” paper to
erase four indiscreet lines thoughtlessly uttered
by an angry Minister. In fact newspapers are
open for “suppression” in Parliament and else-
where unless the speaker be an enemy, and it was
the knowledge of this that incited the public to
go to the expense of setting up Hansard. If the
Speaker or other official can “doctor” Hansard
as it has been recently “ doctored,” the use of the
institution may be well called in question.

Maxwell, Jingo and “ Democrat,” sometime paid
factotum to Vic.’s National Ass., who was so
fully convicted of hopeless Tory bias during his
chairmanship of the Vic. Whiteworkers Board,
has been supplying the Murray party in
Warrnambool electorate with plentiful ammuni-
tion from his own speeches and faux pas. The
statutory declaration of the secretary of a Bible
League, read at one of Maxwell’s meetings, to
the effect that Murray (in a private overheard
conversation) larded a reference to a division upon
Scripture Education in State Schools with a
favorite Australian adjective on a specified but
ludicrously impossible date, has already passed
into the facetiae of hustings’ history. His denials
of a sympathetic leaning, before the Murray
episode, to the views of those English Noncon-
formists, like Stead, who oppose the Boer war
on traditional, religious and historical grounds,
are taken for what they are worth by an electorate
from which, as a candidate of the viciously Jingo-
istic, he wishes to eject Jack Murray, the honest
critic of exotic swashbucklerism in Australia.
Even afternoon meetings of select farmers
cannot ensure him a calm field for the
exercise of his acrobatic earnestness. Unlimited
enthusiasm pumped into skeleton political ideals
warranted to adjust their bones according to pass-
ing circumstances, may satisfy the average simply-
convolutioned brains of Maxwell’s favorite
Pleasant Sunday Afternoon audiences, but it will
hardly pass as current coin with a constituency
accustomed to the brainy and breezy democracy of
Jack Murray.
“Matabele” (who has “been there” to The
Bulletin :
What do all these people (mostly artizans) now rushing
off to the Cape think they are going to do there ? For
months before the war was in sight the Rand had its un-
employed problem, the good times of the early Nineties
were gone, wages were falling, and the Relief Committee
had its hands more than full. The RiiODES-owned press
said the depression was due to the “ oppressive exactions
of a corrupt oligarchy,” practical men knew it was due to
the fact that Johannesburg was built. During the war
there has been little destruction of property, and unless
a new Johannesburg requires building immediately most
of those now rushing thither will find themselves stranded.
Many people imagine that under British rule the gold
production of the Rand will go ahead by leaps and bounds,
bringing about general business prosperity and a great
demand for labour. The capitalistic project really is the
amalgamation (on same lines as Dp, Beers) of the three
great syndicates now controlling the Rand mines. The
effect of this combine will be just what it has been in
Kimberley—viz., great centralisation, great reduction in
wages total, great dividends, and great business stagna-
tion. De Beers Consolidated Ltd. was established in ’BB,
