Town Hall Scherek’s Orchestra (Saturday
afternoon).
Last week it was mentioned in this
column that the manager of the new Biograph—
the one which opened last Saturday at Sydney
Palace—guaranteed his pictures not to wobble.
This week The Bulletin chronicles with deep
regret that that new manager was less reliable
than he might have been, and that the new Bio-
graph does wobble. At the same time it has a less
agonising flicker than any other Biograph that
this writer has seen, and it seems to mark a dis-
tinct improvement in the science of these strange,
uncanny shadows that eat their food on a white
sheet in a dark theatre, The pictures, too, have
an air of comparative honesty about them that is
refreshing. None of the thrilling military epi-
sodes are obviously the work of a lot of dressed-up
supers in a circus. They are at least possible.
There is no evidence before the court that
the Palace war-pictures are genuine; The
Bulletin merely says that there is no posi-
tive evidence that they aren’t, and that
their improbability doesn’t fall on the spectator
like an infant that has just won the leaning-out
competition at a top window. Some of them
are very beautiful. One is exceedingly beautiful,
and worth the price of admission in itself —a view
from the front of a fast-moving engine in a hilly
country, in which bridges, tunnels, cuttings,
curves, and sundries rush headlong at the spectator
in an alarming fashion. The way the longest and
darkest tunnel throws itself head-first at the
audience, wraps its blackness round them like a
garment, and then dissolves again, is a thrill in
itself. Writer has done a spin on the front of an
engine, and the ’Graph gave exactly the same
sensation, all exoept the breathless rushing of
the air, the lost hat, and the cinder which
got in his eye. A certain painful tendency to put
on pictures of a monarch arriving somewhere, or
leaving somewhere, or looking at something, is a
painful feature of the Palace ’Graph, but all
’Graphs appear to have that drawback. As a
joy, and a work of art, and a scenic effect, the
railway picture is worth more than a view of 16
monarchs arriving everywhere, or one monarch
leaving 16 places at once. And there are al-
together too many pictures of somebody reviewing
troops—a number of soldiers standing in a row
and being stared at by some person of eminence.
The picture of one man staring at another man,
or at a lot of men, is not so very much of an
episode after all.
** * #
War-correspondent Donald Macdonald,
from South Africa, gave the first of a series of leo-
tures on the recent unpleasantness, last Saturday,
in Sydney Centenary Hall. Two pictures on the
programme represented the stout Donald as he
appeared before the siege of Ladysmith, and (in
the same hat and the same spotted tie) the thin
Donald who emerged from the beleaguered town
when Buller hammered his way in. A medium
Donald, half-way between the two pictured ones,
delivered the lecture. He did not get a very ex-
tended view of the war, having been shut up in
Ladysmith almost immediately after the armed
coolness commenced, and being invalided home
when the siege was over. But as regards the
siege of Ladysmith itself he can speak as
one having authority—that and a half-eaten
rat and a great resolve to hold out being
almost all they did have in the latter days
of the siege. Macdonald is a tall, strong, bony,
deliberate man, with a bad cold. He has none of
the airs and graces of the professional elocutionist
—the high top note in moments of excitement, the
deep premonitory rumbling in the boots when he
wants to be impressive, the studied flappings of
the arms that look as if they had been practised
before a mirror. He doesn’t stop to take a drink
just when he has worked up almost to the climax
of a great thrill, so as to give the thrill a chance to
soak in, but takes a drink whenever he feels so
inclined—which is frequently. In short, he is
straight, direct, easy, and natural —a big, vigor-
ous, impressive-looking man with a bad cold, who
has a good story to tell, and has learned that the
best way to tell a story is just to tell it—and who
flaps his arms after the manner of an
unspoiled child of nature, who simply
flaps as he wants to and doesn’t care
about the finer details. As he lectures
he prowls up and down the platform as a man
might who had been besieged a long time and
wanted exercise. There is nothing very new in
his lectures, but the same news that looked cold
and inky in the paper sounds very differently
when told by the man who was there, and who
personally sought for copy on the kopje and fished
his facts out of swollen drifts. Altogether Donald
Macdonald promises to be a success in N.S.W.;
£2 wouldn’t pay for the boot-leather which was
worn out on the floor of Centenary Hall last
Saturday, and five good umbrellas were crumpled
up in the applause.

Bland Holt’s “Absent-Minded Beggar
continues to betray its principal mental charac-
teristic at Sydney Royal, and Bland’s advertise-
ment states proudly that “this latest melodrama-
tic production takes the palm.” The Bulletin
has already recognised that it takes something,
but the palm wasn’t exactly the thing that this
paper was thinking of. Yet it recognises that the
piece is about disreputable enough to take almost
anything that might happen to be lying round
loose, and if a palm was foolishly left in its
vicinity it would probably be as ready to take
that as anything else. No change of programme
is announced yet, but the writer has hopes.

To-morrow (Friday) night Alfred Dampier
varies the programme at Sydney Criterion by
doing one consecutive production of “Hamlet.”
Inasmuch as Dampier is a vastly better Prince of
Denmark than many persons who have put on
enormous frill, and got the members of their co.
to write long letters to the papers about them,
the Shakespeare-loving public is cordially invited
to roll up. The “Green Lanes of England”
arrive on Saturday.
** * *
The mild gloom that has hung over Syd-
ney Tivoli off and on the last few months is now
partly dispersed by the re-appearance of Billee
Barlow in Elizabethan locks. Billee’s humor con-
sists in calling various portions of the audience
Gussie and Charlie, and imploring them in
agonised tones to come up on the stage and cheer
her up. Each song requires a different costume,
and the Charlie and Gus ditty is specially honored
with one that looks as if made out of gold and
sunshine intermingled. Ferry the Frog does his
turn at Rickards’ now, and emulates successfully
Lewis Carroll's crocodile which walked along its own
tail till it finished by marching down its nose.
Th 6 Coon Ballet is a pretty study in red and
white, and Miss Daisy Coppin makes her first
appearance (under Rickards) in a dance where she
forms into a wheel part of the time. The
Emmonds-Emmerson trio have introduced into
their latest sketch a patriotic song about the girl
who married a man for his wealth and is conse-
quently a bird in a gilded cage. Some day a
genius will arise to weep over the girl who never
gets the chance of a gilded coop, and has to take
the common iron variety or do without.
JACKY GOVERNOR, “ Wanted ” as one of the
alleged Breelong murderers. —From photo.
taken while he was a police-tracker.
“Fedora,” one of Sardou’s best, but
longest-winded tragedies, came to pass at Her
Majesty’s (Melb.) on Saturday night. Everybody,
m a r olite way of speaking, has been familiar, at
one time or other, with the story of the modem
Russian princess who came to grief over her own
misplaced vengeance, but hardly anyone seems to
get it riveted to the memory. It is a story in a
prologue, three acts, and a suicide, owing its
tragedy on the stage to the assassination of three
people off the stage, three people who don’t
appear in the cast. The first victim was
Princess Fedora’s best boy before the curtain
rose. His assassination (“off”) is discussed
in the prologue, and the evidence of things
unseen is said to point to Count Ipanoff
as the slayer. Fedora, being very Russian
in her loves and hates, follows Ipanoff _ from
St. Petersburg to Paris, and enchants him into a
confession of the deed, and meanwhile loses her
heart to the man. He is quite a nice Count, as it
turns out, whereas the fellow who perished in the
prologue was an undeserving object of Fedora’s
affection, but she has set her royal vengeance
moving before the whole truth is revealed, and
although she manages to save her Ipanoff in
Paris, she can’t prevent the official murder of his
relatives in Russia. The news of their decease
and the discovery that Fedora was responsible for
the little mistake would probably lead to some
vengeance on Ipanoff’s part if the regretful
Princess didn’t take poison to avoid further
unpleasantness.
* * * *
To say, as doubtless the Melbourne press
will say, that Nance O’Neil gives a fine perform-
ance of the name-part is to ignore all the obtrusive
features of the lady’s personality. Author Sardou
gives Nance O’Neil no chance to shape con-
vincingly in such a character. Fedora, firstly,
lastly, and all the time must be a Russian princess
with a true Parisian polish on her, a woman of
courtly arts and graces—in short, a “dream” of
bon ton. Now, Miss O’Neil, as Fedora, is simply
a reality of Nance. She talks to men with the
intonation and gestures and hard commercial
frown of a stock-broker laying down the law to a
minor clerk. True, there is force in her
misrepresentation of the character, the natural
force of a weighty, self - confident Nance
O’Neil. Her capacity for acting as though
she thinks she is doing the right things may
impress those who can’t see that she is doing
the wrong things. The present scribe is bomb-
proof as regards Miss Nance O’Neil. She can’t
blow his critical faculty away with the breath of her
heavy declamation. As Magda, she had novelty, in
so far that she was a vast and not too-artistic
actress playing in a bossful manner a part that
only a very first-class artist could do full justice
to. And as Camille, in which character her
pathos sounded rich, though artificial, Miss O’Neil
was also interesting at the first time of hearing
her. But as Princess Fedora, grabbing her
silk skirt like another Charley’s Aunt, the effect
of Miss O’Neil is eminently unsatisfactory to the
Philistine who records this opinion. The fine
play of emotion that Sardou asks of his heroines
is not even attempted by the lady. She just slogs
at the bowling, and the public like it. Kingston
would be a better Count Ipanoff but for his ner-
vous grimaces. Plimmer and Carne are pleasing,
but not very incisive, as Jean de Lerieux and
Gretch; also, Miss Roxy Barton is a pleasing
young failure as Countess Olga. The staging is
so-so, and the small parts —no matter. Besides,
two or three are neatly filled.
** * *
At the Princess’s, where business seems
to suffer from the Nance boom, “ The Tyranny of
Tears ” was warmly received on Saturday. Had-
don Chambers has written nothing else half so
good as his latest play ; the quality of his dialogue
is so remarkable as to lend certain colour to the
London assertion that Oscar Wilde —another
version gives the credit to a very brilliant woman
—had a hand in the piece. However this maybe,
the play slips along at an even pace, never going
too far in any direction, and it leaves one in doubt
as to whether it was a moral lesson or a charming
study of cause and effect. Brough, the married
man of the plot, has been writing novels and things
for five years, without once getting out on the
loose. His devotion to his wife (Miss Noble) is
sincere but irksome at times. She’s a dear little
woman in her way, and would be even dearer if
she were less often in his way. Just when poor
Brough is busy with a violent leading article for
the Daily Buster his domestic angel thrusts tea
and sentiment upon him, and whispers a mysteri-
ous something in his ear, whereat he laughs con-
jugally. The audience would give sixpence each
to hear her say that something aloud, but they
have to rest dissatisfied with the whisper.
Bachelor friend Lovell proposes to take Brough
away for a week on a small innocent yacht, built
for gentlemen only, and then his wife weeps. She
has been an artist at weeping for five moist, happy
years, so Brough seizes this opportunity for trying
to dry her up in moderation. He succeeds more
or less at the finish (or, rather, he seems likely
to succeed in the future), but the happy
ending _is delayed by the carelessness of
the spinster-secretary (Mrs. Brough), who
gets caught in the act of imprinting a motherly
kiss on his photo. Mrs. Brough’s kiss leads to
complications, and she weeps—but not much.
She is a calm, practical young sec., with a robust
appetite for seed-cake in the hour of trouble, and
bachelor Lovell arranges to marry her at an early
date. These four characters, plus a jaunty old sport
of a father-in-law, runtheshowadmirably. Brough s
performance has little fault, bar his old mannerism
of thinking with his mouth open. Miss Noble
scores another success. Her art is not broad, but
it begins to go deep. Mrs. Brough, who could be
brighter in the first three acts, takes a full half-
share of the honors in the scene where Lovell puts
the marriage question, and Lovell plays his part
to perfection everywhere. It fits him all the way
down. Dartrey is a joyous, wicked father-in-law,
and the scenic artist achieved a double encore for
his garden scene on Saturday. Also, the orchestra
had to repeat the “Cavalleria” intermezzo by
vociferous desire.

“The World Against Her” at the Royal

At the Bijou the Martinettis substituted
“A Terrible Night” for “Robert Macaire” on
Saturday night, and this second lesson in the art
of acting hit a crowded house pretty hard. Paul
Martinetti and his dumb show company are mat-
ters that naturally gather admiration as they go
along in cities where they happen to be new.
Irene Franklin, the ’cute Yankee damsel who
looks rather older than 16, was back at the Bijou
on Saturday, and the Bijou felt glad to see Irene.
Also, a new infant of the male persuasion proved
more acceptable than some recent atoms in short
frocks.

Scherelc’s Orchestrians spread themselves
again over the Town Hall platform last Saturday
afternoon, and were appreciated as per usual.
Miss Una Bourne, the demure young pianist, like-
wise added a couple of successes to her record, and
Stoneham, the flautist, came forth as the com-
poser of a tiny little dance piece. Scherek tries
experiments on the Melbourne public these times.
Probably he was glad to discover that a Strauss
waltz is a little too tinkling for them nowadays.
Popular taste in orchestral music has been edu-
cated to a point higher than Strauss. Two points
higher, perhaps. Another concert next Saturday.
THE ROSE OF PERSIA.
A love-tale quaint and flighty of old Persia’s
empire mighty as it was in the olden, golden
days of Sultan Mahmoud Khan,
When there were gauds and glory and a wealth of
curious story in Sultan Mahmoud’s palace in
turreted Ispahan.
Now in these ages dusty, in her lonesome halls
and musty, a Persian Queen was barred away
for the days of all her life.
And Queen Rose-in-Bloom grew weary of her
latticed chambers dreary, and forth on an
ancient Persian bust fared Sultan Mahmoud’s
wife —
On an innocent, ancient Asian bust, just to get
rid of her mental rust, fared the Sultan’s
cherished wife.
In that grey old town enchanted, down the
streets by asses haunted, where were beggars,
dogs and dervishes, and hairy camels tall,
She wandered on disguised till by her lord sur-
prised, when she wildly fled for refuge in at
Hassan’s open hall.
Hassan’s wives were five-and-twenty; he had ill-
got wealth in plenty; he was a kind of
Abdul-Hooley floating “cats” in Ispahan.
Also, he was charitable, and he fed the hungry
rabble, and just then he was drunker than an
ordinary man,
So wildly, joyously rushing on doom, with a
beery eye on his open tomb, he jeered at
Mahmoud Khan.
For, full of drink and laughter, heedless what
might happen after, Hassan dreamt he was a
Sultan, and gave orders cheerfully
That peers eminent and dreaded should forthwith
be beheaded, and chamberlains and tetrarchs
be slain promiscuously.
And the Sultan’s stricken minions said : “ In all
these wide dominions there aren’t trees
enough whereon to hang him for his sin,
And if, pausing not nor stopping, we keep him
for ages dropping, there aren’t wells enough
in Persia to drop this scoffer in.”
And the Grand Vizier was stricken dumb as he
felt that the world to an end would come ere
they’d sufficiently dropped him in.
So in the next day’s morning, to all drinkers as a
warning, they sat Hassan on the golden throne
in royal Ispahan,
And with Asjan oaths profound swore as they
fro veiled round that he was in the purple
orn as Sultan Mahmoud Khan.
And though he wasn’t quite awake yet, and his
nerves were all a-shake yet, and his hand was
so unsteady that his beer dropped on the floor,
And he had a giddy feeling that the Persian
throne was reeling, yet he ran the empire
wisely for half-an-hour or more
(Just then the writer’s ’bus was going, and the
sands of time were flowing, so he seized his
hat and ’brella and ran swiftly for the door).
Sheep to the Slaughter!
When is this insane folly going to stop? Here
we are, a nascent nation to whose development
ardent young manhood is essential, sending
away the flower of our population—the vital
element of which we have too little, of which we
can never have enough —to be used up in Britain’s
foreign wars. Thousands of fine young fellows
have been sent to the Transvaal at a heavy money
cost to Australasian taxpayers —and at a far
heavier cost in the loss of productive energy.
Already many have been killed, many are coming
home crippled and broken-down to be burdens on
their friends or on the community, and some,
doubtless, will be bribed to stay as settlers under
the Rhodesian companies. The Parliaments who
vote them away do not care; their skins and
salaries are safe; and that is all many so-called
legislators think about. The shouting crowds
■who saw them away do not care ; for a crowd sub-
merges all sense of individual responsibility. It
is true that already the bottom has fallen out of
the local Jingo bucket, so lately brim-full of froth.
Many of those instrumental in sending Australians
to leave their bones in Africa, or to colonise Africa,
are secretly ash aped of themselves. Neverthe-
less, Victoria and N.S. Wales are announced as
sending small contingents to fight in China.
The Legislative Assemblies of Victoria and
N.S. Wales are not wholly without sense.
They must know that the encouragement of this
haphazard militarism is cutting the throat of Aus-
tralian industry. “Glory” will not profit this
country. It will not profit this country that the
man who should be ploughing land or digging
minerals in Australia, making a home and rearing
a family, should be cut off in his prime and leave
his bones in the graveyard of China. Even if the
five nations who are grabbing China deserved
Australian help, we could not afford to give
it. That is the sheer truth—toe can't afford
it. We are living now in a fool’s paradise of bor-
rowed money, discounting our future and piling
up debts for our own children to pay. When the
stream of loan-money stops running and we have
to face our liabilities; we shall want every
ounce of energy, every atom of resource.
To waste men and money in foreign
warfare which does not intimately concern us is
suicidal. Many M’s.P., who dare not stand up
in the House and say so, will tell you that they
are fully persuaded of this. Yet N.S. Wales,
for example, has drifted into this Chinese-
contingent business just because nobody had
the courage to say No! We read that 154
men of # the Naval Brigade and Naval Artil-
lery have volunteered. There is no evidence
that they wanted to volunteer. Apparently some
officer buttonholed the Ministry, and the Ministry,
though no doubt (speaking for most of them)
utterly tired of war, lacked backbone to refuse a
conditional promise. By the time the promise
reached Parliament it was an absolute offer, and
Govt, cracked'the party whip to bring dissen-
ters to their places. Then the .men were called
on to volunteer “ for the honour of the
corps ? ” How could they refuse with the
example of Harkus before them ! The moral
compulsion of the corps-spirit bound them
just as much as convicts’ chains and fetters.
The man who thought of his wife and family and
hung back was confronted with the odium of the
“ white feather” which left Mrs. Harkus a widow
and her children fatherless. They were driven
like sheep to the slaughter, and 154 of them put
down their names. Some of them, doubtless,
really wished to go ; but scores did not, and The
Bulletin believes that the majority will live to
curse the day when they were dragged into the
contingent—if they do not curse it already. And
these men are fine fellows in the prime of life—
many of them men who, after service in the
Imperial navy, had got steady shore-billets and
settled down, as they thought, for life. The great
majority of them are fathers of families —the very
men that the community cannot spare. How
many of them will ever come back ? How many
will be sent to their death at the bidding of the
insane spirit of militarism, chattering politicians,
and esprit-de-corps ?
The s.s. Annerley, which, in a storm be-
tween Newcastle and Melbourne, recently, lost 221
out of 283 bullocks intended for S’Africa, has
since lost 201 horses out of 256 which she shipped
at Melbourne for Durban. A horrible record !

Has Many Imitators ; But No Equals.
Hoffmann, the London chairman of White
Feather Main Reef, speaks from South African experience
concerning machinery. He has learned the value of
heavier machinery than is usually erected in the West,
where the stamps should be capable of treating 4J tons
per day, instead of only 2£ tons —the general limit with
the batteries that he saw. Nor is it necessary to send
’Ome for the right stuff. Of the plant at Bayley’s, which
he instanced as “ one of the finest I have seen,” Hoffmann
said “ If people would only adopt that style of machinery
they would do much better. They make the mistake of
putting up stamps that are too light, small boxes, and
small surface tables.” Furthermore he mentioned that
the O.K. machinery in question “ can be constructed by
Martin and 00. of Gawler, at only about half the cost of
the imported plant, and is to all intents and purposes its
equal."*

The carelessness of certain steam-users in not
looking after their boilers and the results of neglecting to
clean them regularly when incrustation takes place has
often been pointed out in Tub Bulletin. A disencrustat-
ing boiler compound known as “ Litofuge,” with a world-
wide reputation, is being placed upon the market by W.
and J. Lempriere, 506 Little Collins-st., Melbourne, who
upon application will be pleased to forward catalogues to
those interested. “ Litofuge ” compounds are powders
without soda, fatty matter or injurious chemicals, which
injure rather than improve boilers. Its action is mechani-
cal and its introduction into a boiler causes no foaming.*

The Wild Cat Column.
The Australian Joint Stock Bank hasn t shared
in the improvement manifested by so many banking in-
stitutions this half-year, in fact its balance-sheet for six
months to 30th June, 1900, looks much worse than its
predecessor. Some recent results :

The profits represent 6s. 9d. per cent on the shareholders’
capital (£1,168,041) and the nominal reserve fund
(£52,000). If the special reserve fund of £109,473, created
by the repurchase of B deposits at a discount, is included,
then the profits represent only 6s. 2d. per cent. During
the half year the A.J.S. Bank has made a further sum of
£20,565 by the repurchase of its B deposits at a discount,
so it has now these amounts available for writing off
losses without trenching on the capital:
Accumulated Profit £12,064
Reserve Fund .. .. .. .. . • • • 52,000
Special Reserve (discounts on Deposits purchased) 109,473
£173,537
During the half-year the reconstructed deposits have been
reduced by £320,752 and now stand at £5,369,289.
Probably the most remarkable feature about the
A.J.S. Bank nowadays is the dead silence maintained
as to its real position by everybody concerned. The
balance-sheet doesn’t even profess to state the real
situation, or anything like it. Nearly four-fifths of the
assets are described as “ Bills discounted and all other
debts due to the Bank and other assets at old valuations,
£6,719,518.” The fact that these assets were once
supposed to be worth the sum mentioned is in-
teresting but not instructive. The site of Babylon
was also worth a lot at one time. The point at
issue is : What are they worth now ? Assuming that a
bank which gets over £5,000,000 of its deposits very
cheaply, and has them fixed for so long a time that it is
unnecessary to keep any cash reserves against them,
should be able, when its assets are written down to actual
values, to earn 4 per cent, on its funds, which isn’t a
widely extravagant assumption, then the above-mentioned
asset requires to be written down by about £1,250,000.
This would mean writing off all the various reserves and
nearly £1,100,000 of the capital, and starting the A.J.S.
Bank afresh with a paid-up capital of about £90,000. The
directors never explain what the losses are estimated to
be ; whether they intend to clear them off some day by
writing down capital and reserves and calling up
fresh capital, and thus start the A.J.S. Bank
afresh as a small but solvent institution; whe-
ther they simply intend to wait till the slow
accumulation of profits, possibly aided by a rise in values,
wipes out the losses, and when they hope that that will
be ; whether the depreciated properties are further de-
preciating or not, and, if so, if the further depreciation is
swallowing up the alleged profits ; whether the capital
and reserves are likely to cover the losses —in fact, they
give no information at all about the real position of the
bank. And under the disgracefully loose banking laws of
Australia it is perfectly lawful to put admittedly unreliable
values in a balance-sheet, and thus make it a document
which isn’t of the slightest value as showing the real
position of affairs. If the Federal Parliament enacts that
the assets in a company’s balance-sheet must represent
real present values, and puts on Government auditors and
valuators to see that they do, The Bulletin knows of
several banks which will have to publish very different
documents from those they issue now.
* * * *
When The Bulletin suggests that the A. J.S.
Bank, with nominal paid-up capital and reserves of
£1,341,579, may be really worth as much as £90,000 it
takes a much more sanguine view than the share-market
does. The market estimates the shares (£7 10s. paid up)
at about fifteenpence per share, which makes the whole
value of the bank about £9BOO. The share-market,
therefore, assumes the losses still unprovided for to
be about £1,330,000.

The Ballarat Banking Co., for the half-year to
30th June, 1900, shows the largest profits which it has
realised for four years past. Recent earnings have been
as follows: — _
December, 1894 .. £3710 December, 1897 .. £2637
June, 1895 .. .. 3449 June, 1898 .. .. 2785
December, 1895 .. 3463 December, 1898 .. 2906
June, 1896 .. .. 3250 June, 1899 .. .. 3022
December, 1896 .. 2936 December, 1899 .. 2991
June, 1897 .. .. 2881 June, 1900 .. .. 3154
The usual quaint and angular-looking dividend at the
rate of 5* per cent, per annum absorbs £2337 10s.; an
addition of £5OO is made to the reserve fund, raising it to
£56,000, and the other £316, added to £2617 brought for-
ward from last half-year, leaves an undivided profit
balance of £2933. The earnings are equivalent to
£4 9s. Id. per cent, per annum on shareholders’ capi-
tal and reserve. During the half-year deposits have
increased hy £11,390, and cash and securities by
about £lO,OOO. The Ballarat Banking Co.’s reserve
of coin and liquid securities is £87,293, representing 38
per cent, on all liabilities to the public. Also it has one
advantage which many small banks possess in a much
greater degree than large ones ; its capital and reserves
are very large in proportion to liabilities to the public—
so large that even if the value of its advances deteriorated
by a good deal more than one-half it would still be a per-
fectly solvent concern so far as its depositors were con-
cerned. During the last six years the Ballarat Banking
Co., in addition to paying its 5* per cent, dividend regu-
larlv, has added £9134 to its reserves, and its funds are now :
Paid-up capital, £85,000 ; reserve-fund, £56,000 ; profit
balance, £2933.

The Royal Bank of Queensland reports that the
net earnings for the half-year which ended 30th June,
1900, were £8383, which is another slight increase, and
compares with previous earnings a» follows :
June, 1894 .. .. £6360 December, 1897 .. £7440
December, 1894 .. 6477 June, 1898 .. .. 7460
June, 1895 .. .. 5946 December, 1898 .. 7579
December, 1895 .. 6033 June, 1899 .. .. 7830
June, 1896 .. .. 6445 December, 1899 .. 8322
December, 1896 .. 6515 June, 1900 .. .. 8383
June, 1897 .. .. 7463
The earnings represent £3 17s. 9d. per cent, per annum
on the shareholders’ capital and reserve-fund, which is
exactly the same rate as was earned last half-year. The
usual dividend at the rate of 2* per cent, per annum ex-
hausts £4815 ; dividend duty thereon is £241; and £-WX)
is added to reserve-fund, raising it to £48,000. ine re-
maining £1327, added to £2595 brought forward, leaves
£3922 at credit of profit and loss, and out of this the
management has decided to put £3500 to a contingency-
fund, presumably to provide for possible losses and depre-
ciation, leaving £422 to carry forward.
* * • •
The establishment of a contingency fund is cer-
tainly a wise provision ; still, it is so unusual a one that
it will probably create an impression that the Royal Bank
of Queensland sees losses ahead, and set people wondering
as to the possible size of the same. No reason is assigned
for establishing the new fund, apart from the general
reserve, so, in the absence of any special symptoms, it
should doubtless be regarded as a mere commonsense pre-
caution. Things in general look reasonably well with the
Royal Bank. Deposits have increased in the half-year by
nearly £45,000. Cash and liquid securities have increased
by a little over £45,000, and amount to £356,321 —equal to
nearly 40 per cent, of the total liabilities to the public,
which is a very Bolid line of defence. Also the reserve
fund is a real reserve, separately invested in Government
securities. The Royal has just paid the third instalment
of its reconstructed deposits, and in about 18 months
from now they will be finally extinguished.

The Mercantile Mutual has thus earned £84,525 in 7*
years, out of which £32,349 has gone in bonuses to policy-
holders ; £32,349 in shareholders’ dividends; £lOOO has
been set aside to cover depreciation of assets; £11,250
has been capitalised, thus increasing the paid - up
capital from £15,000 to £26,250 ; and £7577 has been
added to the undivided profit balance, raising it from
£lOBl to £8658. No additions have been made to reserve
fund, which remains stationary at £40,000. The addi-
tional capital having come out of profits, the dividend
has really increased instead of diminished, being over 30
per cent, on the capital as it stood in 1893. The Mercan-
tile Mutual is a rather silent company as regards the
nature of its assets, merely stating in a reserved sort of
way that these consist mostly of “ city property and
mortgages, £67,339,” and “ deposits, fixed and at call,
£24,063.” The interest earnings (£2255) represent only
£2lss. sd. per cent, per annum on the investments,
which is very low. Still the Mercantile Mutual is a
bright and shining business example in almost every
respect. Especially interesting is the fact that it produces
such excellent results by confining its business to one
province, when one considers the gall of the bogus in-
surance experts who declare that State fire insurance
can’t pay because it will be necessarily confined to one
province and “ a wide diffusion of risks is necessary to the
successful conducting of fire insurance.”
* • » •
Apropos, the once-great Palatine Insurance Co.,
of England, is arranging to give up the ghost and sell
out, being practically killed, as usual, by the fetish of “ a
wide diffusion of risks.” It bought up the Manufacturers’
and Builders’ Insurance 00. of New York, and diffused
itself in that country, and the far-off foreign business,
not being sufficiently under the eye of the management,
was an unspeakable disaster.

J. S. Foster: The Mutual Life Association is
much the better office. It is impossible to say in advance
what bonuses you will get—they all depend on profits.
And bonuses are not everything ; safety is a big essential.

• • • •
It is rather a serious matter to hint to any old-
established pawnbroking institution that its finance is a
trifle reckless, yet the fact remains that in eight years the
reserves of the N.S.W. Mont de PietC have diminished by
£79oo—from £16,305 to £8405. On three occasions the
dividend alone has exceeded earnings, and on nine
occasions dividend and provision for losses have exceeded
earnings. For 6* years the dividend was kept up to 10
per cent when the case didn’t at all justify it, and it is
now being kept up to 7* per cent when 6 per cent or even
6 might be much better. The present balance-sheet hints
that there are still losses to be provided for, though no
amount is mentioned and the allusion is very indefinite.
The most promising feature is that profits are again on
the increase.
* * * *
Commercial Building and Investment Co.,
Sydney :
June, 1897 Loss .. £3145 I June, 1899 Loss .. £2993
„ 1898 „ .. 4037| „ 1900 „ .. 933
The Commercial Building and Investment Co. has, after
writing off all reserves, a debit balance of £9293, though if
it wrote off its forfeited shares it could clear this liability
and have a small balance to the good and about £120,000
of real capital intact. Its troubles arose through owning
so much more property than it could pay for that ft
owed, in 1897, £155,678 on mortgage, and in the de-
pressed state of the market the interest on the mortgages
absorbed the rents and something over. The mortgages
are now down to £135,275, and as they are reduced, the
institution comes steadily nearer to paying its way again.
In fact, it looks as if it might become a good source of profit
again when it gets rested, and further reduces its mort-
gages, and is able to write down its property values out
of earnings.
* * * *
A. M.P.—Absent-Minded Proxy-paper.
♦ * • *
For the first time within the memory of the
oldest policy-holder the A.M.P. directors enclosed a
stamped envelope with the ambiguous proxy-forms. On
all other occasions members wishing to vote by proxy
had, rightly, of course, to return their papers at their own
expense, but this time the directors were anxious to get
in a large number of papers unaltered. Consequently,
with a stamped envelope ready to his hand, the average
voter, to whom the act of voting is a bore, slipped the
ballot-paper into the envelope first, popped the envelope
into the post, and thought about it afterwards. As a
large proportion of the members live in the twopenny
area, the cost of departing from their usual practice when
circularising their 150,000 policy-holders must have run
into an uncalled-for expense, approaching £lOOO.
Another curious fact in connection with the extension
controversy is that the Melb. Argus and Age agreed in
their views on the subject—a most astonishing circum-
stance. The rival journals advocated the rejection of the
proposed trip abroad.
The A.M.P. directorate worked its game all
right with the stamped-envelope-for-return-of-proxies
racket; but t’other day when A.M.P. chairman Abbott
stood for election as an A.J.S. director he somehow
forgot to send stamped envelopes to shareholders, and so
he “ got left ” by his opponent, who did send stamped
envelopes.
• * * *
B. North half-yearly report to June 30 is
issued very promptly ; it was in shareholders’ hands by
the middle of July. It would have been better perhaps,
though, if the board had been a little longer about it, and
had then told shareholders more. In fact, for all there is
in the directors’ report it might almost have been
written twelve months ago. Blasting among the few
figures at the end shakes out the fact (anyhow, it is more
or less a fact) that the co.’s profit was £11,261; but
neither new machinery nor depreciation is charged,
and one or the other ought to be. A co. must either
charge itself with the cost of its plant as it gets it or
charge itself term by term with depreciation. As to finan-
cial position, the North appears to have liquid assets
amounting to £14,530, of which £6331 is hard cash, while
it owes only £1823 ; and that, after paying £7500 in divs.
last half-year, cannot be reckoned bad for a co. which
gave only about £7OOO for its mine four or five years ago.
In fact, the co.’s paid-up capital amounts to only £lO,OOO
altogether. ,
B.H. Prop.’s half-yearly report (to May 30)
shows that the co. made its £96,000 of divs. and had £l9l
to carry over ; and that is sailing closer to the wind than
ever the co. did before since the div. was reduced to a
shilling a quarter. And yet to get even this result the co.
had to use up more ore than it has been doing since ’95.
Figures herewith
Ore Raised. Net Profit,
tons. £

Mav, 1898 204,731 ... 139,865

Which isn’t too “ grateful and comforting” ; and still less
when one comes to reckon up that silver has been a shade
better this last half-year than it was in the previous term
and that lead averaged a whole £ll4s. 7d. a ton better.
Here are the highest and lowest prices realised for the
co.’s principal metals for some years past:
Silver Lead

1899 .. 2 4| 2 2) 17* 13
1900 (*-year) 2 3| 2 2J 17* 16
So that if lead had stayed where it was five years ago, and
other things had been the same, the B.H. Prop.’s divi-
dend would now be half of nothing. But things would
not have been the same; for if the old slump
price had been maintained there would have had to be a
selection of the ores, and the richest would have been
taken and the rest left ; and at that rate a few years at
the most would have seen the end. As it is, there has to
be some picking and choosing ; in fact the directors say
that in recently putting up ore-freights ’the S.A. railway
people “ prevent the co. from dealing with a portion of its
product owing to the fact that at such a rate it would not
pay to send it to Pt. Pirie for treatment.” Anyhow,
there must be a line somewhere between what is payable
and what isn’t ; and since, even counting in the rich oxi-
dised ores which are still available to some extent, the
average profit per ton of ore was only Bs. 3d., it is clear
that a penny or two must make all the difference between
profit and loss as to a great quantity of the poorer sul-
phides.
Supposing that all the recent sprightliness in
the lead market cannot be kept up (though the B.H.
Prop, directors expect that it will be sprightlier than
ever for a while), the co. does not expect to have its profits
much, if at all, whittled away ; though, when one comes
to think of it, the B.H. Prop, has not been much of a
success at foretelling the periodical reduction of profits
which has occurred. Present disbursements are only 25
per cent, of what they were in the days when the wicked
miners made sleeping-chambers of the underground work-
ings and the ore somehow got itself to surface. Several
things, it is said, are going to help the co. in the future.
First, the briquetting plant is at work now ; so the concen-
trates and fine ore can be made into cubes and then, fed
on them, the furnaces will run better. Next, the big
concentrating mill is also running better, treating more
ore and getting out a higher percentage of metal at less
cost. Third, the open-cuts will yield better results,
because there will not be so much surface waste to get rid
of. Fourthly to tenthly, numerous economies are being
introduced in machine construction. Eleventhly, the co.
has a good mind to erect its own coke ovens on account
of the irregularity of the present supply and the high-
deep-sea freights ; it has already secured a suitable site.
As to magnetic concentration and the marketing of zinc
concentrates, all that is said is that experiments are pro-
ceeding, but that results so far are not good enough to
launch out upon. Which is a different tune to that sung
recently by other directors.

Wanaderry, N.S.W., gold-field is looking up.
The other day claimholder Owen washed half a load for
loz. 18dwt. Another man got 2oz. from one dish.

Eldorado, N.S.W. —Said to have struck some-
thing at 275 ft. level which looks like the continuation of
the Drysdale shoot. Drillings assaying 340 z., but a bulk
sample said to have gone only three. Shares doubled in
value in a few days and are still very low if it is true that
the Drysdale shoot, or another like it, is in the^ground.
“A Shareholder” : Mine has no market value,
and when the market does not talk it’s time to think very
little of the mine.

News of what looks like an important find is
wired from Kalgoorlie. A rich discovery is reported to
have been made about 12 miles from West Leonora by Mr.
R. Penny, a prospector, who found surface stone worth
160 oz. on a reef. He sunk on it 20ft., at which depth the
stone is s*ft. wide, coarse gold showing freely in the
stone. The reef outcrops the full length of the 24-acre
lease.

SOCIETY
Public opinion is based on scare-heads.
The papers are now devoting their scare-type to
Chinese cables, and the Boer kick-up receives
c[uite casual headings. Consequently, the public
is in a fair way to entirely forget Kruger and the
Boers, and to quite overlook the doings of “Our
Boys” at the very time when “Our Boys” are
engaged in real fight. A quarter-page of black
and rampant capitals would have been given to
those recent Orange State battles had they hap-
pened four months back. The average man buys
a pen’orth of opinions every morning, and they
last him all day.
* * * *
Gossip has it that the State Governor of
N.S.W., to take the post when Gov. Beauchamp
moves, is already as good as appointed, and that
Premier Lyne’s hurry to get Parliament to fix the
potentate’s house, salary, and allowances is the
outcome of another message marked “ confiden-
tial.” If the Leg. Assembly lets itself be hood-
winked in this matter it will deserve a general
scatter. The items of the proposed scheme pan out
thus : £20,000 for a mansion in its own grounds ;
£5OOO a year salary and £lOOO a year allowances,
with a five years’ term of office. The whisper is :
“This is the time to get it through; then, if,
when the Federation starts, Parliament talks of
cutting down, it will be tied for five years, or will
have to indemnify the officer with the whole
amount.” The officer designated has, it is said,
insisted tliEtt some such security must be provided.
*m• • *
N.S. W. Premier Lyne, in his anxiety to
get the Governor-General to fix his residence in
Sydney, proposes to evict State Governor
Beauchamp just as summarily as if he were an
Irish tenant in arrears with his rent, and cast him
forth to live in a terrace or some such place. By
this gross inhospitality he will get Government
House clear for Gov.-General Hopetoun. Vic-
toria, not having any State Excellency just now—
only a temporary Madden —can offer its Govern-
ment House without having to throw anybody out
in the cold, and when it gets a new State Gover-
nor can warn him in advance that lie will have to
live in a boarding-house, so that there will be no
misunderstanding. That may seem hard enough,
but it is nothing to bringing out a State Governor
to live in a nice house, and then throwing his
portmanteau into the street because a greater man
is coming.

An insanely-audacious puff par. pops up in
Melb. Table Talk, to the effect that Isaac Isaacs,
who went to London to put a Melb. Tramway Co.
case before the Privy Council, has been offered
the High Commissionership of the Transvaal or
Orange River Colony with “a peerage and £6OOO a
year.” Neither province is likely to want a High
Commissioner for a good while, anyhow; and
lawyer Isaacs is not the sort of distinguished per-
son who will get the job when the bungling majah-
generals have finished “ subjugating the country.”
But the Table Talk yarn doesn’t stop at £6OOO a
year and a peerage for Ikey Ike. It goes on to
state that the prospective Countess Isaacs “had a
personal interview with the Queen, the purpose of
which was to convey the Royal request to her
husband.” The Bulletin looks forward to an
epidemic of similar paragraphs concerning other
Melbourne men and their wives. For instance,
why shouldn’t the Queen cable for Lady Turner
to come along and see her on the matter of a
dookdom for poor old George ?

The Brisbane clergy are indulging in some
pretty little recriminations on the Chinese mis-
sionary question. The Protestant parsons allege
that the ill-feeling shown towards their brethren
in China is due to the fact that R.C. priests in
that country are awarded the status of mandarins.
On the other hand, it is asserted that the same
position has been conferred by the authorities on
Protestant missionaries. It is alleged, further,
that much of the ill-feeling above-mentioned is
due to the usurpation by certain of the Christian
clergy in Chowland of civil (as well as spiritual)
authority—as one of their mandarinic perquisites.
Anyway, this phase of the question is interesting
* » * *
Ex-priest Slattery, who lives by saying
nasty things about one particular brand of religion,
had a disturbance at his “place of worship” in
Melbourne the other Sunday, and when two of
the disturbers were brought before Panton, P.M.,
that thoughtless moralist said: —“Speaking for
myself, I think it is much to be regretted that
any fanatic should come amongst us in this way
and provoke discussion, and cause insult to any-
one’s religion.” Which was an utterly unchristian-
like speech, although it sounded very nice and
proper.

“ Holy Roman ” to The Bulletin :
You must read all the papers to get all the news. From
the Protestant Banner’s report of the big Sydney Orange
gathering of 12th July it appears that one clergyman
publicly impugned the virtue of the nuns byremarking
that “ the less we say about their vows of chastity the
better.” (Cheers.) Another cleric said : “ Sisters of the
Poor ! Always the same name—' Sisters of the Poor.’
God help the poor, I say.” Well, the Little Sisters do help
the poor-as witness the long-ago Argus articles of the
Protestant-born Julian Thomas (“The Vagabond”). “The
Vag.,” unlike the genial shepherds above-quoted (from
the report of their own especial organ), thought that a
refined lady might possibly be actuated by some decent
and utterly unworldly motive if she gave up her life of
ease and even iuxury to go round begging food for the
aged destitute whom she had taken under her care. Let
us gaze with admiration on that big meeting cheering
God, and wonder what He thinks of the splendid asylums,
homes, hospitals and orphanages which haven't been
built and endowed by the aid of the manly clerics who
sneer at and impute unchastity to the Sisters. The Little
Sisters don’t make any money personally by their begging.
When they die they leave nothing behind them but their
clothes, and from day to day they share with their desti-
tute charges the broken victuals collected from the
charitable.

The N.S.W. Railway Commissioners re-
cently secured a conviction against a passenger
from Goulburn to Sydney, for alighting at Moss
Vale, buying another ticket from Moss Yale to
Sydney at tourist rates, and journeying to Sydney
on it in the same train This transaction saved
the traveller a few shillings, and the Commis-
sioners were forced to haul-up the offender, for if
the practice became common they would be com-
pelled to discontinue the cheap trains to the
popular tourist resorts Since this conviction the

THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian srems. *

Mrs. Isabella Downey, ciiarged with a
fatal act of abortion on 3rd Feb., has been finally
acquitted after standing four trials at Melb. Grim.
Court. The first trial went against Mrs. D. (who
seemed to have the sympathy of those who knew
the circumstances of the case) but the verdict was
upset on appeal. The second and third trials
ended iu jury disagreements, one or two of. the
twelye holding out for an acquittal each time.
The fourth jury seem to have thought that the
unlucky woman was sufficiently punished, any-
how, and they found a unanimous verdict of not
guilty.

A Christchurch (M.L.) one-armed lady has
just passed her ambulance first-aid exam. She
bandages with her hand (left), and ties knots with
hand and mouth.
Ex-N.S.W. Education-Minister Hogue,
who loses no opportunity of pouncing upon the
present incumbent of the office, thought he had a
noble chance when Minister Perry averred that,
to his mind, “there was just as much danger in
sending unmarried male teachers into lonely
localities, where there were a number of girls
attending the schools, as there was
in sending female teachers there.”
But Perry is even righter than he
thought. Not only would there
be danger, in the case of a
young male teacher, to a school-
full of damsels in a lonely place,
but there would, in the circum-
stances, be to the said male-
teacher a much-greater danger
of hysterical accusations. For
every man who will unlawfully
tamper with a woman, the experi-
ence of the courts teaches that
there is at least a woman or two
who will falsely swear that they have
been so tampered with.

“Outlaw”: Passing a certain
Melbourne suburban church, the other
Sunday morning, I noticed the sedate-
looking elderly churchwarden sitting
in the porch reading intently—not
his Bible—but his Bulletin ! And in-
side they were singing, “ Oh, sinner, cease
from straying.”
The Brisbane Chinese are in a state of
huge alarm over the troubles in their own
country. Last week they held a meeting (under
clerical auspices) whereat a strange medley of
sacred and secular performances eventuated to the
amusement of the profane outsider. A queer howl
in the Chinese vernacular was passed off as one of
Sankey’s hymns, and several other quaint howls
were accepted as Celestial renderings of well-known
“Clistian ” anthems. Finally the Chows essayed
“ God Save the Queen,” but broke down badly
until picked up by European spectators who took
pity on Her Majesty. The gem of the evening
was a Chinese orator who staggered the commun-
ity by asserting : “ Boxer he not leal Chinaman ;
he allee same Labor Party this countlee ! ” The Q.
Labor Party doesn’t quite know whether to take
this as a compliment or otherwise.
♦ * * *
The discredited Callan Park (Sydney)
Asylum (women’s branch) for the Insane will not
be restored to public confidence by attaching to
the Board of Visitors two hon. lady-members.
The ladies in whose perspicacity and judgment
the public are prepared to trust are such women
as the present factory-inspectors—women who
have devoted years to the business, and possess
eyes and noses of professional alertness. And,
again, before the public will trust even these, it
must know they are fairly paid by the State for
their work and the responsibilities it entails.

“ Melb.” : Gratifying to
note that prison reform is coming
on the tapis. Within the past
month or so, lectures on the
subject have been given here
by Pastor Halley, M'Kenzio,
M.L.A., and Cook, M.L.A. Mr.
Halley spoke of what he saw last
year, in the American Reforma-
tory Prison, at Concord. Won-
derful results in making useless
and expensive criminals into
workmen, able to earn their own
living ; 70 per cent, turn out well.
The pastor addressed 800 of
these fellows in Concord prison
(is any outsider ever allowed to
address convicts in an Australian
prison?) M’Kenzie, M.L.A., was
equally on the nail, describing what
is done at Elmira, U.S. Cook,
M.L.A., who is member for Pen-
tridge district, and is intimately
acquainted with the prison and
prisoners, showed the scope that is
open there for this Concord and
Elmira business.
* * *
Dr. Maloney has before Vic.
Parliament a Bill on the S.A. lines
to enable a prospective mother to
claim confinement expenses, up to
£lO, from the father of her unborn
child. The third reading is due on
12th Sept. The chief objection is
that, in some cases, the wrong
prospective father may have to pay.
On the other hand, if the law waits
till it finds a way of making abso-
lutely sure that it has got at the
right prospective father it will
wait for ever, and the infant die 3
meanwhile. Besides, the Austra-
lian is so addicted to being a pros-
pective father that the law,
though it may occasionally go
wrong in details, can’t go tar
wrong in the lump. Probably
nine out of every ten fathers
who are made to pay for
the wrong infant will also be
fathers who have dodged paying
for the right one.
* * *
Out of 300,000 children of
school age in Victoria, 12,000 are
growing up without instruction, despite the com-
pulsory clauses of the Education Act. In the
suburb of Hawthorn alone 800 children fail to
comply with the Act.
* * * *
Thus His Excellency the Lieut. -Gov. of
Victoria at the annual meeting of the Humane
Society: —
Mr. William Harries, owner of a brickyard, seeing- that
a stack of 18,000 bricks was about to fall on a lad of 16,
covered the lad with his own body, and received the full
force of the bricks. He was terribly injured, but sur-
vived . . . probably because God, seeing- an act so
like His own, was determined that the man should live.
Which is an observation that somehow doesn’t
seem to fit in. The reward, one might think,
would be an entrance through the jasper gates and
a free pass to Paradise. But His Waxcellency’s
idea is that God rewarded the hero in a more
material fashion and let him live on in enjoyment
of a hero’s reputation and the possible nervous
breakdown that followed upon the deed that
earned it. His Waxcellency’s conclusion as to the
motives of Providence are hardly in accord with
the calm, cold, dispassionate reasoning that may
be supposed to animate his legal decisions.
» * * *
Waxellency Madden talking about juvenile
morality to the Carlton (Melb.) Refuge subscribers,
thought it necessary to mention several times that
“ the nature of the subject made it a'diffieult one
to discuss.” Which sounded rather absurd, con-
sidering that His Ex. had taken the whole of his
family to a performance of “Hedda Gabler” a
few days previously, and didn’t seem to shudder
when the Gabler woman owned up to a natural
appetite for forbidden fruit. Anyhow, the subject
which his Waxmodesty found so difficult to discuss
seemed to come easy enough to Miss Jane Suther-
land next day. That honest outspoken spinster,
being interviewed by the Akgus, gave it as her
opinion that dancing saloons provide a lot of
candidates for refuges. At the saloon, said
Miss S., “a girl’s natural instinct to attract young
men gets full play. . . Then one night she walks
home with some young fellow through the gardens
—and finally she” comes to me.” What a refresh-
ing difference between the lady’s plain speaking
and the gentleman’s half-veiled hints.

About the best soprano singer in Mel-
bourne at present is to be married to a musical
young man of means, the “ means ” meaning,
among other things, that he drew La Carabine in
a Tatt.’s sweep. In how many cases does the five
or ten “ bob ” investment in a sweep ticket repre-
sent an entrance fee for the matrimonial stakes ?
And after all, the best thing a man can buy with
money is a nice girl.
* * * *
Ex-R.E. to The Bulletin :
Col. Alphabet Smith, who took that expensive folly, the
N.S.W. “A ” Battery to S’Africa, has made a doleful
official complaint of the hard luck that has prevented his
command seeing service.” It seems his guns and
men are scattered about in various out-of-
the-way places on the Orange River. The
reason he does not state, but it isn’t hard to
guess. The battery is not in a state to be trusted in
action, and for that Col. Smith himself must surely be
at least a little to blame. He has been paid an extrava-
gant salary, as an expert artilleryman, to train the pro-
vince’s gunners. True, he has only had some 30 horses,
apd about 80 men, instead of the 131 horses and 166 men
he should have had to make the battery complete ; and in
Sydney neither horses nor men over got any prac-
tice in their proper business of manoeuvring the guns.
A field-battery is a machine, and, like other machines, it
won’t work unless complete in all its parts ; nor can the
men learn the working of the complete machine by prac-
tice with the incomplete. Also, the best of the few
drivers and horses the Battery had were taken for ambu-
lance work ; and when the Battery was to be sent, they
had to rake in 90 or 100 raw recruits, and over 100 un-
broken (for the purpose) horses. And General French and
Colonel Smith—both real gunners—must have well known
that that was much the same as manning a ship with
land-lubbers. But the authorities, under pressure from
the socially influential “ barracks-push ” had made up
their minds that the Battery should go. And it went,
and there it is.

It has usually been the custom in the past
on the northern rivers for the Colonial Sugar Co.
to cut the cane-crops of the growers with their-
own gangs. An innovation was made this season
at the Richmond by the local manager, in allow-
POOR JOHN.
SUM UP: “Me go China fightee—per’aps get
killed. Mestay here, George-stleet, in Sydney.
No Jightum, all a same get killed ’lectio tlam;
you sabbee.”
ing growers to cut their own crops, and thereby
make an extra bit of “sugar.” No sooner was
the concession granted, however, than several
“mean whites” began making contracts with
Hindoos for cutting. The matter was brought
under the notice of Gen. Manager Knox, who
promptly replied that the concession would only
be granted on the stipulation that Hindoos were
not employed in harvesting the crops, and that
“ colored labor must be discouraged by the co. in
N.S.W.” The “mean whites” have not the
excuse this year of being unable to obtain white
labor, either, as at all the co.’s mills there has been
a far larger number of men offering than were
required.
* * * *
A case of plague having occurred near the
residence of a N.S.W. Govt, official, said official
was suspended during the illness of the patient
(who was too low to be removed to quarantine).
And when the official returned to duty he found
that his suspension-time was to be counted as his
annual holiday leave of absence !

Sydney E. News correspondent, in an
. account of an interview with some of the Mawbey
family, re the recent Gilgandra atrocity, states
that the youth Reginald Mawbey, “stuttered a
great deal owing to an impediment of speech.”
Useful to know this ; otherwise people might have
imagined that the stuttering arose from an in-
growing toe-nail!

at least shall not be taken for a Boxer sympa-

pli’ my Austlalian ?—hail (hair) cut snolt! Me
Clistian ! —dlinkee lum (rum) like hell! ” Good
many white Clistians like that.

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, July 31, 1900.
My Dear Myee.—
I was assured, the other day, that never a group
of Sydney men foregather at lunch, or for any
cause, but someone turns the conversation on His
Ex. From what I can hear, the process seems to
be somewhat similar to turning on an X-ray.
“ What do they say of him ? ” If they are society
men, they reckon up the cost of V.R. dinner-
parties. If they are pure commercials, they esti-
mate the value of a Gov. who draws crowds, and
decimate the consequence of a Gov. who thaws
them. If they are sheer snobs, they describe their
feelin’s at meetin’ Jones, the fruiterer, up there in
the V.R. halls. If they are golfers, they groan
for a Gov. who will elevate what they are pleased
to call good sport. If they are married, they tell
how their wives will never be seen at Gov. House
again. If they are single, they describe how their
invitations were for a Mr. and Mrs. Blank.
Sometimes, a widower joins in, and says his card
was made out for himself and the wife he buried
10 years ago. And sometimes a newly-married
man tells how he was invited en ctlibataire, and
his wife has never been quite the same since.
And yet, all the time, everybody is perfectly
well aware that never, never, never have we had
a Gov. who has Tried so Hard to be a good Gov.
all round, to please everybody—nobodies and
somebodies—from Potts Point to Waterloo, in-
clusive. Those in favor of the motion say aye !
The ayes have it.
According to His Ex’s, own self-supporting
statement, there has only been one perfectly
genuine description of his parties in a Sydney
paper. Other writers have imagined, invented,
spared the couleur de rose, laid on the inky black,
touched up, toned off, made simple, pretty,
unreliable, nasty, unveracious “copy” of the
Govt. -House entertainings. Done everything, in
fact, but paint them as they were meant to seem.
Strangely enough, the only one is a Poet.
His true story covered a lot of space in an
evening paper. I think I may safely say not one
detail was missing. He even dwelt on the white
arms stretching over the supper-table for cham-
pagne. And His Ex. liked the picture awfully.
The literary touch, perhaps, appealed to him.
Anyway, he ordered 250 copies, and “ An Evening
at Government House,” by Exquisite Amber, will
soon be fluttering in selected English circles.
The nick-name of . a prima donna and star so-
prano, is—behind the scenes —“ Come-now-no-
nonsense.” And doesn’t she look it? A funny
man convulsed the company the other night by
suddenly trilling, “Gome, now, no nonsense,” as
the soprano went to embrace him.
My second visit to the “Rose of Persia” was
at the Matinee for the Children’s Hospital last
Friday. Entering the d.c. the scent of lilies and
green things proclaimed that the Gov. was there.
Does that sound queer? I mean, that the V.R.
box was decorated out and in with flowers and red
and white ribbons, the work of Miss Birmingham
and Mrs. Aronson. Lady Mary was in all black,
Lady Bertha in brown, with shell-pink vest. A
smart circle filled the house with feathers and
furs. The Matinee hat—the nice, comfortable,
much-plumed black straw that shuts the stage off
so effectively from six people at a time, was every-
where. One weary man asked me : “Will these
big hats be in fashion long?” “Till the end of
next summer,” I answered. “Then this is my
last Matinee,” said he, “till the end of next
summer.”
Mr. Dowling, of Sydney Mail, and Mr. J. C.
Williamson, speechified in the interval. “You
are letting Melbourne get ahead of you,” said
Mr. Williamson, looking round the house. He
described the enthusiasm with which the whole
company volunteered to play-act for the “kid-
dies.” And perhaps it was a little disappointing
to find that where Melbourne had gone over the
thou. Sydney had only risen to the height of
£2OO.
All my first impressions of the opera were
deepened. I liked the music twice as well, and
the dresses twice as well, and the ballet, and the
magnificent grouping. Also, I liked the Rose as
little, except in madrigal and florid places, where
her high and flexible voice is often very fetching.
A wild controversy rages over the ballet. “ Are
the colours harmony, or just sheer daring ? ” The
ballet frocks are in four shades—sapphire blue,
vivid yellow, dead white, and the loveliest,
richest, warmest green imaginable. Against these
filmy spangled tints dances Tillie Woodlock in
bright pink. That is just a little bit impossible.
“ Dancing Sunbeam ’’—otherwise Dolly Vane-
on her wedding: “I am going to be married
sometime in August. Very quietly, you know-
just a tailor-made. The wedding will be in Mel-
bourne. I leave the stage in a fortnight. No, I
am not waiting till the “ Rose ” is done blooming.
There was a lovely part offering in ‘Florodora,’
too. ‘Won’t you wait?’ 3aid Mr. Williamson.
But no, it didn’t tempt me. lam tired and run
down. Hard work, opera in Australia, although
I love it, mind you. When I leave the R.O.P. I
am going away to Coogee, to rest quietly by
the sea for a fortnight. Then I shall settle
down in Melbourne, but not at Menzies’, I think.
In a house of our own, more probably. Mdme.
Beattie is making me such pretty frocks. I
don’t rave over clothes, never did. Still one must
have them, I suppose, when one is going to be—er
—married. lam looking forward to a rest. Now,
I am forever pinching my cheeks because people
say I am so white, and really I have been suffer-
ing frightfully from nervous prostration lately.
My engagement isn’t altogether brand-new. It is
several months old, in fact, but I have kept it
quiet until now, and now everybody seems to
and the invent— ” And certainly she
looked as little as possible like a convent with her
big, fair, fluffy fringe, and a long, trailing, blue-grey
frock, edged with white, worldly-looking fur.
Speaking of furs, this winter has been quite
Tema.rka.ble as a Fur Season, The warehouses
bave sold all their stocks, and there isn’t a woman
in . Sydney, probably, who hasn’t a bit of some
amma l’s skin when occasion demands (or doesn’t
demand). Catting has grown into a regular
in dustry. Your imagination can supply the pain-
I l . l * details. I am too much of a cat myself to
dilate on the horrid subject.
“ All women,” says Pinero, “look well in furs.”
Helen Mathers—who came Thro’ the Rye with
red poppies in her hair many a year ago—says
some clever things about furs in her new novel,
“Becky.” According to Helen, fair women
should never wear ermine. Black hair, dark eyes,
and damask cheeks alone shine out above a dead
white setting. She implores women with grey
eyes, or blue, to stick hard to chinchilla,
The new fashion of doing-up the hair right on
top seems a half-hearted revival of that other
fashion by which every one wore her locks twisted
on her forehead as if she wanted to butt some-
body. This latter is the chronic condition of
woman anyhow. In reality it is a complete re-
vival of the style in which the axeman did up an
about-to-be-executed mediaeval lady’s hair when
he wanted to keep it clear of his chopper. The
little knob was formerly necessary as a handle to
enable the executioner to hold up the severed end
of his customer for public inspection after death.
Also it came in handy before death ; he took hold
°I *t i n order to adjust the lady’s head in the right
position on the block. Now the old fashion is
revived—and, curiously enough, also for exhibi-
tion on the Block.
I hope you noticed how the S.M.H. announced
that “our war-correspondent, Donald Mac-
donald,” was the guest of the Proprietary Fair-
faxes! This is getting in ahead of William
Beauchamp.

Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“ Mr. Deakin looks a little worn after his Feder-
ation labors, but seems otherwise unaltered, even
as to his spiritualistic leanings. His wife, sister,
and three little girls have all returned with him to
dear old Australia, and are gladly welcomed by
co-believers, especially the first-mentioned lady,
who still bears ner mediumistic honors thick upon
her. One can understand that an educated medium
must be regarded as a pearl of great price by mem-
bers of her own cult. These human half-way
houses, so to speak, are generally such ungram-
matical creatures that it really looks like cruelty
to spirits (granting the possibility) to conjure up
naice souls into such crude go-betweens. It’s not
an uncommon spectacle to see a refined, cultured
mourner trying to reach the ‘dull, cold ear of
death ’by the help of a medium. Very often, the
enquirer is a dear old lady, bereft of a presumably
charming daughter. In preliminary confabs,
the sibyl has gleaned family names, &c., from the
wistful questioner, and has her lesson pat enough
in her vulgar way. When in due course she feels
herself * inhabited ’ by the young lady’s spirit, she
assumes a giddy-girly air, and frisks as only a fat
frump can frisk to pumphandle the sobbing
mother. ‘ ’Ow are yer, mar ? ’ she giggles, ‘ and
’ow’s Hedith and Hethel and ’Erbert and ’Enery ?
I sy, mar, didn’t Hi ’ave a toffy funeral ’ Hi ham
’appy, mar. Par and granmar’s ’ere too. Par ’as
himproved. ’E’s not like the syme man! ’
Between times the medium trots out a racking
cough. ‘ The spirit passed away under those
conditions,’ whispers a believer, meaning that
deceased had ‘ a bad kauf which carried her off.’
What price Spirit-life, if it inherits all the ills of
flesh and parts with all its conveniences ?
“Chief-Justice Madden, who must talk about
something, has been following in the steps of the
tactful Willy Governor, lashing out re juvenile
immorality. His Honor is of opinion that the
sexes mingle too freely in youth, and that girls
should spend more time indoors. But, as a matter
of fact, it would appear that the more the sexes
mix the better the general morality. In America
girls and boys are mixed in the seats of all schools
promiscuously, and the ability of the Yankee girl
to look after herself is everywhere largely ascribed
to this fact. France, on the other hand, seems to
profit little by the fact that she makes her women-
kind prisoners, and that the Respectable French
girl never speaks to a man alone until she has
married him.
“Dr. ‘Charlie’ Molloy, late Supt. of Melb.
Hospital, is in the peculiar and unfortunate posi-
tion of being deeply affected by both the Boer
and Chinese campaigns. The relief of Mafeking
brought the news that his younger brother had
been killed by a shell during the latter days v of the
siege, while his only sister is now hundreds
of miles in the interior of China on a mission-
station.
“‘Reggie’ Bright, who lately rejoiced the
hearts of many dear old friends, male and female,
by recovering from severe pneumonia, has posed
as a gallant longer than any other man on the
‘Block.’ Over a quarter of a century ago debu-
tants took a flirtation with the handsome bachelor
as a hall-mark of success. A thousand times
captivated, Reggie still remains uncaptured in a
matrimonial way of speaking. With his hands
neatly folded under his coat-tails (not in prayer)
he takes his walks abroad, free as air.
“The Anti-Women’s-Suffrage movement in
Melb. should convert any wavering democrats to
the side of the suffragists. The Anti is the Fat
Woman in excelsis. Miss Reid and Miss Derham,
two very nice, well-dressed, pretty society girls,
are nominally bossing the show, but it only has to
be remembered that they are respectively related
to a big soft-goods firm and a big biscuit factory,
to see the real trend of the movement. Just now
it is considered quite the swagger thing to belong
to the anti-suffragists, and for a woman to leave
her name off the protesting petition means social
ostracism equal to having taken-in washing in
past years.
“A mild sensation has been caused in Mel-
bourne feminine and political circles by the
appearance of Colonel Goldstein at the head of an
anti-Woman Suffrage campaign. The Kernel’s
pretty daughter, Miss Vida Goldstein, is hon.
organising secretary of the pro-Suffragist body, so
that the gallant officer may be supposed to have a
happy time of it. The Kernel—perhaps un-
deservedly—bears the repute of being rather a
quarrelsome man, and has enjoyed the distinction
of having a special Parliamentary Committee en-
quiring into his conduct in relation to a Village
Settlement he has been officially running. The
anti-Suffragists think .him rather an acquisition.
They have not had him long enough for him to
quarrel with them, as yet.
“A golden-haired lady of sociable aspect is
trying, to form a club in, Melbourne for women
with time to spare and an inclination to be cosy.
One fears it will be hard to make an affair like
this jolly and sociable. Women mostly join
clubs for advancement, not for home comforts.
However, the golden-haired lady means business.
“Rink skating is fast becoming the rage again.
One long-abandoned rink has now the patronage
of five, different clubs, whose membership totals
750, with whom the proprietor contracts for three
months’ admission at 1J guineas per member.
“ In the ‘ Tyranny of Tears ’ Grace Noble, as a
pretty little nagging wife, looks almost dainty
enough to account for hubby’s long submission to
boo-hooism. That primrose afternoon gown is a
trim affair with straps of its own material en-
circling its long,, close-fitting sleeves, chic bodice
and clinging skirt, and no other trimming but
transparent jack-tar vest. With a delightful
dinner-dress of palest grey, the lissome despot
sports corsage-cluster of dark-red roses, while her
third frock —of summery maize-color—shows wavy
lines of tiny heliotrope ribbon.
“No excuse for the faults of a girl with such
clothes ! ‘ The consciousness of being well-dressed
is worth all the consolations of religion ! ’ Any-
way, Mrs. Brough, as a poor parson’s daughter,
should be judged more leniently, even though her
sins had been as scarlet (which they weren’t).
That kiss wasted on a married employer’s portrait
seems a convincing circumstance. An apostle’s
ewe lamb, as a rule, has quite the average share
of sentimentality to twine round the nearest peg
available. Failing a curate or a bank clerk, she’s
been known to make eyes at a Chinese convert
and cherish in her locket a tuft shorn from the
poll of a colored evangelist.
“ A sign that Nance O’Neil really does cry on
the stage : observe that after every cry-scene she
has to turn her back to the audience and blow
her nose. When women cry they always blow
their noses after it. A shrewd actress who could
not master the trick of shedding tears at will
might help the illusion by using her handkerchief
to her nose after the scene.
“ People who only know George Lauri as a low
comedian don’t know how well lie sings a serious
baritone song. Writer heard him not long
ago at a private shivoo, and heard Brownlow on
the same occasion. Brownlow is, of course, the
truer artist, but Lauri is miles ahead of many
baritones one has heard ; only if he booked dates
as a serious baritone people would insist on laugh-
ing at him. Lauri would be expected to be funny
if lie sang ‘ The Death of Nelson ’; and, for the
matter of that, so he might be !
“The abject littleness of the ‘great’ dailies
was never better exemplified than in the Age’s
report of the Melb. Hospital meeting, at which
the Argus’s cheque for £17,500 was received.
David Syme reported that a collection of this
sum had been made by ‘ a fund started by a Mr.
Iliffe, a well-known resident of the N.E. district.’
The sting of the Argus collection lies in the fact
that the Age tried to promote a rival one and
secured only one donation of £5OO.
Shanghai Villa, a garden-girt domicile of St.
Kilda-road, has been the scene of many happen-
ings. It was built by Mr. G. B. W. Lewis before
the banks lost him all his money. The Welch
family (the drapery Welches) took it, and the
head of the house died there. Next, the Everard
Brownes moved in, and there, with much sawdust-
laying of roads and muffling of knockers, baby
Browne was born. Then the W. R. Wilsons took
it for a term, and poor W. R. gave up the ghost.
Lastly, Mr. and Mrs. Ernest M'Caughan moved
in with all their bridal raiment and brand-new
suites, and there Mrs. M‘Caughan died —a bride
of two months only when she underwent an
operation which resulted in her death. Mrs.
M'Caughan was one of several daughters Of Chief
Secretary Collins of Tasmania. The marriage was
celebrated very quietly at the R.C. Church,
although the bride was of the Anglican denomina-
tion. They came over immediately from the nup-
tials, and finished their honeymoon tour in Melb.
gaieties. A tall, fair-haired, very pretty girl with
a taste in dressing and an air of coquetry in her
manner, many Melb. people had taken Mr. Pat
M'Caughan’s daughter-in-law to their hearts, and
are genuinely sorrowful over her death.
“ Some time back a certain young hopeful of a
much-gilded family bought from the widow of an
Australian station-holder an up-country mansion.
When the bargain was concluded, the fair seller
declared that it would break her heart to part
with a pair of marble dogs perched on pedestals at
the gates, and pleaded that they should be left in
her keeping. But young What’s-his : name stuck
out for his pound of flesh, on the just plea that he
had paid for the property, lock, stock and barrel,
and wanted the goods delivered intact. The
widow wept and argued without avail. Then a
friend suggested that she should appeal to Shy-
lock’s pa, “a dear old soul,” who could put a
finger in the pie if so inclined, on the strength of
having supplied the purchase-money. The lady
took the hint, bathed the Ancient’s feet with tears,
so to speak, and in return was anointed with
figurative butter and honey. Her hearer, ap-
parently bubbling over with sympathy, though
there was a cute twinkle in his eye all the time,
promptly volunteered to give her a letter to the
obdurate “Gussie.” She went off triumphantly
with the promised epistle, but, though the recipient
read it, he firmly and finally refused her request.
When the disappointing interview was over, the
tearful widow picked up a scrap of paper dropped
by the enemy. It was pa’s letter, and read:
“ Stick to the bow-wows, Gussie, my boy ! ”
A ours affectionately,
GOULI-GOULL.
Dr. Frank Tidswell, assistant medical-
officer of N.S.W. Board of Health, whose portrait
is printed in this issue, had a distinguished career
at Sydney University, graduating in 1892 with
the degrees of M.B. and Ch.M. After service at
the Alfred Hospital as resident medico he visited
Europe, devoted himself to a course of study con-
nected with public hygiene, and obtained the
prized Diploma of Public Health of Cambridge.
Returning to Sydney, he was associated with the
Children’s Hospital, Glebe, where he applied him-
self to the modern treatment of diphtheria. He
subsequently became an official of the Board of
Health, where his skill and zeal as a bacteriologist
in connection with the plague epidemic has ob-
tained for him a wide scientific reputation. It is
proposed to send this clever young medico to
India to study the plague under indigenous con-
ditions. .
William the Unspeakable’s latest break-
out. Said he to the German officers proceeding
to China: —
Your mission is to avenge the death of the Ambassadors
and the Europeans. When you close with the enemy,
remember that no quarter must be given and no prisoners
taken. Use your weapons so that for 1000 years to come
no Chinaman will dare look at a German askance. Pre-
pare the way for Christian civilisation ! Good-bye, com-
rades !
This talk about furthering Christian civilisation
by giving the Chows no quarter recalls his saintly
grandfather’s mixture of slaughter and snuffle,
embalmed in the lines :—
William’s words, which, it is now cabled, “were
not intended for publication,” have “caused an
immense sensation in Europe.”
“ I write in haste, my dear Augusta ;
We’ve had another awful buster.
Ten thousand Frenchmen sent below—
Praise God from whom all blessings flow ! ”
