PERSONAL ITEMS.
A Sydneyite remarks that he always de-
tested the editorial “We” until he read John
Norton’s “I’s.”
The nonagenarian Dean Cowper spends a
couple of hours daily compiling reminiscences of
Sydney. His memory is said to be clear back to his
fifth year.
There seems to be some trouble about
getting a Commander-in-Cliief for the allied forces
in China, owing to international jealousies. Why
not give Cronje a show ?
Count Rossi, who again figures in the law
lists in connection with that interminable ecclesias-
tical squabble, is now in his 77th year. His father
was appointed Superintendent of N.S.W. Police
by Earl Bathurst, and held the post till 1834.
John Bede Dailey, the son of Australia’s
W. B. Dailey who most resembles his father, was
at the end of June last called to the English Bar.
He studied at the Inns of Court, London. Forty-
four years ago, W. B. Dailey was called to N.S.W.
Bar.
Lord Howe Island, to which Frank
Farnell has been appointed visiting magistrate,
possesses peculiar charms for most has-been poli-
ticians. Nearly every flattened-out N.S.W. ex-
M.L.A. asks the Govt, for something to do at
Lord Howe.
Barrister “Dick” (Richmond Septimus)
Haynes, now so often mentioned in Westralia, is
a brother of John H. The Westralian man was
the first lawyer employed in Australia by mur-
derer Deeming, when the woman-slayer was
arrested at Southern Cross.
Rev. Dr. Zilluian, who left Australia a few
months ago for America to help in the presidential
campaign of W. J. Bryan, is returning to Sydney
in ill-health. Dr. Zillman carried greetings from
the little Silver League of Sydney to the gigantic
mother league of the States.
Stated that one of the members of the
S.A. first contingent has got into serious military
disgrace, and that his lather has been hastily
summoned to S’Africa to look after him. Names
are given in private whispers but it is not safe to
quote them openly yet awnile.
Robert Lowe (Lord Sherbrooke) sat as
chairman of the select committee / which half-a-
century ago demanded the abolition of Sydney
municipal council. Lowe described the council as
“a hotbed of villainy.” James Martin and
Charles Cowper also signed the demand.
“This gentleman’s name must also be
struck off the rolls,” said Chief Justice Darley in
a very bad, dishonest-solicitor case the other day.
Two minutes later, His Honor referred to him as
“that man.” The solicitor, you see, was a
“gentleman by Act of Parliament,” till disrolled.
Very old man Groom rose the other day
lo announce that he had been elected leader of the
Independent faction in Queensland Parliament.
Groom’s career can be put in a sentence : He has
represented Toowoomba for 38 years, and by
patient and useless continuance in one place he
holds the Australian record.

Rumor thickens (and so does denial) that
John Forrest will, on Westralia entering the
Federation, take the position of local Governor.
John is, and has reason to be, tired of Westralian
politics, and his literary half is still more a-weary,
so that a haven of mellow sunlight would be agree-
able. Forrest at one time prided himself much on
his ancestry, and in several books he has had it
chronicled that he is of ancient French extraction,
with the surname of Foret.
Ex-Crown Prosecutor C. A. Smyth (Vic.),
who lately crossed swords with Pastor Rentoul on
Gladstone’s politics and other things, was imported
from London as a gallery-hand by Melb. Argus
in ’6O. He was a good shorthand writer, but
always destroyed his notes immediately after
transcription. He said it simplified matters in
case of tne accuracy of the report being questioned.
Attorney-General B. R. Wise’s published
denial of an intention to transfer himself to the
Victorian Bar is what most people expected.
From the day of his admission to N.S.W. Bar he
has been the white-headed boy of clients, solicitors
and Govt, after Govt. In his fifth year’s practice
his income passed £lOOO. No Australian Bar will
give him better times professionally than he now
enjoys.
Expected that Dr. Cockburn will resign

A little London weekly called New Ire-
land (naturally a very Hibernian journal) under
the heading, “ Irish Men and Women,” give a

Horace, the journal alleges that “He likes the

as ever.
W. H. Browne, the new leader of Q.
Labor-party, has been successively sailor, miner
and journalist, and has carried his swag from Hill
End, N.S.W., to Croydon, N.Q. He has repre-
sented the Croydon miners for 12 years, and is a
dead certainty for that constituency every time.
He is a well-read, even-tempered man, and as an
authority on mines and mining laws has no
superior in Q. Parliament. And no one calls him
“ Willie ” ; just plain “ Bill.”
Father Dunne, of Albury, who died last
week, aged 81, was the first R.C. clergyman to
officiate on Ballarat. Also, he was positive that
the genuine Roger Tichborne died in the early
50’s, in the Christian Brothers’ Hospital, Geelong ;
that he administered the last rites of the Church
to him, and read the burial service over his
remains in Geelong East cemetery. It was in
Father Dunne’s house that Peter Lalor sought
refuge after the defeat at Eureka Stockade.
Ex - Treasurer W. M‘Millan promised
Sydney Eastern Suburbs deputation the other
day that he will go for a representative seat in the
Commonwealth Parliament. M‘Arthur and Co.’s
business, of which the ex-Treasurer is managing
director, is growing by rapid strides, but the
effect, according to him, is that he will now be
able to give almost the whole of his time to
Commonwealth affairs. By the way, the firm has
purchased and will use as an annexe to its pre-
sent premises, the old York-street Opera House,
once the haunt of opera-bouffe, and later the scene
of the emotional triumphs of the elder Majeronis.

A new story of Robert Lowe
comes from Campbell Bannerman,
leader of the Liberal party in the
Commons: “I remember an old
friend of mine, who is better known
as ‘ Bob Lowe ’ than by any other
name, saying to me in the House of
Commons that whenever a man took
a sip of water it was always in the
middle of a sentence. If he took it at
the end of a sentence there was some
danger of the House thinking that he
had finished what he had to say.” Cunning
speakers in Australian Parliaments try to raise a
cheer and take a drink at that opportune moment.

When the late Prof. C. H. Pearson was
head-master of Melb. Presbyterian Ladies’ College,
he appointed the late Mrs. Fischer, afterwards
musical critic of S.M. Herald, to an important
position in the seminary. The lady had greatly
impressed him in an interview. But the appoint-
ment was far from pleasing to the managing
board, and it was awkward, as there was a signed
written agreement. The trouble that arose on
this account was one of the things which induced
Pearson to resign and go into politics.
“Socialist” to The Bulletin concerning:
a famous war-correspondent:—
Bennett Burleigh, of London Daily Telegraph, gets a
retainer of £lOOO a-3'ear in peace-time, and amuses him-
self by running for Parliament whenever an election is on
—he invariably gets defeated. He is a well-known
Bohemian, and once haunted a club called the “ United
Democratic Club,” Chancery-lane, London. Here all
sorts and conditions of male and female social revolu-
tionists assembled on Saturday nights to a “ house ”-
dinner (price Is.)—William Morris, in his soft blue shirt
and red necktie ; Keir Hardie, Ben Tillett, Tom Mann,
Ilyndman, Mrs. Besant and her handsome daughter,
Morrison Davidson, and various other socialists,
anarchists, journalists, and other discontented and down-
trodden classes. Every three weeks or so there was a
revolution in the management, and the U.D.C. raged
furiously and disunitedly until the new committee was
elected. These rows afforded Burleigh distraction
between wars, and, when the peace of Europe was assured,
you could nearly always depend upon a rovv in Chancery-
lane, with Bennett Burleigh not far off.
A conspicuous Melbourne musician and
several small literary and artistic familiars went
up into the mountains once for rest and change,
and to commune with nature, &la Thoreau. On
the second night a tremendous storm struck the
ridge, and it thundered and lightninged as if all
Gehenna had split. Then the conspicuous musi-
cian arose from’ his bunk, where he had been
prosing rapturously about Wagner, and strode
forth into the darkness and the racket of dismem-
bered nature, and when his friends followed they
found him standing out on a jutting rock, dressed
in nothing much, with a nimbus round his head,
and a stick in his hand, conducting the thunder-
storm !

An old Melb. Argus compositor, who set
type for London Morning Chronicle in ’5O, used
to tell a story of the late Chief Justice Higin-
botham (Vic.), when a reporter for that journal.
The young man was sent to Apsley House to
report the annual banquet. When the Waterloo
heroes got up, one after the other, to make a few
remarks in response to toasts, he was so entranced
by the spectacle that he was incapable of taking
a note, and his editor had to send to another news-
paper office for the necessary account.
Purves, Q.C., gob home neatly on a Vic
doctor during a recent abortion-case. The medico
had testified that a number of things might be, and
many others could have possibly taken place, but
he gave no direct answer to anything. Purves
studied his brief steadily for several seconds, then,
in a preoccupied tone, he said, “Well, Dr. Shields”
The medico fell into the trap, and at once
rapped out, “No, not Dr. Shields—Dr. —
whereupon Purves, raising his head in a surprised
way, said, “ I beg your pardon, but really from
your answers I was thinking that I was speaking
to my dear old friend Dr. Shields.” Shields is
Vic. Govt. Medical Officer, and is so exact-minded
and conscientious that in 30 years he has rarely
if ever been known to give a direct reply in cross-
examination.
A Bulletin correspondent gives some
news of W. C. Piguenit:—
Australia’s well-beloved painter, Piguenit, as was lately
here mentioned, is in England, and likely to stay there
for some time. On his return to Sydney the year before
last he painted two pictures of English scenery, from
sketches taken during his visit to the old country, and
these he sent to the well-known firm of Henry Graves
and Co., 6 Pall Mall. In due time he received a
cable making an offer for both pictures, with all rights,
which offer he accepted. Both pictures are now in the
hands of the etcher, and Mr. Piguenit has again visited
London, partly for the purpose of signing the artist’s-
proofs, and also to obtain further sketches and study
English landscape. Graves and Co. have since purchased
a third Piguenit picture (English landscape) which they
also intend to publish. One or two of the etchings will
be issued towards the close of the year. According to a
letter received in Sydney Mr. Piguenit was at latest a
guest of the famous English landscape-painter, B. W.
Leader, R.A., at his home, near Guildford, in Surrey,
situated in the midst of a beautiful landscape, in which
is to be found almost every tree that grows in England.
The Australian intended to leave for North Wales, the
scenery whereof is in places almost Tasmanian in appear-
ance.
“Heretic” sends The Bulletin this
obituary:
Died at Casino (N.S.W.) on 17th July, Abbe Schurr, the
oldest and best-loved missionary on the North Coast. He
was idolised by decent folk of all creeds and no creed.
Born in Alsace-Lorraine 73 years ago, he had a dis-
tinguished University career on the Continent, was
ordained a priest at 23, and went to the Gold Coast of
Africa as a missionary, lost his health, returned to Europe
and was appointed Professor of Languages at the Catholic
University of Dublin. He was a master of all the European
tongues, and learned Irish while in the Green Isle. He
abandoned his professorship to accompany the first R.C.
Bishop of Grafton and Armidale to Australasia ; was the
first missionary on Richmond River, and lived for years
amongst the early settlers and timber-getters, travelling
along the river in a boat, drifting generally, trusting to
tide or wind with a bush for a sail, and often sleeping
in the craft. He was gifted with an inexhaustible fund of
cheerfulness and humor, and his simple tastes and habits
gave no indication of his mental attainments. Ha was
remarkable for his love of tea, children, and music, and
was as popular with those of other religions or no religion
as with his own flock and with dumb animals. He
suffered from a chronic hole in the pocket, and could
never keep a shilling while he thought another was in
want. There was no better loved, more sympathetic,
self-denying, humble being in all the world than the
venerable, wise, and patient Abbe Schurr.
Bryan O’Loghlen, in a letter to Judge
Molesworth (whose chief qualification for his
position when appointed was that he was
the son of his father), supporting the Judge’s
anti - Marshall - Hall candidature for Melb.
University Council, incidentally remarks that
the Judge’s father was Victoria’s greatest
Judge. This opinion is field by all Vic. lawyers.
He was the last of that great line of Chancery
lawyers of whom Selden was a type. Besides, he
moulded in its infancy the crude but common-
sense and highly original mining legislation of the
early Victorian diggers into the finest body of
mining law in the world. His daring construc-
tiveness overleaped precedents with such success
that he compelled, as in his assertion of the
famous principle of Critchley v. Graham, a
frontage - claim forfeiture case, trembling but
admiring superior courts to follow his sacrilegious
lead. His judgments were rarely upset, either by
Full Court or Privy Council. On the Bench he
appeared to be fast asleep, and very seldom inter-
rupted counsel in their argument. He invariably
reserved judgment, delivering it a few days after-
wards. Written on small pieces of blue paper, in
a hand that suggested a fowl putting its legs in an
ink-pot and walking over the paper, his judgments
were most difficult to decipher; and the only
persons who could read them were Argus reporter
M‘Kinley and the Judge’s associate, O’Grady
Rose. By the way, what strange stories the
ghost of O’Grady could tell, per Witch of Endor,
of attempts by jobbers in mining stock and such
like to get a peep at old Moley’s judgments before
they were formally delivered !
Prof. Pearson's “Life” shows that he
despised Greek and Latin, but was fond of modern
languages. In his “ National Life and Character,”
he predicted that China is the coming Power. It
is a common mistake that his S.A. farming was a
failure. He got on well at it, when he had bought
experience, and only came to Melbourne because
of his wife’s illness. Quaint is his account of
inventing a hydraulic axe, apparently to be thrust
right through trees. He and a friend lost £2OO
over this mechanism. Comparing dates, it seems
that Melba was his pupil at the Presbyterian
College. The squatters fired him out at about the
same time when Prof. Morris, as Higinbotham’s
son-in-law, had to give up the C. of E. College.
The earnest Australian democrat provides three
tabernacles —one for Higinbotham, another for
Wilson Gray, and the third for Pearson.

Reporters working in Melb. Age office
always know when 11 o’clock arrives, for at' pre-
cisely that hour sub-editor Stephens emits a long,
low whistle, like the cry of a curlew. Stephens
consults no watch or clock, but he is never five
minutes out, and the mystery of his whistle has
agitated David Syme’s office since the days when
Alfred Deakin was a sufficiently enthusiastic
spiritualist to attribute it to supernatural influ-
ence.

than three ’bus-proprietors filed in bankruptcy on
June 20, in Sydney.
According to the last yearly report of the
N.S.W. Insp.-General of Insane, the proportion
of the insane to population was 1 in 321, and the
average weekly cost per patient was 11s. Id. It
is remarked as curious that “the wives of pro-
fessional men appear to be almost immune, only
21 being treated ” ; and, as regards causation, the
tender passion is only credited with three cases of
mental capsize. But 21 professional men’s wives
appears to be a high figure for one year out of
N.S.W. population.
How Hori Grey looms as a great man and
a great statesman since the African war began !
He was the great Imperialist—whose Imperialism
made for peace, whose policy was fair treatment
for native races, and friendship for neighbours.;;
Since the war the writers begin to see and to say
that had Grey’s policy on Federal lines been pur-
sued in S’Africa there would have been no neces-
sity for the war of to-day. He was continually
thwarted and snubbed by the Colonial Office of
his day, yet whenever they got into a mess they
had to send for him to help them out. Had Grey
been Governor of the Cape after, the gold and
diamond era, it is safe to say there would have been
no slavery in the Kaffir compounds, no alternate
disasters in Boer and native wars, and the Rhodes
gang of money-grabbing miscreants would have
been kept in their proper places. What there is~
of solid democracy—as embedded in the statute-
book—in N.S.W. to-day we owe to Grey, whose
passionate preaching of one-man-one-vote, in the
convention and out of it, made it impossible for
his rival, Parkes, who had resisted it up till then,
to do so any longer. And it was the great aristo-
cratic democrat who made it possible for Maori-
and to have a Ballance and a Seddon to give the
ountry old-age pensions.

WATSON’S WHISKY.
Aboriginalities.
In a little N.S.W. township, recently,
Mrs. Brown, on returning with her children from
a dance, heard a shuffling noise behind the kitchen
door. Hurrying into the next room, she got a
loaded revolver, went back, and stood on guard
whilst one of the children ran across the road for
Bob Smith. Bob turned up with a tremendous
waddy, three boys similarly armed, and one with
a meat-axe ; but after long conference as to the
best way to attack the desperado, Bob suggested
sending for the police. But the plucky Mrs.
Brown went in—revolver cocked—and challenged
the burglar to show himself or she would shoot.
With each challenge Mrs. Brown’s voice grew
shriller, and presently a crowd had gathered, and
Bob’s wife held him by the coat-tail (as if there
was any need to hold him back), saying, “ Don’t
go in, Bob; don’t go in—he’ll hurt you !” At
last the policeman arrived, and Bob and the boys
plucked up courage and guarded the doors and
windows, whilst the copper, in deep, commanding
tones, ordered the concealed ruffian out in the
Queen’s name. Then there was a rustle of feathers,
and a melancholy voice from behind the door
said “ Poor Cooky ! ”

Tin-kettling still flourishes and breeds
feuds way back. Not long ago, near Cargo,
N.S.W., a man was terribly injured through a
fall from his horse, while fleeing from an infuriated
bridegroom whom he had tin-kettled.

Truth stranger than fiction again. If an
author wrote a story "in which the hero beheaded
himself with an axe, the thing would be scoffed at
as impossible. Yet this feat was practically
accomplished by a Victorian bushman t’other day.
The man raised a, very sharp American axe high
over his head, the log he was cutting slipped from
under hjs foot, and the weapon falling on his neck,
nearly fevered it. He was dead in 3min.

“Milky White : On the road between
Deniliquin and Hay I recently fell in with a
foreigner wheeling a barrow containing two 561 b.
and several lesser weights. First thought—Rats !
but found he was a heavy-weight artist. Reach-
ing a wayside pub. he performed, tossing the
Jcwts. about as if they were of pasteboard, and
scoring a few shillings. Presently a discussion
arose between Big Jack and the foreigner, and in
proof of his ability to beat “ ennyvon under
golony,” the latter piled all his weights in his bar-
row, topped them up with two bags of flour,
crowned the lot with Big Jack himself, then
crawled under the barrow, lifted it bodily from
the ground, and then waltzed around for the sp ice
of 30sec. There was a dray standing near. Big
Jack caught up the barrow —weights, flour and all
—and lifted them gently into the body of the dray.
Then he got a couple of old waggon-wheels and
some scrap-iron, put them in, and looked
for more. There was nothing but the water-
trough, and he was just about to lift
that when the boss suggested that if more
weight were wanted he would sit on the dray.
Jack piled him on and looked for more. The
foreigner volunteered ; another volunteered —we
all volunteered, and Jack piled us all in. “ Yes ”
he said, “I think that’ll do”—mind there were
eight of us in that dray—“but,” he added, “just
wait a minute till I git that bar-cushion. I’ve got
a sore on my shoulder and I don’t want to hurt
it.” He went into the bar for the cushion but
returned with a kilderkin of beer, which he put in
the dray also. Then he disappeared again ; and,
just as we were wondering what had become of
him, a man on horseback rounded the corner and
burst out laughing. It was big Jack! —and lie
took the road with “ You look so nice up there
b’ys, I wouldn’ like to disturb yer. Gooa
evenin’ ! ”

Discovered on the wilds of the Kaituna
Estate, Little River, Canterbury (M.L.), an
ancient tombstone inscribed : •
Sacre 1 to the Memory ot
B. TOWNSEND,
Died a.d. 1774.
The stone, which was embedded more than a foot
in the ground, was of soft slate. Townsend may
have been one of Captain Cook’s crew, but Cook
visited the South Island in 1770. Anyhow, only a
white man could, in those days, have carved the
English inscription on the slab.

days, 3000 hares—and the/ nig lOd. a head.

A dairyman recently appeared before a
certain coraic p.M. i n these parts charged with
~.£ . . * £ ° ,1
not mixing a fair proportion of milk with the
water. Ihe T.M., as an old client of the accused,
was inclined to resent the charge as a personal
affront, and in a smiling, mere-matter-of-form
tone enquired, “Howdo you plead, Air. Chalky?”
“ Waahup,” replied Chalky without
any blushful hesitation. ihe P.M. s face stif-
fened. “What!” he cried, leaning over the
bench to shake an indignant forefinger at the
culprit’s wooden features. “You have the
effrontery to stand there and tell me to my face
you've been serving me with adulterated milk
all these years ? I fine you 40s. or seven days,
and think yourself lucky to escape so easily. ’
The unabashed Chalky stroked his chin thought-
fully. Then he said, “If your Washup will pay
me that £4 odd that’s been owing so long I’ll be
able to part up the 40s. fine.” The P.M. grunted,
but he paid the fine.
WEAT IT HAS COME TO.

“Narked ” : Can any of your readers tell

* • * *
Out in the Never Never, local authorities
get hit every time that they try to regulate the
camel-man. Failing to keep him in bounds other-
wise, Wilcannia Council set aside part of the com-
mon for him; but the first prosecution for disre-
garding these bounds has failed, and the bylaw
lias been brushed aside. Even in populous Broken
Hill camel-trains wander right into the town and
camp there.
* * * *
“A.P.R.” : Queensland to-day seems to
be our pest-purveyor-in-chief. The Noogoora
burr, first introduced amongst cotton-plants, is
50 per cent, worse than its Bathurst relative, and
could have been destroyed a few yearn ago by
Q. Govt, for about £5O. And now millions would
not exterminate it.

Dear Bulletin. —The incident (bucking
the saddle off) which “H.G.” mentions (8.,
14/7/’00) is not so very uncommon. “ Rolfe
Boldrewood,” in his “A Colonial Reformer,”
explains that “the horse sticks down his head
with his nose on the ground ; feet straight out
before him, so that hoofs and nose are almost
touching, legs and neck all in a line.” If the
saddle slips over his shoulder, it must go when he
withdraws his head. All the same, I called the
man who first told me about it a —well, “ roman-
cer.”— H.L.
• * •
“ Fairholm ” : Horse getting rid of sa,ddle
in manner described (“H.G.,” 8., 14/7/’00) is not
due to “animal shrinkage.” I have often seen,
‘ ‘ outlaws ” on stations get rid of saddles quite as
easily when the head was between the two fore
legs while bucking. Facility of “ going through
the girth ” depends upon the proportionate size
of a horse from wither to girth. If this is small
the saddle will be found to slip over the wither
and down the neck quite easily.
* « » *
Dear Bulletin : Be that girth slipping
business. I was riding a 13.2 pony a few weeks
since with breastplate, and she got rid of the
turn out —pack included —without a strap or
buckle parting. Apparent causes : low withers,
loose girth, and head well in between her legs
when operating.—X.

“ Snorter” : I have often seen rider and
saddle thrown by horse in bucking, and in few
cases have girth or girth-straps been broken or
buckles pulled out. Twice I have seen rider and
saddle thrown clean, latter with girth, surcingle,
and breastplate attached, and not a strap or
buckle broken. In one case I saw the bridle also
removed by the saddle in coming over the ho rse’s
head. By the action of bucking the saddle is
moved forward, and, once over the wither, a few
plunges clear the lot. “ H.G.” would know this
if he had ever ridden a horse that struck at the
bit while bucking.

“ Long ’Un ” : Surveying on the Murray
once returned to main depot after fortnight’s
flying-camp ; and found in the centre of a mirror
hanging from the tent-pole,' a swallow’s liest with
two eggs in it. On the same trip, driving through
scrub one morning, caught five dingo pups about
the size of a month-old kitten. Fastened saddle
“D” straps around their necks, and securely
tethered them to a sapling. Called for the “ bag ”
in the afternoon ; saw the mother skulk away into
the malice ; and found nothing left of the litter
but two well-chewed pups, and ; : idry fragments
of the rest.

“ S.S.” : Long ago, and more than once,
per medium of Thu Bulletin, I called upon Aus-
tralians tricky with the stockwhip to work up a
“turn’’out of familiar business, and take it to
the music-halls. Is Dan Hassett, the man lately
doing a whip turn with Rickards at Melb. Bijou,
the result of this tip ? The stockwhippist looks
like a genuine Australian touched up to suit stage
traditions, and he handles his flogger like a star,
but his tricks are not well chosen for spectacular
effect. Remember a performance given on “ The
Corner” at Ballarat that would have paralysed
the Bijou audience. The tricks were not as diffi-
cult as Hassett’s, but much more showy. Cutting
and carving is fetching business, although the
performer need not be as brilliant as the fabulous
puncher who would peel an apple with a ten-foot
lash.
* * * *
Writes a lady who has lived on settlements
where black labor was employed indoors and out :
It is hardly too much to say that no one, unless taken
absolutely by surprise, need be murdered by an Austra-
lian native. Have lived in Queensland where a family
could not exist without the use of our black brother, and
the members, children included, simply had to be taught
how to manage him. The best-treated aboriginal has the
slumbering instinct to kill : but if attached to you will
ofteu give warning when the fit is coming on. Our black
woman, Kitty, was passionately attached to little “ Miss
Maly,” but she often told her never to stand with her back
to any aboriginal ; even she herself when she siw “Miss
Maly’s ” back, always wanted to hit it vvith a nulla-nulla.
Jacky, her son, was in the family nine years, but he
always wanted the master to ride behind him, so that he
wouldn’t see his back and feel bad till he got something
into it. Always face a black, no matter how slight the
weapon may be —a stick, a rolling-pin, anything will do.
The black is half animal yet, and it is the moral effect of
resistance that intimidates. In the late affairs, the girls
who ran away were killed : the woman who stepped out
on the verandah and faced the assailants escaped.
But the black full-blood is one thing, the half-caste
quite another.
“ Pirate” : Re aboriginal fire-making (8..
7 and 14/7/’00). All the fire I’ve seen produced
by white men twirl in;/ a stick in any kind of wood
was perceptible only on the hands, or in the lan-
guage. New Ireland and Solomon Island natives
have a better trick. A groove is scored length-
wise in a piece of dry, soft wood (mangrove,
cottonwood, sycamore, &c.). This is placed be-
tween the feet, the operator being seated on the
f round, and a pointed stick of dry, hard wood,
eld in both hands, is pressed rapidly to and fro
in the groove. The dust produced by friction
accumulates at the far end, chars in a short time,
and finally ignites. Dry leaves, rubbed fine, a
little blowing, some twigs—and there’s your fire.
Time, about 3min. This method enables more
weight to be applied for friction.

“ Uloola ” writes :
Re item (B. 7/7/’00) anent the destruction of aboriginal*
by the use of poisoned flour. My mother relates that,
when blacks were plentiful at Kurnell and what is now
the Holt-Sutherland Estate (between Port Hacking and
Botany Bay), the kindly colonist found a neat way of
eliminating his murky brother by grinding-up glas3, mix-
ing it with flour, and, out of the mixture, making
“dampers” for the aboriginal. Quite as effective as
powder and ball, and, if Providence failed to make the
black man’s digestive organs capable of negotiating
ground-glass, that wasn’t the Australian pioneer’s fault.

A Splendid Success.
If anyone had said a year ago that thousands of
copies of the expensive ninth edition of the
“Encyclopaedia Britannica ” could be sold in
Great Britain within a few months, he would
have been regarded more as a dreamer than as a
judge of the book-trade. The enterprise, busi-
ness ability and advertising skill and boldness of
the proprietors of The Times, however, has made
what seemed an impossible thing a reality, and a
great literary work that hitherto has only been
found in the homes of the wealthy, or on the
shelves of public libraries and similar institutions,
has found its way into the cottage of the artisan,
the book-case of the clerk, and the solitary apart-
ment of the bachelor student. The manner in
which this has been brought about is no less
creditable to the keen commercial instincts of the
proprietors of The, Times than to their faith in
the public, a,nd their enterprise in embarking on
an undertaking that had in it great elements of
risk and financial loss. Unlike many articles sold
on the instalment principle, The Times not only
offers the “ Encyclopaedia Brittanica ” to residents
of New South Wales at much less than the price
charged the original subscribers to the work, and
at much less than it could be bought for at any
booksellers, but they offer a reprint in every
particular as complete, and as handsome in
appearance, as when first issued by the publishers.
The scheme deserved to succeed, and it is succeed-
in this colony, as it did in London, beyond'every
anticipation.*

There were heavy floods
in Grippsland (Vic.) recently,
and settlers in the vicinity of
the Tambo were washed out of
their bunks at night, and forced
to take refuge on the ridge-
poles of their huts; and the
night was wet and cold, and
the wind blew rheumatic
spasms through the perishing
wretches. The morning was
even windier and colder and
more damp than the night had
been, but the water-logged
watchers had the consolation of
knowing that boats and punts
were out on the face of the
waters collecting the castaways,
and storing them in a com-
fortable pub. One energetic
stranger excited particular ad-
miration and regard by the
enthusiasm he displayed in
yanking his old tub through
the flood, but, as he approached
and passed one washed - out
family after another, affection
changed to hatred, and great
grey curses stalked upon the
waters. The young man was
distributing handbills adver-
tising his marvellous cough-
cure, and his amazing remedy
for rheumatism and ingrowing
chilblains.

Jerome Darnefcon Wostralian slavery, &c. :
In connection with your allegation ('often made) that
native slavery exists in Westralia, I may state that I was
talking-lately to a Sydney mining man about the flotation
of a company to work land in Northern Westralia, when
he said (presumably making the assertion serve as
argument in favor of a successful issue to the co’s
operations): “You know they have their own slaves there.”
Thus, no matter how the Legislature and the Magistracy
of Westralia disguise the matter with their precious
“apprenticeship” laws the ordinary man of the world
bluntly styles the natives Slaves, which they indubit-
ably are.

Dear Bulletin. “ Myall’s ” account (8.,
2/6/’00) of how blacks on the Maranoa (S.W.
Queensland) make fire comes as a revelation to us,
who have lived, fsome of us, for 50 years on
said Maranoa. Also, we wonder how we
can have been so blind as never to have
noticed the blacks preparing for an
opium-smoke, or to cook a shin of
beef, as described by “Myall.”
I read the par. to “Lumpy.”
He said —“Thatpfeller plurry
fool. Baal you got ’im Brine
May (Bryant and May), ’tore-
keepit got ’im ble-e-endy Bell
an’ Black.” As a matter of fact,
the only blacks on the Maranoa
are employed on the stations,
except a few wrecks about the
river townships. Also, “Gar.”
(8., 2/6/’00) can’t have any idea
of the cost of opium when he
talks of getting it up in cases,
like jam, to pay all hands on
out-back stations. Also, blacks
are used on a station for the sake of
economy—it would be the reverse to pay
in opium.—N.d.W.
“ W. 8.” : lie opium - smoking
among the blacks in West Queensland
(“Gar.” B. 2/6/’00). I hardly think
the squatters supply them with the
drug directly, but indirectly they do.
On nearly every station there is
a|Chinaman who keeps it, and I know
one where a lot of blacks do odd jobs,
and every sixpence they get goes in
opium. The small money never goes off that
station; when the Chow has a pound or
two in silver he takes it to the book-
keeper, who gives him a cheque in ex-
change ; then the coin goes back to the black, and
so again to the Chow. The manager is a J.P.,
and, of course, knows where the money goes, yet he
does not interfere. Opium is becoming a curse in
back Queensland —used by both whites and blacks.
I know a postmaster and his wife in a little
town who both indulge in it. One Chow told me
he made £2OO a year by selling the drug.
* * * *
Northern Miner (Towers) tells this
story:
Mr. Gallagher (Cobb and Oo.) got down at the new gate
and was charged by a bullock. He ran round the coach
and threw himself down. The bullock ran past him. On
the bullock returning to charge Mr. G. again fell down,
the bullock tripping too. The animal was slow to rise,
and Mr. G., catching him by the horn with one hand,
turned his head, and caught hold of the tail with the
other. A quick movement overturned the bullock, and
his horn was embedded in the ground, and there he lay
till the coach got out of sight.

nor’-westers: they come very often, are very bad
while they last, but don’t last long.”—Judge
Maktin (M.L.).
London Sun now systematically steals
Bulletin illustrations without acknowledgment.
The Moon—which shines only by reflected light—
were a better designation for the Sun.

At the meeting of the Jewish Literary Sdciety
last night there waa a large attendance of mem-
bers and visitors. The president, Mr. M# M;
Phillips, occupied* the cnair. The Rev. Dr;
Abrahams, M. A., delivered a very interesting au<f
entertaining address on “Gambling.” Tuo
reverend lecturer refrained from sermonising op
the subject, although ho necessarily intimated 1 ,
from time to time, that gambling in its various
forms was opposed to-morality. The totalisatov
was condemned, on the ground that it would tend
to increase the number of gamblers in the com-
munity, and the lecturer strongly objected to
legalising, instead of penalising, any forov of
gambling.

POLITI CAL POINTS
If filthy lucre is the cause of one great
trouble, you have only to add the emblem of
Christianity to the one, and you have the other—
so : Boer, and Bo + er.
If half the lawyers who talk of standing
for seats in the Federal Parliament succeed at the
polls, the Commonwealth will be the most lawyer-
ridden nation in the world.
The N.S.W. Socialist party proposes
putting up six candidates for seats in the Common-
wealth Senate. The Socialists will lose, but will
shake the political life out of wobbly Laborists.
The utter insincerity of it all! Ex-Premier
Reid supported the proposal to send a naval con-
tingent to China, “because Govt, having made
the offer, if the House were to refuse to sanction
it, their action would be misconstrued abroad.”
It would be cheaper to give all those
Ministers titles at once. Until they get and
become used to their “handles,” they will con-
tinue to crawl like worms. Australia will reap
naught but trouble for all the tons of gold it is
going to spend in military swagger.
The story goes that Mr. Hanbtrry-Davies,
the well-known barrister, who for some time past
has conducted the Crown cases before N.S.W.
Land Court, has got notice to quit—the idea being
to provide a place for a well-known Ministerial
henchman lately admitted to the Bar.
Writing to S.M. Herald, “Banjo”
Paterson says N.S.W. First Australian Horse in
the Transvaal is reduced to eight men and two
officers. N.S.W. Lancers are only 35 strong, the
rest of the undisabled having no horses. We sow
“Glory ” and reap tombstones and cripples.
Yremier Seddon flatly refuses, even now
when the war is practically over, to let any of the
Maoriland Contingenters have their discharge in
S’Africa. The applicants had offers of billets and
good pay in the S’African military police, and the
intimation that they can’t accept and must return
to Maoriland whether they like it or not will pro-
bably make them curse Oom Seddon considerably
and ask what Liberty is made of. Supposing they
flatly refuse to come back, will Seddon have them
compulsorily returned in chains?
A SLIGHT MISUNDERSTANDING.
PIGEON-TOED PETE : “ What's the good o’ them
’ere allies what they’re usin' to heat the
Chows? Why don’t they take road-metal to
’em? ”
So far, N.S.W. is sending but 200 or 300
troops to help England hold her end up in China.
Even a flea on an elephant doesn’t describe that
situation. Said a prominent Sydney Chinese
resident about the Chow-Jap. war of a few years
ago : “ If allee Chineemen spit two-tlee time, allee
Jap. be dlowned.”
Melbourne Age has lately been writing-
down George Turner as strongly as it formerly
wrote him up. Which is a sign that David, Syme
thinks M‘Lean has come to stay. The Age more
often jumps with the cat than it “leads” public
opinion, as some people believe it does. 9 X few
weeks since nothing M'Lean did was right for the
Age. The attack failed. Now, everything the
M'Lean Govt, does is right, and Turner is always
wrong. Another factor is that Syme is very ner-
vous about the undoubtedly great growth of the
Argus circulation in the country districts. The
political game has rarely been more interesting in
Victoria than it is just now.
Politicians in Adelaide now take it for
f ranted that ex-Premier Kingston will not only
e a candidate for the Federal Parliament, but
that he will be a member of the first Australian
Govt. It seems to be anticipated with equal
confidence that Symon, Q.C., will be one of the
Judges of the High Court. Kingston arrives in
Adelaide per Ortona this month.
Every figurehead of an Australian daily-
newspaper co. should at once be knighted in the
interest of the shareholders, and also for the pro-
tection of the public from ever-recurrent inflic-
tions of Jingo slush. For, on the average, until
he gets his gew-gaw and rests in comparative
peace, you can see the slimy tracks of his be-
baublement-scheme all over the paper he controls.
The thousandth proof that Africa is a land
of lies, as Winston Churchill says, comes in the
tidings that the British War Office, or the Generals
at the front, have been once more suppressing
disasters. Let nobody forget that all the African
news is doctored to suit Jingo ends —triumphs
magnified, reverses minimised—and that when
the facts come to light, if ever, they will be grue-
some, indeed. It is even cabled that no further
publication of official despatches will be made
until the war has been ended.
Fraser, M.H.R. for Napier (M.L.) on the
proposed admission of women to Parliament:
It is not my intention to give a silent vote upon this
most important question. lam going to raise my voice,
and give my vote in favor of this Bill passing—(a pause)—
into oblivion. This is not a place for women to be sitting,
to be pairing with the men. If this clause is passed I will
endeavor to have it amended to conform to the law of
Raratonga, which prohibits any man being seen out at
night with a woman without carrying a lighted torch in
his hand.
There is no accounting for the candour of some
men.
As a reply to the bogus wail that only rich
men can spend enough money to fight an election
in the large Federal constituencies, ex-Minister
Best comes forward with a Bill making it illegal
for a candidate to spend, directly or indirectly,
more than £lOO on any election in Victoria, and
making it also illegal for other people to spend
money on his account. Somewhat similar brutal
interference with the liberty of the rich subject
are already practised in Britain, Maoriland and
S.A., so there is solid precedent behind Mr. Best’s
idea.
When N.S.W. Leg. Council was consider-
ing the Bill to abolish the garnisheeing of wages
under £2 a week Dr. Bowker, M.L.C., lifted up
his voice to complain that the measure would
“enable people to be dishonest and ungrateful.”
The latter adjective might have been left out.
People can’t be made grateful by Act of Parlia-
ment. Writer has met various persons whose
wages had been garnisheed, and none of them
seemed in the least grateful. In fact the garnishee
system, as a means of making the garnisheed one
thankful and friendly, has always been a failure.
A nut from Premier Salisbury (in the
“ Lords ”) for the anti-alcoholic cranks. Has any
Australasian statesman equally the courage
of his opinions ?
I do not drink beer, but it I did I should
want it on Sundays as on other days. It is pre-
posterous to propose that to prevent a number
of people getting drunk, four or five times as
many are to be prevented that free indul-
gence to which they have a right.
Salisbury obviously agrees with the dis-
tinguished bishop who said—“ I would
rather see England free than England
sober! ”
Maoriland proposes to add £750
to the Premier’s salary, making it
£1750, and £450 to other Ministers’
salaries, making them £1250. The
Premier’s increase ought to be to
£5OOO and Ministers to £3OOO. A.
boss bank-manager, who doesn’t
look after one-twentieth of the mere
casli-interests that a Premier has to
carry on his shoulders, gets from
£3OOO to £5OOO. Then it is pro-
. posed to increase the allowances of
the practically ornamental Governor
by £2500, which will make his screw
£7500. A case of £1250 for the
worker who runs the country and
six times as much for the dancing
dervish in a uniform who looks on.
Carruthers’ lone-hand amend-
ment in N.S.W. Assembly against
Lyne’s motion to send troops to
China showed the re-opening of the
badly-healed rift between himself and
Premier Reid, which several times
threatened the existence of the last
Administration.
A much-experienced N.S.W. Parliamen-
tarian distinguishes between a “Green” and
“Yellow ” committee for Parliamentary elections.
“ The Green,” he says, “ means money; bedad, I
know. They’ve all mouths on them, and the
drouth begins early and lasts till next election,
bedad it does. But the Yellow boys stand to their
man. There’s Dash and Blank and Poley—they
hadn’t to put their hands in their pockets the last
time, or any time. Just if they say ‘To h—l with
the Pope,’ off and on, they’re all right.”
Federated Australia should demand that
the Hansard of its House of Representatives be
an absolutely correct and photographic report of
the debates. At present, the provincial Han-
sards are not only put into readable form by the
reporters, but are subject to almost unlimited sub-
editing by the speakers themselves. This practice
is diametrically opposed to theHANSARD principle.
All the idiotic maunderings of the member for
Smith’s Curse should be set down in pitiless detail
for his electors to ponder over, and the Hansard
reporter should be a mere stenographic machine,
instead of the flowery, poetical, descriptive writer
he so often is. Some of the existing Hansard
speeches resemble the real article just about as
much as a gaol-photo, resembles the same person
painted by a society portrait artist.
IT MIGHT BREAK UP
THE SHOW.
PARSON (to Miss A. C.
Erbity): “ May I put you
down for a subscription to our bazaar?”
Miss A. C. R.: “ Well, no. But I shall be glad
to lend my countenance to the cause."
The Australian Natives’ Association of
N.S. Wales was established in a room at Sydney
Temperance Hall, the other night, when 28
signed the register and became the original mem-
bers. Then the meeting proceeded to elect
officers. Someone proposed Mayor Harris as
president, and one-third of those present almost
fell dead with fright when it was pointed out that
Mr. Harris couldn’t be elected, as he wasn’t a
member there-and-then. Had the point failed,
someone intended to move that the newly-formed
A.N.A. be abolished forthwith as imbecile, and
consumptive, and hollow, and doomed to stay out
in the wet. It narrowly escaped Harris —and
inanity.
The new law-term started disastrously for
N.S.W. Govt, when the Full Court delivered
reserved judgments on appeals. The first and
second cases were appeals from the Railway Com-
missioners against two verdicts in which plaintiffs
had been awarded substantial damages. The
Commissioners lost both, with costs. The third
case was the long-standing Josephson v. Young,
which defines the right to compensation of a tem-
porary officer for loss of position. This verdict
will be an expensive one for Government, as
scores of cases will be governed by it. Premier
Lyne is having a return prepared as to the total
value of claims likely to be lodged under the
decision.
It is probable that Chamberlain will have
more to do with appointing the first Federal Pre-
mier than will the greatly-canvassed Hopetoun.
When N.S.W. received Responsible Govt, the
name of the first Premier was similarly a cause of
wide speculation. The Home Office wanted Deas-
Thomson to fill the Bill, and pressed Gov. Deni-
son to nominate him, but Deas-Thomson was not
sure of being able to form a Ministry, and hung
back. Finally, Donaldson, whom neither the
Home Office nor Denison cared for, was placed in
front. Chamberlain has had an opportunity of
personally meeting almost all tfie men passable for
the position, and the likelihood is that Hopetoun’s
mind is already settled on the point.
T. W. Taylor’s victory over Pumpkin
Smith in the second contest for the Canterbury
(N.S.W.) seat, by 47 votes, will add another to the
large Lyne majority. Yet the result can hardly
be termed a Govt, triumph, for the election was
decided almost wholly on personal and local issues.
The winning candidate owes his success to the
townsfolk of Liverpool itself, where he had a
majority of 220. Sydney Smith’s defeat was
rather unexpected, as it was thought the sympathy
always felt for the man who is unseated on
petition would save him. In the adjoining con-
stituency of Parramatta, Dowell O’Reilly upset
Ferris, who had a majority of five at last general
election. At the fresh election Ferris regained
his seat by over 100 majority.
Maoriland Hansar,d chief of staff. Grey,
who has got the sack because of his anti-Jingo
writings, managed to get neatly back on his perse-
cutors. Agent-General Reeves lately sent out to
Premier Seddon a long letter, in reply to the
abuse that has been heaped on him (Reeves) for
telling the truth about the war. In this letter he
proves all his assertions up to the hilt from English
official sources. Seddon laid the letter on the
table of the House without reading it. Under
ordinary circumstances it would not have appeared
in Hansard. Grey seized his chance, however,
and printed it among the debates, where it reads
like an oasis of fact and common-sense amid a
desert of Jingo drivel. When Hansard appeared
there was, of course, a howl over the “ breach of
privilege, ” but it was too late. Seddon disclaimed
all responsibility in the matter. When the affair
was referred to a committee, however, it was
shown that the proof-sheet of his speech handed
to him for revision also contained a proof of the
etter.
S.A. politicians are fearful lest by the
proposed new railway from Portland to the
vicinity of Mt. Gambier, Victoria will practically
annex the best of the S.E. district, lying between
Murray and Glenelg. The strip of country on
the S.A. side of the disputed boundary has always
been more in sympathy with Western
Victoria than with its own capital.
However, S.A. cannot grumble, as
she has appropriated Broken Hill
and is now reaching after the
Queensland border trade.
N.S.W. Attorney-General
Wise has now an opportunity
of doing a great practical
work as a law-reformer.
His grasp of principles,
as evidenced by his
speech on the Arbitra-
tion Bill, would serve
him well in dealing with
a big law-reform, such
as, for instance, the
long-delayed Judicature
Act. N.S.W., it ap-
pears, will be the last
of the self - governing
British possessions to
supersede the present-
obsolete complicated,
stupid and highly-tech-
nical system of plead-
ing and procedure. The
adoption of such an Act
would render useless half
the contents of a N.S.W.
lawyers’ library of to-day.
Obsolete editions of law-books,
useless everywhere else, are
transported to N.S.W., where
they are in full bloom. Any edition of “Bullen
and Leake’s Pleading ” later than 1868 is useless
in N.S.W., as too advanced. Wise, by a Judica-
ture Act, could substitute order and simplicity for
what is now a puzzle alike to litigants, juries,
lawyers and judges.
The Lyne Govb., which, bar that of John
Forrest, is now the oldest in Australia, will not
complete its first year until Sept. 14. None of the
other cabinets date back further than October,
that of Lewis (Tas.) coming next in seniority.
By-the-way, does ex-Premier Reid still stick to*
his opinion that the option of being first Prime
Minister of Federated Australia must be offered
to the Premier who happens to rule N.S. Wales
when the Commonwealth is formed ? It has been
generally assumed that, in that case, Lyne would
pass the office along to Barton ; but latest indica-
tions are that Lyne, if he gets the refusal of the
Federal Premiership, means to clutch it with
hands, feet and teeth. Which tends to upset the
hitherto-accepted story that Lyne and Barton had
an understanding on the matter.
A Mr. Rymill, one of the directors of the
syndicate which fooled Tasmania so long with
promises to build the Great Western Railway, and
got concessions and land-grants, and then failed to-
put up the price of even half-a-mile of line, has
been bellowing and pawing the grass in Hobart.
He told a public meeting that his awful syndicate-
“ had suffered cruel wrongs which, without some
compensation, would be a lasting disgrace to the
colony.” Healso “condemned theaction of Premier
Braddon in sending the telegram which stopped
the flotation.” The syndicate, be it remembered,
wanted over £BOO,OOO cash and shares, in return
for handing over to the buyers the bare right to-
build, at their own cost, a railway worth about
£600,000. It fooled around so long trying to per-
petrate this monstrous job that its concession ex-
pired just as the job was about to come off. And
the syndicate was going right on selling its dead
and expired concession when Braddon honestly
informed the buyers that, until the concession was-
renewed, there was nothing to sell. This is,
apparently, the “cruel wrong” referred to.
Maoriland Government, after five years of
war, arbitration, litigation and acting as bailiff in
possession, has now definitely come into owner-
ship of the unfinished Midland land-grantrailway
through failure of the much defaulted co. to build
the line. Like every other private railway co. in
Australasia, it refused to risk its own money on
its own railway, and borrowed instead at huge
interest. When a co. says in effect, “We have so*
little faith in our own spec, and our own manage-
ment and our own honesty that we won’t risk our
own coin, but we want to risk yours,” it has-
always to pay ruinous interest. The interest, the
huge expenses in London and the general mis-
management killed the Midland co. ; it went on as-
long as it could borrow, and when it had borrowed
as much as the finished line was worth and hadn’t
half built the line, and couldn’t borrow any more
at any price, it stopped. Now the people who
lent it money, and were swindled, appeal to the
M.L. Govt, to give them back their coin ; and the
London papers are reasonably unanimous that
they should have it back. If M.L. pays them,
its Midland line will cost nearly twice what it
is worth, and then it will probably be abused in
the London press for not giving the fraudulent
co. back the money it spent on chainpaene antics-
in its London office.

So far, Adelaide has had six cases of
alleged plague in as many months, and all
have been discovered in Adelaide Hospital. It is
freely asserted that a rat which had been experi-
mented upon escaped, and as the public firmly
believe the statement, there is no excitement,
although two fresh cases came to light last week.
One was that of a patient who had been in the
hospital for three weeks with chronic rheumatism,
and the other sufferer had been in for pleurisy.
The poor woman whose husband was
crushed to death in the Enmore (Sydney) tram col-
lision recently (for which a tram-driver stands com-
mitted on a charge of manslaughter) was badgered
by the solicitor representing the tram commis-
sioners in the coroner’s court. “Was your hus-
band in employment?” “He was going to a
job.” “Now, was he not one of the unem-
ployed ? ” The meaning of this line of question-
ing was that N.S.W. courts only give damages
proportioned to the position in life of the deceased,
and the attorney wanted to show that the widow
couldn’t or shouldn’t claim much if her husband
was earning nothing.
“ Ceth ” : Be that Bulletin par. concern-
ing N.S.W. Justice Owen’s decision on ques-
tion of domicile. The facts as therein set out are
not quite correct, and the Judge’s decision was
not as stated, although his decision was, to say
the least, startling. Respondent had, prior to his
arrest on a charge of bigamy, lived at Annandale,
Sydney, during which time he was periodically
away in Queensland on his business. Re-
spondent set up and endeavoured to contend that
he never legally acquired a domicile in N.S.W.
Judge Owen held that the N.S.W. Court had no
jurisdiction, and ruled that respondent was not
domiciled in N.S.W. at present, having abandoned
his domicile there at time of his arrest ; and
further that he was not domiciled in Queensland
although he had been in that province for very
many years. The Judge also decided, that Re-
spondent’s domicile now was that of his origin—
viz., Canada ! Ye gods !

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Coming elections for three vacancies on A. J.C.
committee promise something interesting. Which is a
change, for hitherto it has ever been the same old combi-
nation. Eight or nine candidates are mentioned—among
them W. P. Crick, whose self-imposed wing-clipping mis-
sion may be affected by the result, especially as erstwhile
chairman W. A. Long is among the seceders. Hon. W.
P.’s platform is said to be open-door inquiries, and legis-
lation in the direction of improving the position of country
clubs.
• * * *
List of winning owners in Australia for season
closed Tuesday is topped by the late Mr. W. R. Wilson
(£6oo7)—just over £2OO in excess of sum won per medium
of Merriwee by Mr. H. Power.
* * * *
Private letters tell that Cornstalk-leviathan
“ Oxy ”is setting 'em ago at ’Ome. He’s been scooping
big piles at punting, and gave 40 English sports a recherche
spread at the Hotel Cecil, which topped most things of
its kind seen there.
* • ■ *
Wollar Mountains, for which the Breelong
(N.S.W.) aboriginal murderers are said to have made, are
the ranges where the racehorse Cranbrook was hidden
when stolen from Havilah station. Cranbrook was taken
out of Havilah stables one night and secreted in the grim
ranges, with the object of having him do a season s stud-
dutv, and, when that purpose had been served, word was
sent to Havilah where the horse was to be found, and he
was discovered safe and sound. It must be nearly.five
years since Cranbrook did his mountain-trip, and it is
about time that some upstanding chestnuts blazed down
the nose were winning races about that region.
* m * •
When Baden-Powell learns the price paid for
his presentation hunters —£150 and £lO0 —he will not
form a very high opinion of the value, as measured by
price, of Australian studs.
Paris —winner of successive Caulfield Cups—was
seen out at Rosehill, Saturday, for first time since that
lengthy and unproductive trip ’Ome. The old ’un showed
little of his one-time dash, and 7 fur. of the big ’andicap
found him out so badly that he whipped the field in, a
100yds. or more away. He presented a jolly-and-fat ap-
pearance, but doesn’t look like a possible future winner,
considering the weights he will be asked to carry, so
writer rises to say that Mr. White should adhere to his
original determination, and allow the ancient to end his
days luxuriating in the well-grassed paddocks of Havilah.
The privilege has been well earned.
“ Lemon ” : The Jack M’Gowan-Cullen fight in Melb. last
week proved, as anticipated, a victory for the scienced
hard-hitting man over the one who wa3 a hard-hitter
alone. M’Gowan scored early with a sweeping knock-
down blow ; three rounds later he had Cullen in a corner,
fairly dazed with bod.v blows until the gong saved him ;
finally, he practically knocked him out with a clean
right drive. Cullen came up, but fell at first tap, and the
fight went to M’Gowan. Though the beaten man did
nothing sensational he fought all through with pluck and
spirit, and made M’Gowan fight all he knew. That is a
bit less than when he went away with Bland Holt. His
last fight prior to leaving was with Tot Higgins, probably
the cleverest 10-stoner in Australia, and, although
M’Gowan was giving away 2in. and 101 b., he out-pointed
Higgins from start to finish. Cullen, on the other hand,
is lighter than his visitor, and makes no pretence at
cleverness. So M’Gowan will have to pick up lost form
to have a show in his projected fight with champion
Hegarty.
• • • *
Broken Hill (N.S.W.) pug-boom is still strong,
albeit frequency of draws gives the game a bad flavor.
Nine-tenths of the matches end without a winner, but the
purse is available just the same. Latest cases were Billy
M’Kell and Wal M’Kay, and Billy Williams and Hiscutt.
Last-named is a miner, and a new hand at bruising, but
he’s a good ’un who’ll be heard from often. Cycling-
trainer Billy Williams has reduced the art of cutting-even
to a science. Wonder how Billy and “ Cocker ” Tweedie
will finish up their approaching go?
* * * *
“ Old Timer” : Nick Dooley and Bill Doherty
—present premier heavyweight pug. of the provinces—
will tussle for the title and what looks like being a big
“ gate ”on Monday night in Sydney. Writer saw Dooley
on Sunday. He never looked better or more confident.
Doherty will be as well as when he met the weak-hitting
and weaker-hearted lathy black (Felix) recently, and he
couldn’t be much better than then. Dooley should win,
ancient and all though the whilom Sydney Govt. Printing
Office comp, may be. He is cleverer, heavier and longer
in the reach than Doherty, who can hit like a mule kick-
ing, but has to get within a yard of his man to do it, and
then mostly swings. He ought not to take the champion-
ship back to Westralia; but punishing fighters of
Doherty’s pluck always have a chance with the cleverest.
Vancouver mail last week brought a huge sur-
prise in the news that the Akron giant—Gus Ruhlin—had
badly beaten Sailor Sharkey, and, on his showing, looked
a sure conqueror of world’s champion Jeffries. The fight
was of the fiercest, and Sharkey only succumbed after
sustaining a terrific, battering in 15 rounds of hurricane
battle. Up to this Ruhlin ranked among third-raters
in public estimation, but it turns out now that Pompa-
dour Jim Corbett has been coaching him quietly for a
long time. Six thousand people went to see Sharkey
make a chopping-block of Ruhlin, for he had settled him
with one punch two years before. Ruhlin shaped amaz-
ingly and showed great judgment. Straight lefts met
the ’ Sailor’s rushes and bruised his face beyond
recognition. Sharkey was blinded, and, to all in-
tents and purposes, absolutely helpless when Ruhlin
floored him first in the isth round. Five times subse-
quently the broken-up bull struggled up and charged,
only to be sent back smash. Sharkey rushed all through
the struggle and swung blows of fearful force, but none
landed fair enough to do much hurt, while the enemy’s
smashes often jarred from head to heel. Ruhlin—like the
most accomplished of the Mace school—took no chances
in mixing unless forced to, and used the sinister mauley
exclusively until he had his man where he wanted him.
Sharkey was literally whipped to ribbons from the start.
Ruhlin’s display and the outcome created a sensation and
at latest the winner was hot after Jeffries with piles of
dollars behind him. Ruhlin did to Sharkey in 15 rounds
what Jeffries failed to do in 25 when the pair had that
hair-raising, bashing battle some few months back.
* * * *
In Victoria the howl of the battered umpire is
again loud in the land. Umpires are appealing tremu-
lously to be excused from officiating at this ground or
that out of consideration for their wives and families. At
Wodonga t’other day the umpire fled for his life on a
bike, and two men were carted out of the scrum to the
nearest hospital. When the barracker rises up against
the umpire with bricks and sticks, gate-takings grow
bulky and the footballer is abundantly glorified. A con-
scientious umpire would grin and bear it for the sake of
the ganie.
* * , * *
Recent football match at Thurgoona (N.S.W.)
between Jindera and Black Range Clubs supplied heaps of
fight. Opposing players knocked lumps off each other.
Previous struggles and happenings pointed to trouble,
hence the neutral ground ; but that made no difference—
the row came just the same. Roll-call found three
“ Rangers ”in parlous plight; one filled a vacant bed at
Albury Hospital later. Umpire’s cuteness in bringing a
bike enabled him to get off whole, otherwise he might
have been gathered up in sections. Records were not in it
with his dash for life.
« * * *
So far, Sydney ’Varsity football club takes the
bun for maimed players this season. Marks (a last year’s
international) was carried off Saturday with a broken
ankle. Invalided (all badly used up) list is now Wood,
White, Marks and ’Blue, while several others are tem-
porarily incapacitated.
Hard enough is the lot of the football umpire
who fails to please both teams, but as for him who pleases
neither—words fail! At a junior match in Vic. recently
a guileless youth had volunteered to act as umpire for a
remuneration of four “ long-sleevers.” Falling foul of
both sides, the respective captains, at the end of the
game, took turn about at sitting heavily on his stomach
and smiting him on the nose with the ball, the players
meantime chanting, “ What ho, she bumps ! ” Then he
was rolled in the mud and finally escorted to the nearest
pub. and swamped with consolatory tanglefoot.
♦ * * *
The sensation of Saturday’s Sydney football was
provided at N. Sydney, where anticipations were knocked
endways by home-side romping over Western Suburbs,
totting up 44 to 3. First-grade competition hasn’t (up to
now) produced anything so convincing as this. Winning
side’s (all-round) preponderance, of points was big.
Balmain broke its egg by downing Newtown —25 to 3.
Glebe gave S. Sydney no hope from start to finish ; and
“ Unis” notched 29 to E. Suburbs’ 3.
* » * *
M. L. football-rulers, have decided not to invite
a N.S.W. team over this season owing to the insufficiency
of guarantees made by affiliated unions towards exes, the
generally expressed wish that each province should in
future finance its own inter-state tours, and the brief time
at disposal for making arrangements.
Victoria and S.A. met again at football in
Adelaide, July 21, after a big interval. Former won
easily by 9 goals to 5, but the game is booming again in
Adelaide, as shown by the gate, £270, the biggest on local
record. Visitors played splendid football.
# * * *
S.A. probably loses fast-bowler Jones, who is
negotiating for the lease of a Broken Hill hotel. Jones
has not worked on the ground staff of Adelaide Oval
since his return from England, and S.A. Cricket Associa-
tion is almost certain not to renew his retainer of £2 10s.
per week when it expires in Sept. S.A. eleven will be
very weak next season with Jones and Darling absent.
M’Kibbin will probably be available, but it is suggested
that a first-class pro. should be brought from England.
* * * *
Vic. lacrosse team —though not the best that
province could send —fairly “laid over” N.S.W. at Syd-
ney Cricket Ground, Saturday. Home men showed little
science, comparatively speaking, and lack of condition
often broke them up completely. They mulled passes
with monotonous frequency and attacked inefficiently
through want of combination. Visitors’ play proved an
eye-opener. Onlookers saw lacrosse in Sydney (as she
should be played) for first time. N.S.W. scored two
goals in initial quarter, but did nothing subsequently,
and opposing side finished 10 to the good. T’other day
S.A. badly beat ari admittedly powerful Vic. combination
—which fact sets one thinking how N.S.W. men rank as
exponents of the game, and why they want to get a team
from Canada (the home of lacrosse) over here. Hadn’t
they better learn a bit first ?
* • * •
Yankee Plugger Bilmartin, in Australia now,
will quit these parts for U.S. shortly. Someone has found
out that from ’B5 to ’99 inclusive Bill got away with 221
first, 74 second and 37 third prizes. Most fruitful year
for him was ’95, when he raced into places 118 times—B4
firsts. But for a deal of travelling in ’97 and ’9B total
might have reached far bigger figures. Australia has
treated Bill not unkindly.
* * * •
World’s-champion billiardist, Roberts, has been
engaged in an exhibition-match in Sydney with Aus-
tralian top-notcher, Weiss, afternoon and evening since
Wednesday. Saturday night saw him perform marvel-
lously, reeling off a break of 500 in 32min., during which
every conceivable stroke was shown. Weiss looked on
amazed, and when the white stopped at the jaws of the
left-hand upper pocket on the verge of tottering—thus
cutting the score at the even half-thousand—the scene
was thrilling. Latest achievement ranks amongst
Roberts’ very best. Once he compiled 597 and, twice,
over 500. Dawson’s 722 is the record, with Stevenson’s
648 next. Right up to the end of the run interest was at
high tension, as there seemed a possibility of a world’s
best being put up in Sydney.
* * • •
Australian pigeon-shot Don. Mackintosh shoots
from 33yds. at ’Ome now. June 9 saw him, at Notting
Hill, annex a big £5 sweep (cup added) from 32yds. Two
or three days later, at same place and from a yard
further off, he grassed 16 out of 18 and got away with
four pools.
# * * *
After all, N.S.W. scullers Towns and Wray
haven’t got together “down under” for the ’Ome
supremacy, and are not likely to, apparently. Last mail
told that another wrangle had occurred. Wray wanted
sterns of boats moored at starting-place ; Towns stuck out
for bows. Neither would budge. Stump outrigger and
the old style of shell are at the bottom of the difference.
Towns’condition means a level send-off. Wray couldn’t
be coaxed to Lopdon to argue the thing out. Doesn’t
look as if Wray is over-anxious for the match, nor has he
seemed so all along.

Flapdoodle fed to his readers by the dog-reporter
of Sydney D.T. : “ Master Graham Sands, son of Mr.
Robert Sands, of Waverley (a D.T. director, by the way) is
a great lover of dogs, and has chosen Irish terriers as his
hobby. A wise decision, as the breed is very game, com-
panionable, and popular. Master Sands’ initial selection
is an exceptionally well-bred puppy, being by Gordon
Blarneystone ex Gordon Bush Girl.” Also Mr. Dinny
M’Rourke’s little boy Tim has started a kennel and has
chosen miscellaneous yellow dogs for his hobby, his
initial selection being a slut puppy ex Mrs. Hogap’s wall-
eyed bitch Bonescruncher by the dog that belongs to the
dustman in Frog Hollow, behind O’Hoolighan’s pub.
The strain is a highly fashionable one. M’Rourke will be
cordially welcomed to the fold of the Fancy.
■* ♦ * *
Dear Bulletin. —In his criticisms (8., 21/7/’00)
on our last annual sports meeting Mr. H. Stockdale was
evidently going upon hearsay. Only two events clashed,
and, to rectify matters, consolation races were run imme-
diately, thus allowing the programme to be run to time.
Mr. S. says that no events should be judged by masters.
But the judging is done by the head-masters of the Syd-
ney schools, the inspectors, and officers of the P.1.D.,
gentlemen who cannot, except in rare cases, be ac-
quainted with the contestants. There is, therefore, no
favoritism or unfairness in the matter. Mr. S. has more
justice in his remarks about the unfair walking, though
he should take into 'account the difficulty in discriminat-
ing between a fast walk apd.running, also the scarcity of
competent ■ judges of walking, and the difference of
opinion as to points'; In the quarter-mile walking event
mentioned we allow the 11-years-and-under boy 50 yards,
and it is only a printer’s error which makes it seem other-
wise. The throwing-at-wicket competitions we leave in
the hands of experts who furnish the competitors
with details before competing. We, however, thank Mr.
S. for his well-meant suggestions.— W. Gee, General Sec.
P.S.A.A.

“Spike”: That note (8., 28/7/’00) re cornstalk
sprinter Rowley on opposite side of the sphere contains a
nasty vein of spite—in its insinuations about axes to grind,
bunglers, &c. Rowley went with the best possible en-
dorsement, viz., Australian premiership, and his particu-
lar mission was the Championship of England, wherein he
ran fourth after (a day or two before) defeating the then
holder—Wadsley—by 2yds. Writer would stake his all that
first, second and third men were Yankee 9 4-5 performers,
en route to Olympian Games. Mail will shortly bring de-
tails. Author of par referred to must have been dead
a long time, otherwise he wouldn’t question lOsec. feats
for 100yds. ; that used to be the game 10 or 15 years ago,
but doubters have been brought up with a round turn
often since. Further proof that manufacturer of par in
question has had a Rip-Van-Winkle sleep is evidenced bv
his having to go back to Botany Handicaps (Ancient his-
tory), and “ when London Referee was started.” Very
much up-to-date argument, isn’t it ?
* * * *
“Melb.”: Touching your footnote to par. of
30/6/’OO, a ref. to Argus or Age of 2nd Jan., ’69, will show
that on previous day, at V.R.O. meeting, poet Gordon’s
b. g. Viking, 6 yrs., won a race styled the “ Grand National
Steeplechase.” It was peculiar for the fact that, during
the second round, the rails generally taken down in order
to allow the horses to come into the straight were not re-
moved, and Ballarat, Babbler and Ingleside, not noticing
the mistake, went on inside, thereby allowing Denny
Callanan, on Viking, to jump into the course and steal
the race out of the fire. Viking’s rider was the father of
the present Denny Callanan, who started his career as a
jockey in the late W. R. Wilson’s colors.
Petersham (Sydney) electorate cricket club re-
cently elected its office-bearers for the next season—
patron, president, and vice-presidents. To the last
billets the mayors and aldermen of Petersham and
Marrickville (20 in all) were first appointed. Then 4 0
individuals were elected in addition ! Apparently not a
resident in the electorate who is likely to have a spare
guinea has escaped. It would be a huge joke on the
executive of the club if all of the 60 vice-presidents were
to unexpectedly turn up at a committee meeting and
assume the control of affairs.
* * * *
Joe Darling’s Tasmanian purchase is a 7000-acre
sheep-station in the north of the island, on which the
left-hander intends to combine sheep and wheat-growing.
* * * *
Dead heats in “lepping” races are rare, but
Wellington (M.L.) witnessed one t’other day, when Cheva-
lier (12st. up) and Hauriri (9st.) couldn’t be separated in
£3OO hurdles. Fourteen lined up. Chevalier started a
hot pot, but Hauriri had little support. Unplaced lot in-
cluded Plain Bill (12.4), who staggered punters next day
by easily annexing three miles steeple, and 350 sovs.
connected therewith-humping 31b. more in saddle. Ways
of nags or manipulators thereof are truly bewildering,
which is why Mo waxes fat.
At Poverty Point.
Mrs. Langtry was married to her first husband at the
age of 18, he being 35. She treated him badly and
deserted him. The famous beauty is now 43, and very
much in love with hubby No. 2 (De Bathe), who is 17
years younger. The youth is fighting in the Boer war,
and the constant fear that something serious will befall
him has injured his spouse’s health so much that her re-
cent American dramatic season was a failure. She even
broke down in reciting the “ Absent Minded Beggar.”
Nemesis !
According to London Truth, Lalla Miranda, at her
debut in London as Gilda in “ Rigoletto,” amused the
house by making the traditional blunder of the part in
leaving her lighted candle outside in the garden, and,
presumably, going to bed in the dark, while warbling
“ Caro nome.”
“ Constance Clyde ” : An interesting gloom pervaded
Sydney Criterion on Friday night, where the audience
wept softly and enjoyed itself in a scholastic way when a
certain unfortunate Israelite was exposed to scoffs and
jeers merely for offering to reduce a stout Venetian to
more reasonable proportions. That is how Dampier acts
the Jew. The pathetic side of Shylock’s character comes
more to the front with every generation of actors, and
the time will arrive when the gallery boy will cheer the
knife-whetting process, and shout “ Give the poor old
bloke his pounder flesh, can’t yer ! ” when the good Duke
tries to make plaintiff not only render up his meat, but
actually consent to lose the money as well- all this in
consideration of having been spat on by the defaulter
when the latter was prosperous and suffered from swelled
Venetian head. Under Dampier’s management there were
two gems in the performance—Shylock and the old man
who brought along the basket of provisions to give to any
nobleman who would take his son into his employ. Evi-
dently the aristocracy of these days were not- proud.
Portia did best where she looked her part least. This is
a dark saying and best not expounded. Bassanio’s
glorious, gold-sheathed limbs made the artistic mind
keenly regret the waste of statuesquely-formed men con-
demned to shapeless coats and baggy pants under our
present civilisation, where men are only permitted to be
beautiful above the collar-line. A Greek god might be
seated next us in the tram any day, and under the pre-
sent system of tubular clothing we should never know it.
After months of parley, F. H. Pollock succeeds Wybert
Reeve as lessee of Adelaide Royal. Pollock has been
mixed up with the profession for 40 years, and managed
Garner’s Rooms, Adelaide (now Rickards’) for Reeve 30
years ago. Wybert Reeve goes to London for good.
Regina Nagel it seems, was brought out by Melba
within a month of her arrival in London. She sang first
at the Duchess of Sutherland’s place. Previously Melba
had taken her to Panzani, the little Italian singing master,
who was for years with Marchesi as accompanist, and who
left the old lady to go to London on his own. Panzani
pronounced Nagel’s to be one of the finest voices possible
to have, especially in the lower register.
“ Edson ”: The talk about unusual booming of
“ Magda ” O’Neil in Melb. as a business trick is absurd, as
the O’Neil has only been advertised exactly as any other
showman or woman is advertised. The appearance of
exceptional booming is entirely due to the heated contro-
versy created by Nance’s work and her peculiar person-
ality. No actress who has struck Yarrapolis has created
so much stree.t-corner barneying and common talk as the
latest American. Nance O’Neil dodges the booming for
which visiting actresses are usually most greedy—that
which comes from social hob-nobbing. In standing aloof
very likely Nance is complying with a cute business idea
of Rankin’s. Duse did the same in London, enveloping
herself in mystery when “ off,” and it “ took ” immensely.
Says a Melbourne weekly: “ The mother of that talented
singer, Miss Amy Castles, is not too-well pleased with her
daughter’s tuition. The great Marchesi’s fees amount to
some £2O a week, and then the teacher does not always
give personal supervision.” As a matter of fact, Marchesi,
in her wealthy old age, is getting to be a very casual
instructress, but the idea of Amy’s mother, of Bendigo,
being “ not too-well pleased” with her daughter’s educa-
tion is regarded as a rather good joke by people personally
acquainted with the various characters in the Castles
Romance. By the way, little Amy has been heard of
from London, where she interviewed Melba, who told her
she had a “ perfectly beautiful voice,” and would be “ a
credit to Australia.” The last phrase sounds very
patronising. There are already a lot of exported damsels
doing credit to Australia in a lower musical world than
that of Melba, Frances Saville, and Miranda, not one of
whom had £5OOO, or even £5OO, thrown at her before she
went to Europe.
Sydney D.T.’s London correspondent: “ The details of
Melba’s divorce, which arrived by this week’s mail, will
not add to the diva’s popularity. The demand for seats on
Melba nights at the Opera is (the war notwithstanding) as
great as ever. But the Princess of Wales doesn’t seem to
be occupying the Royal box quite so frequently.”
The plans of George Musgrove, who will open his show
at Melbourne Princess in October, are still shrouded in
diplomatic mystery. All the same there is a notion
abroad that Musgrove has secured Henry Irving and Co.
for an Australian tour.
Cinquevalli, in a recent letter to his compatriot Benno
Scherek, talks of returning to Australia next year, and
when he comes the miraculous Pole will probably be even
a little more popular than before. He left a name behind
him, whereas other big “ turns ” that have headed the
bill at Rickards’s were—with the exception of, perhaps,
Chirgwin—pretty well worked out in six months. The
juggler had a wider constituency than any of them, and
it was still spreading when his engagement ended. He
got to be spoken of among the outer-circle people who are
never tempted into a theatre except by a very particular
curiosity. One hears such people regretting that they
didn’t see Cinquevalli. They will make a point of seeing
him if he turns up again.
