The strained relations which have long
subsisted between one of the chief officers and a
subordinate in N.S.W. Registrar of titles Office
have resulted in the sacking of the latter by the
P.S.B. The decision is to be challenged m Par-
liament, and the Service awaits the result with
interest.
The cure of piles by the new “ Cannalin
treatment is fully described in the pamphlet,
“Piles, and How to Cure Them,” by Albert
Sharp. Copies, with testimonials, sent free to any
colony from the depot, 257 George-street, Sydney.*
Commenting recently on a case of police-
hounding-down of an ex-convict who was striving
to lead an honest life, a Sydney Judge adverted
to the fact that there is not in Sydney, as in Mel-
bourne, a Discharged Prisoners’ Aid Society—
whereupon the inevitable professional-philanthro-
pist. Ardill was instantly to the front with an
assertion that he runs an association of the kind.
Will Ardill kindly publish—in serial instalments,
of course—a list of all the “ philanthropic ” irons
he has in the fire.
Be the recent wreck of the schooner Em-
press of India, at Forster (N.S.W.), and the con-
sequent drowning of some persons of no importance,
the coroner’s jury returned a verdict of “acci-
dentally drowned,” and “expressed surprise at
the vessel being allowed to leave Sydney without
life-belts aboard.” It might reasonably have ex-
pressed anger, but to express surprise was foolish.
If a vessel was allowed to leave Sydney without
bottom or sides there might be room for a certain
perfunctory astonishment, but nothing less than
that is worth being surprised about.
The Absent-Minded Premier was sulking
over something the other night—possibly over
something said by the obese Oppositionist—and
although the morning paper men sent in re-
peatedly to see him and ask him “ what was on,”
or likely to be, he preserved a frigid and repellant
attitude and would not leave the House. During
the evening he (as he thought) laid on the table a
certain paper meant for Parliamentary consump-
tion, which was actually a confidential paper from
the Agent-General re an appointment. The
A.-G. barracked for this appointment, which was
not the one intended by the Absent-Minded
Premier ; his was a political appointment—the
A.-G’s was a common-sense commercial one.
When the pressmen, as usual, approached to find
out what had been laid before the House, they
came across the confidential letter, and, not un-
wondering, copied it. Some hours later—to be
precise, about 3 a.m.—the Absent-Minded Premier
found out that he had laid the wrong egg, or
something, dashed out for a belated cab, and drove
furiously round to the offices of S.M. Herald and
Telegraph to stop publication. He was received
most politely by the sympathetic night-subs.—but,
alas ! the editions had gone to press, and, indeed,
the earlier ones to the country. The agitated
politician drove back glumly.

S.M. Herald, giving a list of aboriginal
names suitable for the N.S.W. Federal consti-
tuencies, includes “Tara” among the bunch!
Alas for “ The Harp that Once ! ”
At the formal hoisting of the British flag
at Johannesburg, a tall Free State artillerist, it is
related by Bennett Burleigh, refused to remove
his hat, and a fellow-spectator tried to pull it o f.
Whereupon a British soldier standing near—tore
the man’s hat from his head and trampled it
underfoot, as one of the Australian stay-at-home
Jingoes would have done ? No—he pushed the
aggressor away, saying—“ Let the man alone !
He has fought for his flag—you don’t fight for
any ! ” Which was true chivalry.
Justice G. B. Simpson, trying the Patrick
Pratt murder-case, in Sydney Criminal Court, last
week, adjourned at the usual hour in the evening,
observing to the jury that “ it is improper in a
capital case to sit until a late hour.” It probably
did not occur to his Honor that he sat in the seat
formerly occupied by Judges who bullied juries
into verdicts in capital cases through all hours of
the night.
With the unconditional surrender of 5000
of the Boers’ slender remaining forces, the end of
the S’African War (which, it is officially an-
nounced, will by February next have cost sixty
millions sterling) can surely not be far off. The
Transvaalers announce that they intend to con-
tinue the present guerilla warfare while awaiting
the result of the U.S. presidential election, and
declare that the Democrats have promised to inter-
vene if the election is in their favor. Which
prospect of another foreign war is not likely to
help the Democrats in their electoral campaign.
The regret for the recent assassination of
the King of Italy involves, for the world in
general, something of a personal character, inas-
much as Humbert, like his father before him, was
a man of the “real human” stamp, with little
taste for statecraft, and a hearty dislike of every
kind of “flummery.” Ever since the belauded
unification of Italy, however, the burden
of trying to be a Great Power has been
breeding revolutionary mischief. The people
have found that it is possible to pay too dearly for
a sentimental satisfaction; and, though the mur-
dering anarchist is an extreme development, the
present temper of the Italian masses, especially in
the South, is hardly one to be struck with any
overpowering horror at the deed.
Joseph Abbott, the heavy-minded ex-
Speaker of N.S.W. Assembly, was hard hit last
week when Miles beat him for the Joint Stock
Bank directorship by some 400 votes. Taken with
the odium which Abbott has earned in the A.M.P.
Society as directors’ chairman and upholder of the
crooked proxy-vote, this means that Joe’s chances
for Federal election are very small. That is all
the better, for Abbott has no brains to place at
the Federal service, and stolid Respectability is
cheap. Abbott’s life has been an excellent
example of the public reverence for Outsides.
Give the public a 6ft. 2in. man, broad and weighty
in proportion, with a face modelled and shaved in
the Respectable way; and the public will push
that man far if he looks wise and says little.
New Zealand Loan and Mercantile Agency
Co. reports a fair amount doing in wheat during the
week at late prices, local millers and Queensland shippers
being the chief operators. Home and foreign agents have
been unable to keep up to the local prices except for the
very best samples. London advises market depressed
owing to favorable harvest prospects : N.Z. f.a.q. worth
30s. for 4861b5. ; longberried, 31s. for 4961b5. ; quotations
nominal. Which means, locally, 2s. 6Ad. per bushel f.0.b.,
New Zealand. With the co., 2s. lOd. is top quotation for
prime quality, chickwheat and pinched being sold at
2s. 3d. to 2s. 6d. per bushel.

A correspondent writes:—
I have a great idea—the biggest Fat Man idea of the
age. I propose that the landlords of each large Austra-
lian city and its suburbs should form an association, and
I think it possible, by a judicious exposition of the
advantages to be gained, to get them all into it. In fact
lam sure I could get them all into it. All rents would
be raised 50 per cent. The Association’s officers would fix
a minimum rent for every house, office or warehouse, on
the basis of 50 per cent, advance on present average rates,
and the landlord would bind himself under heavy penal-
ties not to let below that rent —he could let as much
above it as he pleased.' This would be easy; landlords
don’t object to more rent. The great increase of rents
wouldn’t drive people away, for there would be no
cheaper place to go to. Of the extra rent one-fourth
would be paid to the Association ( i.e ., the landlord whose
house is now let at 20s. a week would raise the rent to
30s. and get 275. 6d. for himself, paying the Association
the other 2s. 6d). Out of the fund thus created the Asso-
ciation would pay half-rents on all empty houses
(thus the house which now brings in 20s. a week
when occupied would then fetch 13s. 9d. a week when
empty.) This would prevent landlords being driven to
leave the Association and let their houses at non-union
rates if they chanced to have much empty property on
hand. Rents on empty houses would take only a small
part of the Association’s funds ; the balance would be
spent at first in buying up vacant land in all the more
onvenient suburbs in order to prevent promiscuous
building by non-union landlords. The Landlords’ Asso-
ciation would build on the purchased land, but would do
it judiciously, so as not to create an undue supply of pro-
, Perty. The rents from" its properties would enable it to
buy more land, till it had a grip on all adjacent vacant
soil, and could prevent any non-union landlord building
within easy reach of the city. By this great accumula-
tion of property it would have a huge income apart from
the contributions of its members. It could start Pen-
sions for Aged and Decayed Landlords. It could lend
money on mortgage to its own members at 2£ per cent.,
so that the Associated Landlords would have a great pull
on non-union landlords. In the end it could cease taking
contributions from its members, and might even pay
them dividends. And every member would be getting
275. 6d. a week rent where he used to get only £l, and
13s. 9d. a week where he used to get nothing, all these
other advantages being thrown in gratis. The idea of a
Cast-Iron Association of United Landlords has great
possibilities about it.
The British Jingo papers scent danger to the
Empiah in the presence of colonial contingents in
South Africa. Extract from a Pearson publica-
tion
The Biggest Danger That Has Ever Menaced The
United Kingdom.
We are so much accustomed to having the probabilities
of a war between Great Britain and the rest of Europe
flung in our faces, that, it is to be feared a great many
people consider that the only danger to the British Em-
pire is in such a conflict.
But few people seem to take into consideration the fact
that there is a far greater menace to the British Empire,
and that is—the Empire itself.
This may seem a paradox. It is not so. Far and wide
the Anglo-Saxon has established the feet of its manhood,
until the tiny country in Europe which we call the
United Kingdom, finds itself at the head of the mightiest
Empire the world has ever seen.
Aye, and a united Empire, indeed ! The war in South
Africa has proved that. From Canada, from Australia,
from India, from New Zealand the sons of the old Empire
have rallied round the flag with unequalled loyalty.
“ Quite so,” you say contentedly. “ Then why croak
about there being any danger from"the Empire ?”
One moment! What will happen after the war? The
men of Canada, the men of New Zealand, the men of Aus-
tralia will go back to their homes glowing with patriotic
pride. That is the danger. It is then that the reaction
will come.
They will say : “ What have we done? We have proved
that we can raise soldiers, as good and brave fighters, as
skilful as any in the world.”
That will be the first grumble. Then they will say :
“ Look at America. At one time she was a colony of
England. It is true she was shamefully governed,
while we have been governed wisely and well, But still,
we are not our own masters. The Imperial Parliament at
Westminster is our master. What does the Imperial Par-
liament know of us ? ”
Then the grumble will become a roar. The young lion
cub will have suddenly awakened to the fact tM- he has
claws. The next step will be secession.
An Australian Scout-corporal, from
Boesman’s Kop, South Africa, May 30 :
Dear Bulletin.—l don’t suppose the Orange Free State
exists any longer, for we could hear the guns from Bloem-
fontein, the other day, proclaiming the territory British.
On Queen’s Birthday some 14,000 troops paraded before
Gen. Kelly-Kenny and staff, including the Duke of Nor-
folk, who, with all his “ blood ” and money, is undersized
and as black and grizzly as an old-man chimpanzee.
Kelly said the Scouts were a splendid body of men, and
he meant it, for next day 35 of us were told off to scout
for different battalions. Bloemfontein one would expect
to be extremely healthy, as it is clean enough and built
on the open veldt ; yet the average daily mortality is 15,
rising some days as high as 50! At any moment one has
only to glance towards the cemetery to see a funeral. I
was told off as corporal with four men to scout for a
division of the Imperial Yeomanry, and, so far, have found
the officers all right. In fact, they rely on me for nearly
everything. “Corporal, do you think it is worth while
, to search that farm ? ” “ Corporal, shall I give this man a
pass or not ? ” In fact, if a horse is lost, it’s—“ See the
• corporal of scouts ; he and his men will find it ” ; which is
about the simplest thing we are asked to do. Scouting,
however, isn’t all velvet. F’rinstance : From our camp
here at Boesman’s (pronounced Bushman’s) Kop, between
Bloemfontein and the Waterworks, I w'ent out yesterday
about six miles ; but, nearing a farm, I left two men out
of range, while I went up to reconnoitre. I pretended to
be passing the place, when buzz ! buzz ! came two bullets
unpleasantly close, so 1 skipped for shelter, and sent one
of the other chaps back with a note to the division, while
we other two kept watch and blazed away at their win-
dows—not without getting a few in return. However, on
the arrival of 20 of the division with a gun, the white flag
was shown, and two men made prisoners. The farms I
have visited, though, are mostly only occupied by women,
the men being either still fighting or prisoners with the
British. Some of the women talk English. Eggs they
*ell you at 2s. 6d. a dozen ; a sheep, 255. ; and dried
fruit," Is. per pound. If you ask the distance to
any place, they give it in time—counting about six miles to
the hour. One cannot help pitying these poor people.
They are hospitable to a stranger, and have been led in
their ignorance to believe that the Britisher is little short
of a fiend. Nevertheless, the majority of. Boer girls, after
they know a Britisher or Australian for a time, and find
him a decent fellow, would sooner marry him
than a Boer. The British, by the way, will, I
think, make a tremendous mistake over the native ques-
tion. The Kaffirs were not brutally treated by the
Boer, as a rule, but were respectful, and took their hats
off to a white man. Now, however, they are quite
cheeky. Is not the Britisher their brother and their
equal? As for the much-talked-of peculiarities of the
British “ toff” officer, our own colonel and captain (both
M’sP.) are thorough gentlemen, and good fighters, but
their “ swearing” simply makes an Australian shriek with j
laughter. A nigger with his mule-team ran into the guy-
ropes of their mess-tent one morning, whereupon out
rushed one and let off the following objurgation : “ Oh,
you dashed miserable niggah !—don’t you evah da-ah to
run into an officers’ tent any moah ! Oh 1 you wretched
niggah ! ” I nearly burst thinking of how an Australian
would have handled the siituation.
An Australian Johannesberger—now on a
flying visit to Melbourne—who lately did three
months fighting under Buller, and has sampled
British and Boer sentiment in the Orange State,
Natal and Cape Colony, declares that the present
dragging-on of the war is due to the feebleness of
Roberts’ early proclamations. He talked like a
diplomatic suppliant rather than an inevitable
conqueror, and the Boers took him at what they
thQught to be his own valuation.
Men just out from S’Africa state that
young Winston Churchill has made an utter ass of
himself in his straining after effect. His “views”
and allegations have disgusted the moderates (who
know the country) by being pro-Boer when they
might fairly have been pro-British, and vice-versa.
Churchill’s own story of his escape from Pretoria
is quoted as an instance of his suppressing truths
that would have told against the Boer Govt, in
order to make himself look heroic.


BOTH SIDES of the CASE.
Is it worth While to Buy the
Encyclopaedia Britannica ?
VyHEN a man asks himself whether it is worth while to buy a book, the burden
or proof rests upon the book, and not upon the purchaser.
The vis inertias , the rule that change and movement shall not occur without a
cause, is the elementary law that keeps the planets in their places, and it is as sound
a rule ot conduct as of physics. There is the money at the banker’s, capable of
transformation into any one of a thousand pleasing forms, and desirable in itself
without%ny transformation ; a power in reserve, a sword in the scabbard : and a
sword that does not rust, but grows longer and sharper day by day : doubling itself
after a few years, if one leaves it undisturbed. Yet within the last year more than
twenty-six thousand of the people of Great Britain and her colonies have exchanged
money for copies of ££ The Times” reprint of the Encyclopaedia Britannica, be-
lieving that the books are of more use to them than the sovereigns would have been.
Why?
To begin with first principles, every man lives by the exercise of his wit, the use
of ins mind ; the capitalist and the landowner as well the merchant and the pro-
fessional man, for it needs as much intelligence nowadays to keep money when one
as it as to make money m the first place; the percentage of persons who are
unable to take care of the money they have, and to get the best value for the
money they make, being quite as large as the percentage of persons who are
unable to acquire money. The brain is the watchdog, as well as the money-getter •
the housekeeper as well as the breadwinner. Thought has taken the place of
physical force. The cave-dweller who could run fastest and strike hardest
brought home the largest store of meat and pelts, when the world was young.
-Nowadays the man who thinks quickly and accurately is the man who attains
ins object in life; whether it be power or pleasure, power for good or
tor evil, enjoyment for himself or for other people. The cave-dweller had
to eat meat and wear skins in order to gain the strength to seek for more
meat and more skins, and we have to spend money in order to train our minds.
. unn § t “ e . earlier years of life we give nearly all our time and energy to this train-
ing, but it is only the foundation that we can hope to build before the struggle beo-ins.
A man cannot cease to read and think when he has completed the process of formal
education any more than he can cease eating when he attains adult stature. His
rmnd works with or without his consent: it is a ship in deep water; it may be
guided, but there is no anchorage, it must move in one direction or another. Of all
the winds and currents which affect its course there is one influence that never ceases
to influence of the printed page. Everybody reads, everybody thinks about
Z f ie reac ‘ s - . he rea ds nothing but jests, his thoughts never rise above a titter :
i e reads nothing but the weekly police reports, his mind never escapes from the
og of crime and misery; if he reads nothing but cheap fiction, his mind never
grasps, the essential facts of life. Everyone, however, reads one thing worth reading
nowadays, tne daily newspaper. The man who has not heard of the South African
campaign, of any one of the dozen topics of the day, is unimaginable, he ceased to
exist when popular education became part of our national life. And the reading of
ie news of the day is m itself a keen stimulus to mental activity. Fiction is mixed
with the tacts ; matters not worth a thought must find their place in the newspapers
as ley co in the world s history, which it is the function of the newspaper to
chiomcle from day to day. But, on the whole, the world’s doings yield sound food
01 thought, and every man who reads his newspaper, and thinks about what he
leads m it, is using his mind to good purpose. If he does not think about it, his
time has been wasted ; he might as well have been shaping the paper into boats, like
a child, or tearing it with his teeth, like a puppy.
It is when he begins to think about his newspaper that he finds the need of
books. The newspaper may print a war-map to help him understand the move-
ments of an army, but it cannot print a treatise upon every subject which the news
of the day invites attention. Yet the treatise must be accessible if the news is to
be clearly apprehended; the reader must refresh his recollections in one case, acquire
new information in another case, in order intelligently to form his opinions. Books
aie as indispensable to the newspaper reader—and therefore to everyone—as a
chart is to a navigator. A library of some sort is a necessary of intellectual life.
_ The question, then, changes. Is it worth while to buy the Encyclopaedia
Britannica rather than some other systematised library ? A systematised library
it must be, since its function is to answer questions to meet any one of the doubts
that present themselves from day to day. Here, again, the consideration of cost,
m the other uses for money, must be faced. The Times (London) Reprint of the
.Ninth Edition of the Encyclopaedia. Britannica is a much lower-priced
book than the original impression, completed nearly ten years ago, although it is a
verbatim reprint—the same words in the same type, the same illustrations, no
abridgement or curtailment. But, notwithstanding the reduction in price, it costs,
even now, in the cloth binding, ,£l7, or, in monthly payments, 17 guineas, at the
rate of one guinea a month.
Can an equivalent library be purchased for less money ? Not in the form of a
woik of reference, that is certain, because there is in the English language no other
wot k of reference which attempts to treat in detail any such a variety of subjects as
are included in the Encyclopedia Britannica ; it is, in its own field, quite with-
out a competitor.
Cannot a man, however, purchase isolated treatises, dealing with the various
branches of knowledge, for his money ?
That is a question which everyone can answer for himself; it is a mere matter
of figures, and the calculation will, in itself, furnish agreeable and instructive food
for thought.
Of the sixteen thousand articles in the EncyclOpSSdia Britannica.,
at least fourteen thousand are brief essays. There are certainly not more than two
thousand subjects which are each important enough to justify the existence of a
separate book upon each subject. Of these, not more than five hundred are long
enough to make each a book in itself.
Upon nearly every one of these five hundred subjects separate books have been
published ; many of them written by the same men who wrote the articles for the
Encyclopaedia Britannica. Everybody knows roughly what books cost,
a good history, so much ; a good biography, so much ; a good hand-book upon any
special subject, so much.
, r^/ ie satisfactory way to answer the question at the head of this column,
taen, is to take pencil and paper and estimate the cost of five hundred good books
If the total be more than a hundred guineas, as against seventeen guineas, there
is very little room for doubt, and the order form which follows presents itself
opportunely.

Worn out with Work.
run down in health.
DYSPEPTIC AND WEARY.
Still another Great Recovery.
The Case of
MRS. LILY DUNNICLIFF.
(A SPECIAL INQUIRY.) ' „
Most of the suburbs of Newcastle are
swamps in wet weather, dust bins in dry,
and alike destitute of gardens and greenery.
A notable exception is Waratah, as the
residents have found out ; and so thought
our reporter, as lie looked about for the
residence of Mrs. Dunnicliff, a well-known
dressmaker, on the Maitland Road, at North
YVaratah. Mrs. LKmnicliff was at home, and
expressed her entire willingness to afford
our representative every information as to
her sickness and cure. ‘‘lt has often been
in my mind, ” said that lady, “ to write my-
self and tell what wonders Clements Tonic
has worked for me ; and nob only myself,
but my husband also.”
'■ Will you kindly tell me, Mrs. Dunni-
cliff, how your sickness arose ? ”
“I was worn out with work, thoroughly
run down in health, and a sufferer from a
severe form of indigestion ; also my mind
was affected, so that I lost my memory; I
could not recall a thought the minute after
it bail occurred to me. I would go into
another room for something I wanted, and
when I got there would forget what I came
for. A'l over my body I had a weary, sink-
ing feeling, with no desire or energy to do
anything. I lost my interest in what was
going on about me, and felt drowsy and
dull. My appetite was hardly worth calling
by that name. I had no liking for my food,
and what I did eat did not seem to afford
me any nourishment, while it gave me
extreme pain and distress.”
“ What sort of pain, Mrs. Dunnicliff?”
“ After a meal a great feeling of weight,
ike a hard lump, would come in the chest,
i’hen I suffered from a wretched headache,
with pains down the neck and spine ; a pain
between the shoulders that went like a knife
right through my chest; a pain that felt,
Its nearly as I can describe it, like the agony
of toothache in a new place. My nerves
were quite upset; I started and trembled at
the least thing, my nerves being always at
a painful tension. At the slightest sound I
would jump and sometimes scream ; I could
not restrain or control myself; and my
\eart troubled me, its palpitation at times
>eing most distressing. Another complica-
ion arising from my sickness was an
bstinate constipation that would not yield
o ordinary treatment. All day long I
vould be drowsy, but at night-time I could
et no proper rest. I would turn from side
i side, trying to find an easy place ; and
vhen I dozed I was troubled by dreams—
nd always unpleasant dreams—horrible
hings that frightened me so that
-[ woke up with a start and a cry. I found
ny eyesight suffer, my eyes becoming quite
weak, so that I had to take to glasses ; but
they were affected most strongly when I
went to stoop down or bend over to lift any-
thing. Then I felt as though the top of my
head was coming off in one piece—a most
horrible sensation that convinced me that
something was radically wrong, and the
precursor of some dire calamity. And when,
after stooping, I stood up, my brain would,
be all of a whirl eyesight SO'
obscured that for a time I could not see at
all.”
“ Surely, Mrs. Dunnicliff, you did not let
yourself get so bad as this without seeking a
remedy, or obtaining medical advice ? ”
enquired the reporter.
“As for doctors,” said Mrs. Dunnicliff,
“I never went to one ; but I have a volume
by an eminent M.D. with many valuable
recipes, some of wire!) appeared to be suited
to my case. I trial one after the other,
getting them made up at the chemist’s ; but
for all the btmdit 1 derived I might as well
have taken so much water.”
“ What did you do nex 1 1 ”
“ I came across a book describing the
merits and curative effects of Clements
Tonic. There were records of a number of
cases cured, with the symptoms from which
the patients suffered. 1 started to read
carelessly, but as I proceeded I became more
and more interested, for I found there cases
just like my own. All my troubles, all my
aches and pains, were recounted with a
minuteness and fidelity, as though it was
myself who had giveu the testimony. When
I had finished perusing the boo> 1 closed it,
firmly convinced that 1 had at last found a
remedy for my troubles, and that my days
of misery were numbered. I told my luisb ud
of the opinion I had formed, and he readily
agreed. This is how I came to take Cle-
ments Tonic, and so find the medicine that
restored my long lost health ; for it was
restored, and very quickly. After I com-
menced the new treatment I was able to
throw off the languid and weary feeling that

THE ORIGIN OF A GREAT DISCOVERY.
Amongst the citizens of Australia, and in all parts of the world, Bile Beans FOB Biliousness have earned for themselves by their un-
equalled excellence a reputation which places them to-day far in advance of any other similar preparation. This specific is not a carelessly
prepared nostrum ; on the contrary, it is the result of long and thoughtful research on the part of a medical man, who, after years of study
discovered a natural vegetable substance which, after further study, he succeeded in compounding with other ingredients so that its effect
on the digestive organism of the human system is identical with that of the natural bile created by the liver. Finding this, then to be the
most natural cathartic obtainable, the public rapidly accepted it in preference to all others, and consequently the old methods of starving
and the use of pepsin and bismuth sank into insignificance and oblivion. Acting on the bowels in a gentle manner, Bile Beans do not
weaken or in any way distress the patient. This is a point in which they differ materially from nearly all laxative remedies sold to-day as the
latter usually act in a violent manner, weakening the patient and in many cases doing more harm than good. Bile Beans have been aptly termed
“a medicine for the people at a popular price.” Many wonder how this remedy can have the great virtue that we claim for it and still be sold
at so reasonable a price; yet the solution is simple. Millions upon millions of Bile Beans are consumed annually (a fact that in itself is of
positive proof that the world’s inhabitants know how to appreciate a really genuine remedy, and that they have found that remedy in Bile
Beans for Biliousness). Owing to this immense annual sale we are enabled to purchase the very best drugs in exceedingly large quantities
and thus get them at a great deal lower price than if we bought only moderate quantities at a time. . Then again, Bile Beans are manufac-
tured in possibly the largest and best-equipped laboratories m the world. In this, establishment only the very best of latest improved
machinery is used, and this fact also greatly minimises the cost of production. Thus it will be seen that through the great consumption the
proprietors are enabled to sell this valuable remedy at a price that places it within the reach of all.
Bile Beans are sold everywhere at 13|d. per large box, or may be obtained from the Aust. Depdt, Bile Beans Manufacturing Oov

“Clara, my dear,” said her mother, I think
we will have a cup of tea before we go out.”
“Just the very thing, mother,” answered her
daughter, as she rang the bell for the maid.
“Makesome tea, Mary,, as mother and I have
decided to have a cup. Don’t be long, Mary, for
we have little time to spare.”

into the sitting-room, but neither mother nor

Shergold, of Wilton-street, Newcastle, was in bad
health for 19 years, and when seen by a New-
castle Hekald reporter, she made the following
statement:— °
“ They would almost save you from your coffin.
I shall ever be grateful,” she added, “to the lady
who advised me to try them. I had been suffer-
ing for four or five years, more than I could de-
scribe, and these Bile Beans have cured me. X
am now able to do that which I could not think of
attempting before.”
“ w hat were your symptoms ? ” inquired the re-
porter.
X had been bad off and on for nearly five years
suffering from general weakness and a bad
stomach, and for 19 years I had suffered with my
head. A little over a year ago I consulted a doc-
tor, and he gave me a prescription for indigestion
This gave me no permanent relief. Then I tried
another doctor in Maitland, who said I had rheu-
matics in the head. He, too, failed to do me any
real good, and as month succeeded month I grew
worse, and the more I tried to rest the more
tired I became. My nights were a perfect
misery to me, for the want of sleep I
got so bad eventually that it was not only
a difficulty for me to walk, but it was painful
to even bow my head. I commenced taking the
Beans about eight months ago. I felt a relief
after the first couple of doses, and in a short time
I felt completely well; I can now eat and sleep
splendidly, and it is a pleasure for me to take a
walk out. Yes,” added Mrs. Shergold, in reply

to publish my testimony. It is the truth I have
told you, and while I tell you the troth I do not
care if it is placarded all over Mere wether.”

ana adjustments of plant, show as good figures as the
previous term did—and they were the best in the co.’s
history. In view of what has happened in connection
with the South, it is just as well to note that there are
contributing as well as paid shares in the British. In pro-
portion to the output the South is, however, the biggest
gainer by the rise in lead. Its ore averages just under
zu per cent, lead (in the week before writing it was 19'9
per cent.), Boz. silver, and 181 per cent. zinc. (The aver-
age for the main workings of the British is 16 per cent,
lead lloz. silver and 15£ per cent, lead.) The South’s
developments continue good. Also here, as at almost
every mine on the main lode, a great deal of machinery
construction is in progress. Since the beginning of the
year nearly 1000 men have been added to the B.H. com-
panies’ staffs.

Mr. Jaquet, N.S.W. Geological-Surveyor, haa
reported in favor of smelting-, at Lithgow, the Oarcoar
iron-deposits. He estimates that there are over 24 mil-
lion tons ore in sight, enough to keep a blast furnace,
consuming 2000 tons of ore weekly, going for 25 years on
ore containing 50 to 65 per cent, metallic iron and 6 to 7
per He estimates that pig-iron could be pro-
duced at Lithgow at £2 Bs. per ton—more than £1 less
than its value m England to-day. Mr. Jaquet is now pre-
paring a report to show how the Carcoar ore-supplies
might be supplemented by those from other districts on
the Western line. Mr. Jaquet, doubtless the ablest
mining-man in N.S.W. Govt, service, is well worth lis-
tening to.

Mount Drysdale (N.5.W.).-It was printed last
week that 50 tons from between 242 ft. and 275 ft. levels
had been treated at Dapto for 14250 z. ; a shilling div. was
declared. After a day or two it transpired, in the usual
casual Mt. Drysdale kind of way, that the yield
wasnt 14250 z but £1425 worth. Stated (also in
the usual Mt. Drysdale way) that the ore at bottom
level is fabulously rich, one section assaying l 90oz.
gold PC* - ton, another over 400 oz. It is too much to ex-
pect that the bulk will go anything like this, but it is
reasonable to think that the co. will have a number of
very rich ore consignments. The show consists of a mas-
sive, low-grade, but payable formation, in which there
‘,L a „r nder ? llly »ch P‘P e - 11 has Paid Bs. 6d. —over
£20,000—111 divs. within six months, and the price of the
shares, 265., is sufficient proof that there are a number of
others in sight. The stigma of a patch mine will stick
to the Drysdale, because of the sudden transformation of
five years ago. But the biggest of the Kalgoorlie
mines is more or less a patch mine. They are all
made up of rich and poor ore-lenses, and it is
the number of patches established by development in the
few great mines that enable something like regular pro-
duction to be kept up. Imagine a long string of
sausages, some of them beef, some pork, and a lot of them
sawdust, and the reader will get some faint idea of what
writer means. Imagine, too, the Mt. Drvsdale mine
within, or on the fringe of, the “ Marvellous Mile ” at
Hannan s ! It has now a market value of about £60,000.
If it were over there it would be considered quite cheap at
hve times the money. There isamine,theGoldenLink(why
not the Missing Link ?), within the Mile which has more
than that value. It has never had a crushing worthy of the
name ; at any rate, it has never had a div.—it hasn’t the
hope of one ; and there are half-a-dozen mines outside the
Mile which have a higher market value than the Mt.
Drysdale to-day not one of which has found any payable
ore, while each has spent more than the value of the
Drysdale in fruitless prospecting. For monuments of
mining folly go to Hannan’s.
• • • m
Wentworth mines (N.S.W.) : The untiring
Newman, M.P., has at last got the management of the
whole group—Wentworth, D’Arcy Wentworth, Aladdin’s
Lamp, and Wentworth Extended. Writer has heard
several persons, ex-managers and others, declare what
they could do if they only had the control of the mine for
a time. Newman has been given the opportunity. He
has lived more than half his life on them, knows every
inch of the ground, and should be able, better than any
other man, to unearth some rich bonanzas if there are
many of them lying about. Of a hundred stories concern-
ing the concealment of bonanzas 99 are false. The next-
hardest thing to finding a bonanza in any mine is to leave
one behind.

Girilambone (N.S.W.): “Shares have been in
improved demand of late.” Even that immortal tipster
“ has never lost faith in them.” Many people would have
preferred to have lost their faith if they had saved their
money ; anyhow there is a cross-cut going out which will
prove the mine at a deeper level, and it recently struck
some ore where none was expected. It was not very rich
but is “ improving every foot.” It is also making a little
water, and has other signs generally regarded as good.
North Nymagee, too, took a spurt lately. Made a little
money by sending down a parcel of ore,' and another was
expected. Last report is not encouraging. Lode in face
• about 2ft. wide, composed of quartz and gossan carrying
a “Me gray ore and native copper. For the last 10 or
12ft. the lode has been somewhat pinched owing to a
bulge m footwall." Anyhow there are four men breaking
ore in a stope, and there are buyers at 2s. 6d.

Horn Island. — Official advices of first clean-up,
2800 tons, 2060 z. bar gold, besides 36 tons concentrates of
value unstated. There is the usual excuse : “ a consider-
able quantity put through the battery had to be removed
from the quarries to admit of operations on the payable
ore.” Well, why crush it if of no value? It is much
cheaper to remove mullock from the battery than to mill
it. Manager wired : “ I have no doubt we can improve
upon this ; results so far satisfactory.” Great Scott!
What would be considered unsatisfactory? Considerably
less than 2dwts. to the ton ! The concentrates would have
to be very rich seeing that less than one ton comes from
100. Manager estimates that “ when the mine is in full
work the cost of mining and milling will not exceed ldwt.
per ton.” Well, it costs more than that at Cobar, where
there is unlimited ore worked by open-cut, and with
bigger batteries than they have or are likely to have at
n° r O Island. It costs even more than that at the
Alaska Treadwell, where they have the biggest battery
in the world. It costs more than that all over
the world where wages are infinitely lower than they are
or are ever likely to be at Horn Island. Distance lends,
&c. A year ago Horn Island was declared to be a 15dwt.
show, and many people bought shares up to 15s. under
that impression. No explanation of this disappointment.
“ Results considered satisfactory 1"

Mr. Fred Merton, of W.A., claims to own Aus-
tralia’s “biggest” lone-hand gold-show. He writes to
The Bulletin, from Perth :—“ Enclosed par. from Perth
Morning Herald, which I have signed as correct. It will
give you a true impression re my mine, for which, I may
tell you, I would not accept the best price ever offered for
any mine in Australia. Watch my returns, and you can
form your own ideas. Mine open for inspection by any-
one at any time. More gold showing over a larger area
than in any other mine in W.A.” Here is the Perth
Herald par. :
“ MERTON’S REWARD.—THE JUNE YIELD.
“ Mr. Fred Merton, sole proprietor of the famous Merton
Reward mine, at Mertondale, in Mt. Margaret district, has
arrived in Perth, on a fortnight’s holiday. He informs us
that during the month of June he was able to work the
battery only 12 days, owing to water running short.
During those 12 days 400 tons of ore were crushed for a
return of 1108 oz.—just over 2foz. per ton. It was in
March of last year that Mr. Merton discovered the pro-
perty, and since then he has been delayed by the usual
difficulties that dog the feet of the prospector in the back
country, and also by a lengthy legal action. These
troubles notwithstanding, he had by Sept, last got out
about 1400 oz. of gold, and erected a 20-head battery,
which has since been running as regularly as the supply
of water will admit. In all he has crushed 2450 tons ore
for 71570 z. gold. The value of the gold per ounce varies
slightly, but will average over £3 15s. So far no stoping
has been done on the mine, practically all the ore treated
having been taken from development work. No. 1 incline
shaft is down 120 ft., and there the lode is 30ft. wide, and
will yield 2ioz. gold to ton. No. 2 incline shaft has been
sunk to 195 ft., and at that depth the lode is Ift. wide, and
averages over soz. In No. 3 shaft, down to 120 ft., the
lode is 6ft. wide, and worth 2£oz. Stone a free-milling
quartz, thickly studded with fine gold. It would be safe
to say that Merton’s Reward is by far the richest singly-
owned mine in the province, and the economy which is
exercised in its development and the treatment of the ore
is as remarkable as the wonderful richness of the pro*
perty. Mr. Merton claims to pay higher wages than
anyone else on the field, but says that his milling costs
him only \4s. 2d. per ton, and mining only 12s. He
further states that in the course of two or three months,
when the provision he is now making for the securing of a
regular supply of water has been completed, and when
he starts stoping ore, he will be able to show a clear profit
on 3dwt dirt. Mr. Merton manages his mine himself, and
to that fact may be attributed much of his success. _ It is
much to be feared that in the case of a public co.
having a battery that would crush 1000 tons of ore per
month, and a mine that could keep up a yield of that
quantity of stone, giving an average return of 2Joz. per
ton, no very serious attempt would be made to keep the
total working cost down to a basis of 3dwt. ’ per ton.
Throughout the province, and particularly in the district
north of Menzies, numerous instances have been furnished
of working miners making big profits out of properties
that have been abandoned as worthless by English
co’s, and a comparison of Mr. Merton’s working costs with
the working costs of almost any mining co. in the province
should throw some light on this subject. Mr. Merton
says that he has no intention of selling his mine. He
thinks it probable that at a later date he will put up a 50-
head mill, but even under present conditions he has
enormous profits in view.
» • * *
Mt. Morgan G.M. Co.: The 15th annual re-
port, giving the transactions of the half-year and year, is
in every respect the finest that writer has seen—an object-
lesson for every G.M. co. that has a mine to work. It is
complete in every detail, and though comprehensive con-
tains no padding. 0utput—238,276 tons, an increase of
34,774 tons for the year; yield, 187,3520 z., or 22,4420 z.
more than last year, the average showing a slight falling-
off—l44grs. per ton. Nearly 60 per cent, consisted of
low-grade oxidised ore, 16 per cent, was medium-grade
oxidised ore, a little over 7 per cent, higher-grade mundio
ore, and 13J per cent, low-grade ditto. Average grade of
all ore treated during year, slightly over 15Jdwts. per ton
—about Is. 9d. less than last year’s average value. Divs.
for year, 7d. monthly, or £350,000 in all. Wages alone
absorbed £126,921 ; div. duty cost £17,500, and royalty
£8641. Directors’ fees, £l6OO, which is not excessive,
considering the magnitude of the mine, and modest
compared with the sums paid in London for infinitely
less value in services rendered. Last year the mundic ore
yielded 2oz. Bdwts. per ton ; oxidised ore, lOjdwts. per
ton, the low-grade a little under Bdwts. This year the
mundic ore averaged 2oz. 2|dwts.—a decrease of nearly
sJdwts. ; oxidised ore, a fraction under 2oz. per ton —an
increase of over'lOdwts. in favor of this year. The low-
grade ore was slightly lower this year than last. No less
than 28210 z. gold came from slag, ash, lead-scrapings,
etc., sent to Aldershot during the year. In the produc-
tion of chlorine there were used—manganese, 956 tons ;
salt, 900 tons ; sulphuric, acid, 3307 tons. It is interesting,
too, how the percentage of chloride used in treating the
mundic is coming down like that of cyanide in solutions.
This year’s consumption of chlorine is 26 per cent, less
than last, though the chlorine requirements of the high
and low-grade oxidised ores remain practically the
same. The percentage of extraction is slightly higher
on all the ores. The bulk of the manganese
comes from Gladstone district, and still maintains its
high quality. The salt supply comes from S.A., the
sulphur from Japan, and from these commodities the co.
makes its own sulphuric acid. Every g.m. manager in
Australia should try and get a copy of this report. In-
teresting as it is it seems to have escaped the attention of
all the big Australian dailies.
• * * •
Westralian gold production : —First six months
of year 698,4170 z. as compared with 647,9140 z. for corres-
ponding period of ’99 ; but latter part of last year was
notorious for a phenomenal production, which is unlikely
soon to be repeated. Lake View alone turned it out at
the rate of nearly a ton-and-a-half a month, and the Asso-
ciated averaged more than four times its present output.
The Western Argus, the champion W.A. boomster, “ has
reason to suppose that history will repeat itself, and that
the figures for the ensuing six months will show a large
increase on corresponding period.” It is indeed hard to
believe this yarn. True, there will be a few returns from
Boulder Perseverance, Lake View, and Great Boulder
sulphide plants, three or four at most, but it is
utterly unlikely that all combined will make up the
phenomenal yields of last year. No mention of the fact
that the Boulder and Perseverance returns were consider-
ably larger this time last year, or that the Ivanhoe returns
are bound to be considerably lower during the remainder
of this. New auriferous areas are being opened up at
Lawler’s, Mt. Higgins, Leonora, and other places, but
time will not permit their adding much to this year’s
gold output.

A Barbarian.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
See you, ye pious frauds, in me,
The Father of a Family !
Not fashion’s niggard two, not four—
A good barbarian half-a-score.
Big girls and small, fat boys and lean,
Of course a baby yet to wean.
O ye who prate philosophy,
Shame on your sleek economy !
See you, ye pious frauds, in me,
A generous and full-bearing tree !
And proud I am that part to play,
I met a chap the other day,
Bays he —“ How many be they now ? ”
Says I—l taps my ample brow—
They run close up to half-a-score.”
Says he—“ God send you many more ! ”
“ God send you many more, and luck 1 ”
His record, blow me !is a duck.
I pray that he may live to be
The Father of a Family,
Not fashion’s niggard two, not four,
A good barbarian half-a-score . . .
■Maybe he’ll wonder where he are,
And value babies under par.
I have my griefs, I have my cares,
I don’t get high up Fortune’s stairs;
'There is no joke in half-a-score
"Who clean their plates, and ask for more ;
There’s Bob—he’s great on bread and jam,
And what togs cost don’t care a damn!
He’s jam from collar down to knee—
I s’pose I’ve “ too much family.”
But just two kids—no, that ain’t right!
For some it’s one, and then “ good-night. ”
Why, bless your heart, that isn’t biz.,
That’s not the way a nation’s riz.!
Two don’t add one, as you will see,
Therefore, it is no family . . .
Say, missus ! stop that blooming row,
And let me finish—here and now.
You smoke cigars and drink champagne—
Those luxuries I must disdain ;
Perhaps you neither sow nor spin,
I lick the kid who wastes a pin ;
While you are free at large to roam,
I am a slave tied down to home ;
You coddle self ; they coddle me,
The missus and the family.
And this, ye pious frauds, I know :
The years may come, the years may go,
And age may chill me to the bone,
I never shall be left alone
To fret life’s lees out by myself,
Unloved, forgotten, on the shelf.
He lacks not love who boasts, like me,
A good barbarian family.
y.
ANDREW SARD.
Lonesomehurst.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
On the crest of that long, low hill, that bites a big,
dark mouthful out of the straight Southern sky-
line, is Lonesomehurst. Most of these imposing
houses are tenantless. The larrikin-winds play
devastatingly in their empty rooms, the pale grass
grows in the eraberless fireplaces and struggles to
kiss the anaemic light-rays that wander in at the
ever-widening wall crannies. Outside, frogs croak
unceasingly in the unused cisterns, and the
weather-beaten, ant-tunnelled fences stoop with
the langour of swift-coming collapse.
I’ve gone to live at Lonesomehurst. No more
city, awhile, for me. The plague-germ is a social
little beggar and likes crowds. He will probably
avoid Lonesomehurst.
Tell you about Lonesomehurst! Its Genesis or
its Exodus? It has had both, you know. Well,
12 or 14 years ago some portly city men, with the
hodden-grey intelligences that develop through
much concentration of thought on hides and
talldw and jute-goods and fencing-wire, suddenly
and unexpectedly disclosed the possession of the
imaginative faculty. Spurred by a desire to find
a quicker avenue to wealth than is afforded by the
drab, well-trodden paths of commerce, they suc-
ceeded in convincing themselves and others that an
organisedinflationofland-values was the true Phil-
osopher’s Stone. All that was wanted was plenty of
wind, and as the fat men were in purple earnest they
soon produced an iridescent bubble of a land-boom;
but, alas ! as unsubstantial as the dome of a palace
seen in dreams. How it shone and glittered and
glinted in the meretricious sunlight of a delusive
prosperity ; and what faery fortunes were minted
daily, hourly, midst its prismatic lights!
Presently, north, south, east and west spread the
flying buttresses of this dream-palace. Far out
beyond city and nearer suburb arched these
shimmering promises of wealth —mere illusive
rainbows —but one of them seemed to rest for a
moment on a green southern hill. Everybody
knows there’s a pot of gold at the end of a rainbow
—and so Lonesomehurst was conjured out of
bricks and mortar in a month.
*,* * *
“How on earth did you come to put your
money into such a rotten spec. ? ” I asked the
question with ready, if shallow interest, to a dis-
gruntled pioneer of Lonesomehurst who spends his
days and his nigh*- groaning over his vanished
“Vez o'rter seen the plan !” he replied, with the
ardor of self-justification. “Twuz the foinest
plan ye ever see ! Twuz mounted on rollers no
lis§ !- —an’ the ’lotmmts wuz all a beautiful skoy-
biae, an’ the name .of th’ blashted place was
printed in goold, sir—goold ! ”
And the look he gave me was an appeal tor
sympathy for a man who had been lured into th©..
purchase of sky-blue allotments in a place with a
gold name, at some £8 a foot!
* « * *
The boomsters of Lonesomehurst must have been
men with big ideas; nothing so common as mere
streets for them. To this day each grass-grown,
vaguely-indicated thoroughfare is an esplanade,
or a “parade,” or a “boulevard ” 1
The dejected pioneer’s temper rises when the
word “ boulevard ” is used in his hearing.
I had asked him if he had seen my children
gathering mushrooms down the Boulevard.
“Drat thim furrin names ! ” he exclaimed with
some heat. “If we hadn’t been all clane crazy
the very sound uv thim shud have frightened us
out uv it. An’ we shud a taken warnin’
from the very threes they planted in their
boolevard. Nort’ uv Oireland pines —no
liss —and cupressy macrocarpy, an’ outlandish
threes loike that! An’ look at thim now ! Did
as hirrins, bad cess to thim, and the little spal-
panes uv gum saplins shows their contimpt by
goin’ a-boolevard-in’ all over the place on their
own, as the savin’ is ! ”
True, the laying out of Lonesomehurst is a
monument to the vanity of human prescience.
The track of the primal bullock-dray—temporarily
obstructed in the boom-time hey-day—has pre-
vailed over the pretentious “esplanades” with
their straight lines and severe right-angles, and
follows its old-time line of least resistance with an
exaggerated meandering, as if it gloried with
muddy truculence over the reversion to primitive-
ness. That bullock-track always suggests to me a
Goth trampling on the cherished ideals of a
decadent and unworthy civilisation.
• • * <
It appears that when the bubble-dome of the
boom-palace collapsed, and the tentacular rain-
bows had shrunk back to their initial nothingness,
Lonesomehurst, with its 15 or 20 houses, under-
went the vicissitude called “ falling back into the
hands of the Building Society.” This euphemism
is loose in its descriptive quality. Property may
“ fall back ” with the pleasant appetising flop of
an egg into a frying-pan, or it may “ fall back ”
with the destructive velocity of a ballista-thrown
boulder. In Lonesomehurst’s case it was such a
“ hot delivery ” that the Building Society tottered
and fell, and the weight of the whole concern
eventually settled down on the overburdened
shoulders of a weary Titan of a bank.
And so a reconstructed bank, with all sorts of
rum-looking lines in its balance-sheets about de-
ferred deposits and preference debentures, and
whose shareholders were for the most part wan
men with sponged belltoppers and Point de Galle
on their shirt-cuffs, became the unwilling and un-
appreciative landlord of Lonesomehurst ; and
’twas its agent who sent me there house-hunting.
“ Only four or five lef, and 15 at least to choose
from,” he said, with the airy indifference of the
janitor of a morgue—the tragedies of land-owning
were as nothing to him.
Lonesomehurst at first sight seemed to be
covered with “to-let” notices, and it was out of
sheer inconsequent affability that I asked the first
resident I came across if he knew of any vacant
houses in the vicinity ?
But this question, obviously so easy of answer,
apparently caused the man extreme mental travail.
Having made a pantomimic display of all-absorb-
ing thought, he said slowly, “Lemmesee!” (Long
pause.) “I—rather—think—not.” (Pause and
lengthy scrutiny of his questioner.) “ But I’ll ask
Julier if y’like. Ju— li—err!”
A sad-eyed woman came to the door with sus-
picious alacrity. She had, no doubt, heard it all.
“Naw! I don’t know of no houses to let,” she
said** sullenly, and then proceeded to nervously
chew the comer of her ragged apron.
Clearly, there was nothing more to be said.
Two hoydenish girls hung over a broken fence a
few doors down, and I besought them to advise me
as to the most desirable of the empty houses.
They gave a soprano giggle in perfect unison,
stepped back from the fence with a one, two,
three, and struck a stagey pose of astonishment
that would have passed muster as the prelude to a
pas de deux at the Tivoli.
“Law! but a toff like you would’nt come to
live here ! ’ ’ exclaimed one of them—the last word
in most depreciatory diminuendo.
“Why not?” I enquired stoutly. “Only a
couple of miles from the station, healthy ; not too
far from town.”
The girls wriggled their shoulders and giggled
harmoniously.
I was getting annoyed. I was annoyed, and
didn’t conceal it. “ Why not ? ” I again queried
sharply.
“Bees!” said the girl who had previously
spoken.
“Bees!” I echoed in mystification, and said
that a casual apiary I did not regard as a disad-
vantage.
“No, not them sort of bees” —came cheekily
from the spokeswoman. “B-flats! Noffok
’Owids ! Bergs, bergs, ” and the pair rushed off,
laughing shrilly, as though at an indecency,
after the manner of rudimentary womankind.
* * * *
Eventually I took the villa, and found that it
was free from vermin, and, with a little fixing up,
quite a comfortable domicile. Why, then, had
the residents made these barefaced attempts to
choke me off from a tenancy ?
I gleaned this knowledge by degrees. The ob-
jection to me was not in any sense a personal one ;
it was instigated solely by the primary law of
self-preservation. The citizens of Lonesome-
hurst, it seemed, regarded the payment of rent—
that is, the regular payment—as an economic error
of the worst kind. The unnecessary .abjectness
and effusive thanks of the collector when I paid
him one whole fortnight’s rent at once was the
first intelligible indication of this local idiosyn-
crasy. I subsequently found that he had trum-
peted my praises throughout the entire neighbor-
hood, and the resident to whom I had first applied
for information came to me in a monitory mood.
“Look ’ere, Mister,” he said ; “ p’raps yous has
got into the ’abit of payin’ rent reg ler, an’ it
comes natural. But we ’uns up ’ere aint paid rent
reg’ler for years, an’ the Weedin’ ole bank, afore
yous kem, were darn glad to hev us live on its
rotten ole proputty, to keep it from bein’ carried
away. Now you come with your ‘ fortnight’s rent
in advance,’ an’ nark us all,” and the gentleman
spat vigorously and departed.
And then the mother of the girls with the song-
and-dance manners further enlightened me. “This
is the long an’ the short of it, sir,” she said, after
she had amicably borrowed a cupful of sugar and
a candle ; “you aint popular here. You’re a nice,
kind gentleman, an’ so is the good lady, but sence
yous kem that there bank is startin’ to put on airs.
Why, the collector act’ly talked ‘bailiff’ to me
this morn in’ ! An’ him as has alius been so meek
an’ mild, an’ glad to get a bob or two now an’
then. Why, that’s been a-goin’ on ever
sence Harabeller there was a kiddy. I’m
afraid yous ’as done us old ’ands ’ere
a lot o’ ’arm—tho’ quite unbeknownt, a-course—
quite unbeknownst ! ” Then she continued, in-
sinuatingly : “Now, as things is gittin’ ’ealthy
ag’in in the city, p’raps yous ’ull be for goin’
back ! ”
And a sudden thought seemed to strike her.
“ Harabeller ! ” she called. “ Harabeller !—no
—Yera, you’ll do ! Gut down to Mr. Murphy’s
as ’ard as ye like, an’ borrer this mornin’s pyper
ag’in. Tell ’im, pertic’lar, that I want to show
the gentleman ’ere where it says that there aint
bin no plygue in the city for two ’ole days ! ”
J AMES A.PHILP.
